# O
## O for awesome
 
 <i>phr.</i> [Boxer David Tua's famous request on Wheel of Fortune 1992]

>  <i>(Apr 9, 2011,Dominion Post,B1</i>)

>  Asked to choose a vowel, he paused for a moment before asking for an "O for awesome". <i>(Apr 9, 2011,Dominion Post,B1</i>)



## O'Briens
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) distinctive farm boots, particularly for mustering [orig. trade name.]

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> O'Briens: see under Pannells and R and Gs. All are terms for boots. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,56</i>)

>  "O'Briens and "Pannells" also have a place as distinctive boots worn by country folk, and particularly those mustering sheep. <i>(Feb 11, 1977,Press, The,19</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR & TROTTER</b> I was lucky I was well shod, O'Brien mustering boots on, my saddletweed trousers tied down, so I was dry as could be. <i>(1994,From Caithness to Southland,17</i>)



## O'Leary
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> There are games recorded in this period for which there is no record before 1900 though they may well have existed under other names. West Coast: Dunk and Davey, Spang Weazling; Wellington: Who Goes Round My Stone Wall, Beg o' My Neighbor, Bellahonie, Run a Mile, On and Off the Bridge, Here Come Two Nuns, Colours, Isaac and Rebecca. Scrag, Diabolo, Bumble Puppy, Homai, Fives; Auckland: My Fair Young Lady, Rushes and Reeds, Surrender the Tower, Walls, Ball Hopscotch, ball games, Solomon Says; Hawkes Bay: Wolf Wolf Go Home, Homai; Canterbury: Poor Old Tom, Rats and Rabbits and Rips, Steps, Skin the Snake, Steal a Peg, Toodle-em-Buck, The Gag; Golden Bay: Who's That Walking Round My House Tonight, I've Come to See Poor Mary Jane, Steps and Stairs, Creeping, Branding, O'Leary; Otago: Sir Roger de Coverley, Who's That Knocking at My Door. <i>Ibid.</i> 273 Ball-bouncing games have come into their own on the asphalt playgrounds of modern schools and are played in many schools throughout the country. There is no evidence of such games before 1900, but a number of instances between 1900 and 1920 are recorded. The games were known as Fives, Tens, Sevens, and Walls and have been recorded more often since under the names O'Leary or Sevens. Before 1920 there is only one report of the game being played with a rhyme, and this was a rhyme more often used in skipping. <i>(1981,History of Children's Play,231</i>)



## O.B.
 
 <i>abbr.</i> ordinary building [sawmillers' term meaning 'ordinary building' (rimu) as contrasted with heart wood]

>  <b>WILSON</b> There were good boards among these facecuts which today would be classed as O.B. <i>(1955,Land of My Children,28</i>)



## O.M.
 
 <i>abbr.</i> the overland mail (as distinct from that carried by sea) in the North Island

>  No less than a dozen native letters were received by yesterday's O.M. <i>(1856,McLean Papers,3/10</i>) [NOTES: J.Webster]



## O.P. whisky
 
 <i>n.</i> over-proof whisky

>  <b>MASTERS</b> Goods would have to be delivered at various places along the route, and often among the goods would be demijohns of O.P. whisky. <i>(1960,Back-Country Tales,168</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> Fourteen musterers, two packmen, one cook (old Wattie), two rabbiters, one rouseabout, three gallons of O.P. whisky, one accordion ... [sic] mix well - and the result will surprise you! <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,16</i>)



## O.P.S.O.
 
 <i>abbr.</i> On Public Service Only, used on mail from Government Departments sent overseas from 1891 - 1906 or on stamps identifying such mail. [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  Fold up your form, place it in an envelope, address it to the Registrar of Electors for Wairau, or Picton, as the case may be, at the top of the envelope write, "O.P.S.O. - Claim to Vote," which renders a postage stamp unecessary, and drop the letter into the nearest Post Office box. <i>(Jun 9, 1887,Marlborough Express,3</i>)

>  It is .. safe to assume that if any of the happy Liberals of this district .. receive from their President the message, "O.P.S.O. - Memo., urgent - take precedence - Attend without fail at Massey's meeting, and give him the worst turn he ever had", it will be accompanied by the police reminder that not even the back of the telegram should be used as the vehicle for the questions that are to shatter the reputation of the champion of "the selfish few". <i>(May 4, 1905,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  The Premier himself could hardly have made a better job of that telegram, yet it does not appear to have been sent O.P.S.O. - Memo, Urgent - Take Precedence." <i>(Nov 18, 1905,Evening Post,4</i>)



## O.Pip
 
 <i>abbr.</i> observation post [WW1]

>  <b>TREADWELL</b> ... the soldiers were using the spire as an O. Pip, or observation post. ... The corporal who had been using this O.P. preceded me up the spire. <i>(1936,Recollections of an Amateur Soldier,110</i>)



## oak
 
 <i>n.</i> an early name applied to various New Zealand trees thought to resemble the English oak either in appearance or in the quality of their timber; (espec <i>Vitex lucens</i>, PURIRI) [obs.]

>  <b>YATE</b> <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles)</i>) [NOTES: get quot.]

>  Puriri, which has been called the Oak of the Pacific, and the New Zealand Oak. <i>(1840,NZ Journal,1/11/139</i>)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> <i>Of Vervenaceae</i> (same order as the teak), the Vitex littoralis is the Puriri of the natives and the New Zealand oak of the colonists. <i>(1843,Travels in NZ,1/431</i>)

>  <b>HOCHSTETTER</b> <i>Vitex lucens</i> <i>(1867,New Zealand (Hochstetter)</i>) [NOTES: (list of trees from Taylor 1848)]

>  <b>KIRK</b> <i>Vitex lucens</i> <i>(1889,Forest Flora</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Vitex lucens</i> <i>(1906,Plants of NZ</i>)

>  <b>BROOKES</b> The puriri is called the New Zealand oak. <i>(1892,Frontier Life,174</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Vitex lucens (The Puriri)</i>. A fine tree, from 50ft. to 60ft. in height, often called the New Zealand Oak, on account of the strength and durability of its timber. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,350,1940 p. 372 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> <i>Vitex lucens</i> <i>(1915,Reminiscences of Earliest Canterbury</i>)

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> <i>N.Z. Oak (Puriri)</i>, see <i>Vitex lucens</i> <i>(1943,NZ Gardening Dictionary, The,128</i>)

>  <i>Oak (&obreve.k), n. (Bot.) [Add.] ... New Zealand oak</i>. See under PURIRI. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2028</i>)

>  The Puriri, or New Zealand Oak, or Teak, (<i>Vitex littoralis</i>,) is a large tree of irregular growth. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/43 (</i>) [NOTES: Colenso Botany North Isd]

>  <b>BARLOW</b> The Puriri (<i>Vitex littoralis</i>), sometimes called the New Zealand oak, is perhaps the next in importance to the Kauri ... It is principally used for railway sleepers, house blocks, ... and fencing posts. <i>(1888,Kaipara and Waitemata Echo,114</i>)

>  <b>KEENE</b> <i>puriri</i>: New Zealand oak, a large, handsome tree with hard timber. <i>(1963,O Te Raki,194</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> pohutukawa

>  <b>POLACK</b> The Puriri (<i>Metrocideros florida</i> or <i>quercus Australis</i>), is a wood whose durability equals any of the timbers in the country; it is very tough and close grained, and has been termed the Oak of the Pacific; but the Teak would be more appropriate, ... <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),2/393</i>) [NOTES: [some confusion here]]

>  <b>BENNETT</b> [The pohutukawa] is the New Zealand Oak and Fire-tree of the Europeans. <i>(1860,Gatherings of a Naturalist,347</i>)

>  <b>HOCHSTETTER</b> Metrosideros robusta New Zealand Oak-elm <i>(1867,New Zealand (Hochstetter)</i>) [NOTES: (list of trees from Taylor 1848)(A]



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> titoki, NZ ash.

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>(1898,Austral English</i>)

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> <i>Alectryon excelsum </i> <i>(1890,Descriptive & Priced List</i>)

>  <i>Alectryon excelsum </i> <i>(1916,Official Trade Register of Nurserymen</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Oak, n. The Oak of the Northern Hemisphere (<i>Quercus</i>) is not found among the indigenous trees of Australia; ... The word <i>Oak</i> is further extended to a few trees, not <i>Casuarinae</i>, given below; and in New Zealand it is also applied to <i>Matipo</i>(q.v.) and <i>Titoki</i>, or <i>Alectryon</i>(q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,325</i>)

>  <b>DUNCAN & DAVIES</b> <i>Pittosporum eugenioides </i> <i>(1917,Descriptive Catalogue</i>)

>  <b>BEGG</b> (|= macron precedes) oak, e|nak [inaka or grass tree] <i>(1979,Boultbee Journal,283</i>) [NOTES: 1826-28 Boultbee Vocabulary in 1979 Begg]

>  oak - e|n|ak [inaka] <i>(1835,Boultbee Journal</i>) [NOTES: c1826-1827 list of Maori words in c1835 Boultbee Journal]

>  <i>Carpodetus serratus </i> <i>(1872,Catalogue of the Colonial and Vienna Exhibition</i>)



 
<b>7</b> <i>n.</i> ? kahikatea ? miro ? birch

>  <b>McNABB</b> Q. Are there many harbours there [on the west side of New Zealand]? A. Several ... All covered with wood Q. Of what sort? A. Of white and red pine and a wood called `black oak'. Q. Is it of the species called `kaikatua'? A. It is. Q. Did you see any of the species called `cowrie'? A. I did not. <i>(1908,Historical Records,1/559</i>) [NOTES: 1821 May Evidence of McDonald before Commissioner Bigge in 1908 McNab Historical Records]



## oak-weed
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MARTIN</b> <i>Oakweed or Oak-leafed Sea-wrack (Landsburgia quercifolia)</i>. The OAK-WEED belongs to yet another endemic genus with two distinct species, ... [It bears] much-lobed leaves shaped like those of the English oak. <i>(1961,Flora NZ,16,1929 ed.</i>)



## Oamarooster
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  His name it isn't Thomson, / And he is no Masseyite; / But a life-long loyal Liberal, / Though not much given to "skite." / A well-known Oamarooster / On whom none look askance, / A chap who has been uster / Much dealings with finance; / A one-time Seddon booster / Who made the Tories dance. <i>(Oct 5, 1918,NZ Truth,6</i>)

>  When "Critic" pictures some of the blacksmith shops he knows still "carrying on," that Oamarooster "smiddy" must be in a bad way indeed. <i>(Oct 30, 1920,NZ Truth,1</i>)

>  An Oamarooster tells of an interesting case of egg-vitality which came under his notice in London. <i>(Oct 9, 1909,Observer (Auckland),16</i>)

>  "OAMAROOSTERS" Would Like to Leave the Country [hdg] Berry and Brunie Board a Boat And Indulge in "Wholesale Bribery and Corruption" But are Landed by the "Lor" <i>(Jul 7, 1917,NZ Truth,2</i>)



## Oamaru pudding
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McCREDIE</b> ... The Bungalow recipe Book 51 OAMARU PUDDING ... milk ... gelatine ... sugar ... rind of 1 lemon ... yolks of 2 eggs ... soak ... gelatine ... in ... milk ... boil the rind tn the rest ... add soaked gelatine and sugar; stir in the yolks ... strain into wet shape; turn out when cold, and stick almonds over it. <i>(1915,Bungalow Recipe Book, The</i>)



## Oamaru stone
 
 <i>n.</i> a white building stone formerly much used. [from Oamaru in the South Island]

>  The church will cost GBP20,000 finished, being built of concrete with Oamaru stone facings and ornaments. <i>(Feb 7, 1910,Colonist, The,2</i>)

>  On sale at Greenfield and Stewarts Steam Saw Mills, Courtenay Place ... Tombstones of Oamaru stone, with blue stone base. <i>(Jan 25, 1867,Evening Post,1</i>)

>  [Hdg] Rotten Oamaru stone on Auckland City Town Hall. <i>(Mar 13, 1926,NZ Observer,2</i>)

>  The complex was partly built from Oamaru stone, in imitation of Indian sandstone. <i>(Jun 10, 1992,Evening Post,23</i>)

>  Oamaru, with its brilliant white buildings of Oamaru stone, can be seen a long way off on a sunny day. <i>(Aug 11, 1938,NZ Observer,11</i>)

>  I presume this formation has been generally considered to overlie immediatley the oamaru building stone. ...Interposed between the `Blue clay' and Oamaru stone, is a layer of sand ... <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/168</i>) [NOTES: Traill Oamaru and Moeraki]

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> <i>(1874,Colonial Experiences,18</i>)

>  <b>VOGEL</b> Oamaru Stone <i>(1875,Official Handbook of NZ,107</i>)

>  The most important building material that hitherto has been used in Otago. viz. the Oamaru Stone. The use of this material is coeval with the settlement of the district in which it occurs, but it was little known beyond until 1866, when an export trade commenced with Dunedin... The Oamaru stone, correctly speaking, is a white granular limestone. <i>(1875,TrNZI,8/140</i>) [NOTES: Blair `Building Materials in Otago']

>  <b>HECTOR</b> `A white, granular limestone, called the Oamaru stone, is worked in extensive quarries in the Oamaru district. ...A considerable quantity has been exported to Melbourne.' <i>(1883,Handbook for New Zealand, The,64</i>)

>  A white granular limestone called the Oamaru stone is worked in extensive quarries in the Oamaru district; ... The principal buildings of Dunedin are built of this stone, which shows a very fair amount of durability. <i>(1892,NZ Official Handbook,197</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Oamaru Stone, n. Oamaru is a town on the east coast of the South Island of New Zealand. It produces a fine building stone. <i>(1898,Austral English,327</i>)

>  There is abundance of good building stone [in South Canterbury], chiefly bluestone (dolomite), and white stone something like the famous Oamaru stone, ... <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,129</i>)

>  Oamaru stone was used in these constructions. <i>(1914,History of North Otago,12,1978 ed.</i>)

>  There are some men sawing Oamaru stone in Princes Street [for the university building]. <i>(1923,Auckland Capping Book,16</i>)

>  <b>CRESSWELL</b> The architect was Strouts, and he set up on the bare plain an Elizabethan style in brick, faced with white Oamaru stone. <i>(1951,Canterbury Tales,81</i>)

>  <b>WALLIS</b> He was squatting on his heels on the [Christchurch] balcony, the Oamaru stone balustrade level with his head. <i>(1963,Point of Origin,108</i>)

>  ~ n. fine New Zealand building stone. [from name of place]. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621</i>)

>  Oamaru stone ... n. fine N.Z. building stone [From name of place]. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)

>  Oamaru Stone white NZ building stone. [NZ town] <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,516</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 334]]

>  <b>SIMMONS</b> There is a noticeable advantage attending the use of this Oamaru stone. <i>(1879,Old England and New Zealand,66</i>)

>  As all the houses [sc. in Oamaru] are built of the peculiar, handsome Oamaru white stone, the appearance is very striking. <i>(Sep 16, 1890,Otago Witness,34</i>)

>  The Oamaru stone is perhaps the best known building stone in Otago. <i>(Oct 4, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,15</i>)

>  The property consists of a large and elegant stone store .. built of Oamaru stone, and after the most approved style of architecture. <i>(Nov 2, 1865,North Otago Times,4</i>)

>  The plans of the Presbyterian Church .. represent a building of the Gothic order, which, when finished in Oamaru stone, will show considerable architectural beauty. <i>(Sep 1, 1864,Oamaru Times,1</i>)

>  The material to be employed [on the Episcopal Parish Church of St John, Tokomairiro] is pressed bricks, made in the neighbourhood. Oamaru stone dressings will be employed about the windows, and bands of the same and black glazed bricks will run round the building. <i>(Jan 27, 1866,Otago Witness,3</i>)

>  The beauty of the Oamaru stone, the facility with which it can be worked, and its capability to with stand the action of the atmosphere, will ensure its being largely used for building purposes so soon as it becomes generally known throughout the colonies. <i>(Jul 26, 1866,North Otago Times,3</i>)

>  Oamaru stone in Melbourne. I think the quality of Oamaru stone to which I refer is a much superior building material to any of the Hobart Town stone that I have seen in this Province. <i>(Nov 12, 1867,North Otago Times,2</i>)

>  The chalk stone is white - like Oamarustone of New Zealand - only finer anddenser in structure. <i>(Jan 2, 1918,Chronicles NZEF,III/34 : 19</i>)

>  Estimates given for Headstones in Marble, and Granite and Oamaru Stone; also Tomb Railings. <i>(Sep 22, 1896,North Otago Times,1</i>)

>  About GBP13,000 has been collected or promised towards the Roman Catholic Cathedral in Buckle-street, which is expected to cost GBP40,000. It will be built of brick and Oamaru stone. <i>(Mar 22, 1900,Taranaki Herald,3</i>)

>  Tenders were requested for erecting the building in Waikava [sic] stone, Oamaru stone, Portobello stone, and brick and cement. <i>(Aug 20, 1864,Otago Witness,13</i>)

>  From Fortification Hill, is being got the Oamaru stone which is being used in the erection of the Chief Post Office, in Dunedin, which stone is being shipped at Moeraki, as in every way the more convenient and safer place. <i>(Apr 15, 1865,Otago Witness,6</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MARSHALL</b> Our farm was in two parts ... The bottom place had an old Oamaru-stone house on it when my father bought it. <i>(1982,Master of Big Jingles,68</i>)

## oar-weed
 
 <i>n.</i> paddle-weed, octopus-weed

>  <b>MARTIN</b> <i>Oar-weed, Paddle-weed, or Octopus-weed, (<i>Ecklonia radiata</i>) ... It ranges to Otago, but is uncommon save on North Isalnd coasts ... <i>(1961,Flora NZ,11,1929 p. 3 ed.</i>)



## oarfish
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Regalecus</i> sp.

>  In November, 1902, I received a silvery Oar-fish, which was washed ashore ... near PortChalmers ... <i>(1904,TrNZI,36/198</i>)

>  <b>HUTTON</b> REGALECUS GLADIUS. Walb.? Oar Fish. <i>(1872,Fishes,35, 56</i>)

>  In shape, they are usually long, deep and very much compressed and flattened on thesides, so much so that their local appellations always embody some idea of these peculiarities - such as ribband-fish, lath or deal fish, band-fish or blade-fish, and also oar-fish. <i>(1881,TrNZI,13/196</i>) [NOTES: Clarke]

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> <i>Regalecus gladius</i> Walb. Oar-fish <i>(1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,303</i>)

>  On its arrival in Christchurch ... I found the fish to be a species of Regalecus, or oar-fish of unusually large proportions. <i>(1891,NZ Journal of Science,1/154</i>)

>  <i>On a Species of</i> Regalecus<i> or Great Oar-fish, caught in O'Kain's Bay.[title] </i> <i>(1892,TrNZI,24/192</i>)

>  <b>DRUMMOND & HUTTON</b> The flesh of deep sea fishes, such as the ribbon fish and the oarfish, becomes a jelly when boiled, and is quite unfit to eat. <i>(1902,Nature in NZ,74</i>)

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Regalecus pacificus</i> Haast. OAR-FISH. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,26,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SPEIGHT</b> The Oar-fish, 12 to 20 feet long, with a high, flame-tipped head-crest (the elongated fin rays) is probably the sea-serpent of repute. Its pelvic fins are narrow, elongated processe, hence the name Oar-fish. The Deal-fish is much smaller, but of the same type, but we know very little of the habits of either of them. <i>(1927,Natural History of Canterbury,198</i>) [NOTES: Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury]

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> [note to 1882 Oct 16 Found a peculiar fish stranded. 11ft. long ... Bright silver colour ...] Regalecus argentius, Oar fish, a deep sea fish. <i>(1950,Chudleigh Diary,313</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Oarfish <i>Regalecus pacificus</i> Haast Syn. <i>R. argenteus</i> Parker) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,404</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>WILSON</b> OARFISH A strange fish that is believed to have contributed to at least some of the sea serpent legends. The Oarfish belongs to the Ribbonfish family and is long, thin and extreamely fragile. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,170</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> RIBBONFISHES: Order ALLOTRIOGNATHI ... Long, thin fishes of this group ... sometimes come [ashore.] [captions Fig. 65] 1. Oarfish, <i>Regalecus</i> ... 5. Ribbonfish, <i>Agrostichthys. </i> <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,216</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>OARFISH Regalecus glesne (Regalecus pacificus</i>) ... It has been suggested that sightings of large oarfishes have been responsible for sailors' reports of sea serpents. <i>(1982,Collins Guide,190,1984 ed.</i>)

>  [species name] oarfish .. [scientific name] <i>Regalecus glesne</i> .. [other name(s)] -- <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Oarfish Regalecus glesne</i> World-wide; ... <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,69</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] oarfish 1088-1101 OARFISHES 1086-1101 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

>  Mr. Travers gave an interesting account of several rare species of fish found on our coasts. Among them is Bank's oarfish. One of these was cast ashore at Nelson, and is now in the British Museum; it is of an extraordinary form, fifteen feet in length, twice that width [?], and almost as thin as the blade of an oar. <i>(Sep 26, 1868,Otago Witness,13</i>)

>  Mr Travers gave a very interesting account of several rare species of fish which had been found on our coasts, such as Bank's oarfish, one of these (the fourth of the kind ever obtained) was cast ashore at Nelson, and was now in the British Museum; it was of an extraordinary form, 15 feet in length, twice that width, and almost as thin as the blade of an oar. <i>(Sep 17, 1868,Wellington Independent,3</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Regalecus argenteus</i> Parker. PARKER'S OAR-FISH. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,26,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] oarfish, Parker's 1090 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>POWELL</b> SILVERY OARFISH (<i>Regalecus argenteus</i>).This is a bizarre inhabitant of the ocean depths, which comes to the surface on rare occasions in New Zealand waters....On the under side near the head are two long feelers with broadened tips and from these it gets its popular name of oar-fish. Oar-fishes of several species are widely distributed.... <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,65</i>)

>  <b>PAUL ET AL.</b> [index to common names 283-293] oarfish, silvery [TS] 1090, 1099 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)



## oat
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <i>Oat (&omacr.t), n. (Bot.) [Add.] Oat grass, Anthistirta avenacea. - Wild oats</i>, the seaside brome grass <i>(Bromus arenarius). See under GRASS. </i> <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2028</i>)



## oat races
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> At or near Maori villages courses were formed through scrub and tea tree and, to keep within the law, many of the prizes were sacks of oats. The term "oat races" is thought to have sprung from this time. <i>(1987,Racing for Gold,72</i>)

>  <b>HULME</b> I have never won anything. I reach across, grab his hand, shake it. ... `Bloody oath, Mat what good luck!' <i>(1986,Te Kaihau,79</i>)



## oatmeal water
 
 <i>n.</i> brose, a refreshment for shearers or outdoor workers, a mixture of water, sugar and oatmeal.

>  [We] supped on the last three sheep's tongues, washed down with oatmeal water and the last drop of whisky. <i>(Apr 7, 1883,Otago Witness,9</i>)

>  OATMEAL WATER: A billy of water in which oatmeal and sugar is mixed is kept in the shearing shed. Plain water is inclined to have a weakening effect on anyone who is working hard. <i>(1949,High Country Days,196</i>)

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> After breakfast Dad left the house with mugs and a cream can of oatmeal water - a refreshing drink of rain water, lemon juice and a large handful of oatmeal. <i>(1987,Swagger on Our Doorstep,188</i>)

>  If very thirsty, drink a little tea (cold or hot) or half a pint of oatmeal water. <i>(Feb 28, 1883,Tuapeka Times,6</i>)

>  That oatmeal water is an excellent drink for people who are troubled with constipation. <i>(Feb 20, 1907,Otago Witness,65</i>)

>  A capital substitute for beer in hot weather ... boil the oatmeal water, stir thoroughly, when cold add the lime juice and sugar, and then use ... It is known as "Stokos", and is popular as the best drink for hard work. <i>(Jan 30, 1886,Tuapeka Times,2</i>)

>  To remove freckles from the face and hands try rubbing the juice of a lemon on at night, then in the morning to soften the skin bathe the face in oatmeal water. <i>(Apr 1, 1887,Otago Witness,32</i>)

>  To accomplish the work nearly 5000 men were employed, and they worked two successive days of 17 hours with only short intervals for means. The strain on men was exceptional, yet not beer but oatmeal and water was provided by the railway company ... This triumph of oatmeal over beer should not pass unnoticed. <i>(Oct 11, 1892,Hawera & Normanby Star,2</i>)

>  For very young infants, in lieu of mother's or nurse's milk, cream with barley, rice, or oatmeal water, to which milk, sugar, common salt, phosphate of lime, or sode [?], or limewater, in small quantities is added, seems best. <i>(May 7, 1890,Tuapeka Times,4</i>)

>  The honor carried with it the all-too-severe sentence of two years' reformative treatment at Pakatoa, where shikkers are herded together to cultivate a taste for nothing stronger than milk shake and oatmeal water. <i>(Jun 20, 1914,NZ Truth,2</i>)

>  When you have heavy or tiring work to do, do not take either Beer or spirits. By far the best drink is thin oatmeal water, with a little sugar. <i>(Jan 28, 1888,Te Aroha News,1</i>)

## Oceania
 
 <i>n.</i> Australia, New Zealand and the Pacific Islands

>  Big international corporates are now beginning to use the nearly 200-year old term "Oceania" to refer to their Australia, New Zealand and Pacific Islands operations. The very word "Oceania" makes this seem like a much more attractive part of the world than "Australasia" does; it glistens with moisture, just as a seashell or rock under water is more tantalisingly pretty than when it is dried off and disappointingly dull. <i>(Oct 28, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),27</i>)

## octopus clamp
 
 <i>n.</i> a hold in wrestling, the trademark of Lofty Blomfield [ca1938 ca1930s (R. Mason)]

>  <b>McGILL</b> Wrestling holds were popular. The half-nelson, the full nelson, the octopus, the scissors. <i>(1989,Kiwi Baby Boomers,47</i>)

>  Lofty Blomfield is returning to New Zealand because Australian referees object to his "octopus clamp" hold. <i>(Sep 27, 1943,NZEF Times,III/117 : 12</i>)

>  Lofty evened the count with an octopus clamp that brooked no denial. <i>(Apr 28, 1938,NZ Observer,22</i>)

>  When Lofty gets that octopus clamp on the nozzle of a petrol pump, look out! <i>(Nov 24, 1938,NZ Observer,5</i>)

>  <b>SMITH</b> Octopus cramp. [jocular] <i>(1947,From N to Z</i>) [NOTES: 1947 Smith From N to Z in 1965 Reid 155]

>  <b>GEE</b> ` ... Remember Earl McCready and Lofty Blomfield? We'll do a few holds, eh? Reckon you can get the octopus clamp on me?' ... `Try the old clamp, eh? Blomfield special?' <i>(1965,Special Flower,41</i>)

>  <b>McCARTHY</b> Lofty [Blomfield] was just perfecting his own special hold, the Octopus Clamp or, as Hutter used to call it, the Rangitoto special. It was applied by Blomfield scissoring his opponent's legs while on the ground, then by force of his legs bringing the opponent over on to his face, at the same time forcing the opponent's knee up over his own hnee while still retaining the scissors. <i>(1973,Listen...!,55</i>)

>  St Kilda: Famous for lifesavers and brass bands. Labour's plan to give Golden Kiwi grants to brass bands composed of life-savers should strengthen its octopus clamp on St Kilda. <i>(Nov 28, 1981,Listener (New Zealand Listener),33/1</i>)

>  <b>SMITH</b> ... throws him over the ropes and then throws him back again; puts on a couple of octupus [sic] cramps [sic] (or should it be octupi cramp ?) ... <i>(1947,From N to Z,111</i>)

>  Loft Blomfield and his famous hold the "octopus clamp" was part of New Zealand's wrestling history, as was Earl McCready and his famous "rocking chair splits" hold. <i>(Jun 2003,NZ Memories,No. 42/54</i>)

>  There are neat vignettes of social history: Aunt Daisy in full incoherence; suspender belts; Lofty Blomfield and his Octopus Clamp. <i>(Jul 10, 1999,Listener (New Zealand Listener),46</i>)

>  Today, when anybody finds himself in aninextricable position, he is said to bein an "octopus clamp." <i>(Jun 22, 1950,Southern Cross (Wgton),11</i>)

>  Thereby hangs the tale of the "Octopus Clamp" and the new name of Maynell Lofty Blomfield. The hold is a cross between that Boston crab of Jack Forsgren's and Chief Thunderbird's Indian deathlock. <i>(Jan 19, 1938,Auckland Weekly News,63</i>)

>  It is evident that Lofty has developed his specialty, the octopus clamp, during his tour of the United States, and now clamps it on from all angles. <i>(Apr 27, 1938,Auckland Weekly News,64</i>)

>  <b>INGRAM</b> Wellingtonians saw Alf Jenkins.. untangling the wrestlers from what was reported as "a clamp applied by an octopus". Thus was born the octopus clamp. <i>(1962,Legends in Their Lifetime,18</i>)

>  Perhaps Blomfield is most famous for hisoctopus clamp, the most deadly hold ofall, which has electrified many anaudience and incapacitated many anopponent <i>(Jun 18, 1949,Southern Cross (Wgton),2</i>)

>  Kenneth, in the sixth round, was caught in an octopus clamp. <i>(Nov 24, 1941,NZEF Times,11</i>)

## odd
 
 <i>adj.</i> Constr: the odd + singular noun a few, one or two; + plural noun sense [CCR notes means something close to occasional] [ca1946-50 (frequent) eg. `I'll just have the odd beer with you; I'm in a hurry.' [=a few beers]]

## odd-bod
 
 <i>n.</i> 1.a person who cannot be classified into a social or personal type; someone of no fixed classification; no certain type; `odd-man-out'



 
 <i>n.</i> 1.a person who cannot be classified into a social or personal type; someone of no fixed classification; no certain type; `odd-man-out'

>  Are you a `type' or an `odd-bod'? It was several days before I fathomed this out. The cry would go up, `Ruapehu types arriving, get cracking.' The best club cups would be sorted out, an official or prominent member would greet the new-comers show them proudly over the house and dispense tea and hospitality with a cordial air. Then I'd hear, `Odd bods coming up the track'. A member would supervise his arrival, see that no one entered the club-room with nailed boots on, and offer a cup of tea, but there was not that ostentatious display of club china, `eats' and cordiality. I found that a `type' is a member of a recognised club, and therefore someone to be treated with respect. An `odd bod' is also a tramper or mountaineer, but of no specified breed, though entitled to shelter. The same law holds for bush and mountain: hospitality must be dispensed for folk of the right ilk, so that the billy is always on the boil. But tourists ! Well, I'd just hate to be a tourist ! They were treated with the loftiest contempt. I suppose I came under the heading `odd bod' vouched for by a `type'. ... <i>(Jun 14, 1951,NZ Woman's Weekly,24</i>)

>  <b>TRAIL</b> He was an odd-bod of a man who worked a claim in one of the dim, sunless gullies up one of the tributaries of the Arrow River. <i>(1984,Child of the Arrow,82</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a crank; a peculiar or disliked person [(University undergraduates) ca1946 [These senses are probably merely general English]]

The Miner's Hall / was there for the weekend pictures, the Women's Institute / Concert, and big hoolies like the Centennial Ball. Oddfellows / to me were just peppermints. <i>(Sep 28, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),61</i>) [NOTES: Jeffrey Paparoa Holman]

## OE
 
 <i>n.</i> overseas experience [ORIGIN: initialism] (See [big OE](../B#big-oe))

>  Like <i>The Book of Fame</i>, then, this novel depicts the compulsion of OE. <i>(Mar 16, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),59</i>)

>  The quins are now at their OE stage - Deborah lives with her older sister Lee-Ann in Kansas, Lisa and Shirleen flat together in Sydney, ... <i>(Apr 8, 1986,Evening Post,22</i>)

>  I actually got married and Peter and I went on the 'big OE'. <i>(Oct 1999,Tui Motu Interislands,11</i>)

>  <b>IVORY</b> OE ... Overseas Experience <i>(1982,Pacific Index of Abbreviations,229</i>)

>  Mum looks leave her alone, [sic] `She hasn't settled down from her OE yet, we've got to give her time to sort herself out,' buttering scones. <i>(Nov 1984,Islands,34/95</i>) [NOTES: Chalmers]

>  Julia became interested in goats while doing her O.E. on the cheap more than 15 years ago. <i>(Oct 1985,More Magazine,69</i>)

>  OE abbr. Overseas Experience. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,519</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]

>  <b>OVENDEN</b> O.E., as every New Zealander knows, means Overseas Experience. The term refers, as in such colloquial phrases as: `He's gone to get his O.E., eh,'; or `Got your O.E. yet then?' to that most rare of concepts, an abstract consumer durable. It has little in common with the Australian vernacular `O.S.', which is a mere descriptive term, as in `He's gone O.S. and won't come back.' <i>(1986,O.E.,6</i>)

>  O.E. falls into a tangle [heading] In his new novel O.E. Keith Ovenden returns to the basic elements of 1984's Ratatui ... Despite the bludgeoning conclusiveness of its title O.E. leaves a great deal unsaid about the strange place overseas experience holds in our cultural texture. <i>(Nov 1, 1986,Dominion,5</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDSON</b> She was to spend six months in London. `O.E. at last,' she said. <i>(1986,Choices,64</i>)

>  We had our formal educations, our OE, our 2.5 children and we were mortgaged, it seemed, for ever. <i>(May 1987,Metro,62</i>) [NOTES: Roger `Auckland Life']

>  <b>McGILL</b> OE overseas experience, the young Kiwi's working holiday abroad, mandatory and meaningful; coined by John Muirhead and popularised in late 1970s by friend and columnist Tom Scott; in 1980s, title of Keith Ovenden novel. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,78</i>)

>  Most of my friends are either post-OEers - full of tales from afar - or ante-OEers - full of tales about what they're going to do when they go afar... But these trips don't cut the mustard as far as serious OEing goes. <i>(Feb 6, 2000,Sunday Star Times,D2</i>)

>  Suddenly it seemed everyone under 25 had done a couple of years of what became known as OE. <i>(Mar 2004,North & South,65</i>)

>  I need change. / New people, new face, new ideas. / An OE, I guess you'd call it, only it doesn't have to be overseas, / just anywhere will do. <i>(Aug 9, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),45</i>)

>  The paper [by Auckland Professor Kerr Inkson] compared New Zealanders who went on OE - and found their own jobs while they were away - with those who were sent by their employers for an expatriate assignment (EA). <i>(Sep 13, 1997,Dominion,1</i>)

>  In the second, a young woman on "oe" prints out an enormous photograph of the view from her home on Auckland's North Shore and pastes it on the wall of her cellar flat before calling her mum... <i>(Apr 4, 2001,Dominion,24</i>)

>  He met Lesley in Scotland. when he was over there "doing my OE", and they started out as sharemilkers in Te Puke. <i>(Nov 1992,North & South,90</i>)

>  Camilla Bellich says student debt has turned OE into "a money-making venture as opposed to a holiday or a life-experience. . . ." <i>(Nov 2005,North & South,38</i>)

>  One is this very New Zealand thing of going out in our 20s, like to war, or OE, and seeing these extraordinary places, but actually ending up finding out more about ourselves. <i>(Nov 21, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),36</i>)

>  Three years ago she did her OE in somewhat unsual style, starting with South America. <i>(May 5, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),44</i>)

>  The great OE, the New Zealand rite of passage, tattooed in our psyche, embedded in our genes. Young New Zealanders have been setting off on their OEs for over half a century. <i>Ibid.</i> 33 "OE has become an almost obligatory rite of cultural consumerism... For a middle-class young Pakeha New Zealander, not taking an OE is now unusual." <i>(Apr 12, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32</i>)

>  I found on my brief OE that Englishmeneverywhere warmed appreciably when I wasable to prove I wasn't from Queensland. <i>(Aug 21, 1976,Listener (New Zealand Listener),12</i>)

>  <b>SCOTT</b> We are both suffering from what the richcall a jaded palate, others call"Cultural Imperialism" and what mysophisticated friends call OE depression.OE, of course, is the abbreviation foroverseas experience - and no NewZealander, it is claimed, is completewithout it. As well as being vital toemotional and intellectual (and sexual)development, OE very nicely fills thatawkward gap between high school andmarriage. <i>(1977,Tom Scott's Life & Times,76</i>)

>  Now the average Kiwi going on his or her OE is older and, arguably, smarter. Far from funding their OE working in a London pub, they're more likely to work in an office... Auckland's Jess Startup, 24, who went overseas in 1998, believes the pub-working OE is a thing of the past for most New Zealanders... For example, going to teach English in Korea or Japan is a lucrative way of having a Big OE. <i>(Aug 23, 2003,Dominion Post,F1</i>)

>  Horror of horrors, there are returning OErs among us who are smarting from the fact that their visas expired, or that they ran out of money in Pisa, or Florence, or Rome, or that they had to come home for a family wedding and can't afford to get away again. <i>(Feb 10, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7</i>)

>  Most of my friends are either post-OEers - full of tales from afar - or ante-OEers - full of tales about what they're going to do when they go afar... But these trips don't cut the mustard as far as serious OEing goes. <i>(Feb 6, 2000,Sunday Star Times,D2</i>)



## oehiakura
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Oehiakura, Lindsay. <i>Dicksonia squarrosa. </i> <i>(1867,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766</i>)



## OEing
 
 <i>n.</i> [doing OE - used as verbal noun]

>  Most of my friends are either post-OEers - full of tales from afar - or ante-OEers - full of tales about what they're going to do when they go afar... But these trips don't cut the mustard as far as serious OEing goes. <i>(Feb 6, 2000,Sunday Star Times,D2</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> overseas travelling

>  ...there's a wistfulness developing which wasn't there when our offsprings' OEing first began. <i>(Jun 2004,North & South,10</i>)



## off
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  Off.- (1) See Near. (2) Slang. A shearer speaks of being o. when he has finished a sheep and put him out through the porthole (C.) <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>BOWEN</b> NOTE: The sheep's right legs and right side are referred to as the `near' and the sheep's left side and legs as the `off.' <i>(1955,Wool Away!,21</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> get rid of

>  He is presently working out exactly which board members and which senior management he will be offing. <i>(Mar 18, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),21</i>)



## off like redshanks
 
 <i>phr.</i> departing quickly

>  <b>CHAMIER</b> Without another thought or look behind us we were off like red shanks. <i>(1891,Philosopher Dick,466</i>)

>  They would be off into the bush like redshanks. <i>(1903,NZ Illustrated Magazine,8/93</i>)



## off one's dot
 
 <i>phr.</i> on model OFF ONE'S HEAD, indicating madness, drunkenness, or under the influence of drugs [(obsolescent)]

>  His Worship: `Was the accused sober?' Witness: `Well, he looked muzzy.' His Worship: `What do you mean by that?' Witness: `He looked either drunk or a shingle short.' His Worship: `A what?' Witness: `A screw loose.' His Worship: `I don't understand you.' Witness: `He seemed to have a rat in his garret.' His Worship: `Oh, speak English!' Witness: `Off his dot - balmy [sic] - dilly.' Sergeant: `He says the man was mad, your Worship.' Witness: `No, I didn't. I said he was either drunk or ratty.' <i>(Apr 16, 1910,NZ Truth,251/1/6</i>)



## off one's nana
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>H.S. Gajadhar (Wellington)</b> <i>(1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: [AND 1.[?] off one's nana 1966 2. a foolish person1941.]]



## off one's pannikin
 
 <i>phr.</i> off one's head, crazy

>  .. if this writer is not right off his `pannikin'. <i>(Feb 24, 1906,NZ Truth,36/5/6</i>)

>  Waterloo [a footballer], however, `goes off his pannikin' [`does his block'] with excitement at times. <i>(May 26, 1906,NZ Truth,49/2/8</i>)

>  <i>pannican ... n</i>. small tin cup used in the bush: (<i>Coll.</i>) the head; <i>off one's pannican,</i> insane, demented; <i>pannican boss,</i> subordinate manager or overseer as opposed to big or quartpot boss. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),15</i>)



## off the blades
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sheep] newly shorn.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> It was the practice then to dip the wethers off the blades (straight after shearing) <i>(1947,Wayleggo,39</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> off the blades: Newly shorn i.e. sheep dipped straight after shearing are dipped 'off the blades.' <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,188</i>)



## off the books
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [ farm stock] purchased unseen, solely from description.

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Instead, therefore, of collecting the sheep from every part of the run and counting them in the yards, we took delivery 'off the books' that is, we accepted the flock on the previous shearing and lambing tally. <i>(1921,Tutira,154</i>)



## off the chain
 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> out on the loose for the evening, on the loose (temporarily, esp. from wife), free from restraint. [1947 only[???]]

>  If he can't tell his son from his daughter, or his uncle from his aunt, the old guy shouldn't be off the chain - he's myopic as well as sozzled. <i>(Jan 31, 1944,NZEF Times,III/136 : 7</i>)



## off the forks
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [potatoes] purchased unseen, directly from harvest.

>  While a proportion of the crop is sold "off the forks," the greater part is pitted on the grower's farm immediately after digging. <i>(Aug 15, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,141</i>)



## off the hill
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [high country] used with reference to the completion of a mustering beat or a return to the station.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> The season at Mesopotamia finished when snow drove us off the hill, while scratching for sheep ... <i>(1947,Wayleggo,63</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> But we got our mail, and were glad to be for a day or so 'off the hill.' <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,29</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> We got 'off the hill' quite early, put the sheep across Cat Creek at Tarndale, and raced for the homestead. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,18</i>)



## off the hook(s)
 
 <i>phr.</i> (of clothes and occas. other articles), ready-made, off the peg [ca1938 eg I walked in and bought a suit straight off the hooks]



## off the pine
 
 <i>phr.</i> off the reserves bench

>  He added another test cap, again off the pine, against Scotland and now he's earned himself the starting spot for Argentina. <i>(Dec 2, 2001,Sunday Star Times,B3</i>)



## off the shears
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sheep] newly shorn. (See [off the blades](../O#off-the-blades))

>  Now, any practical man holding such country would naturally like to have the option of dipping these dry sheep off the shears and be done with them until the following shearing. <i>(Jun 1898,NZ Farmer,201</i>)

>  "I believe it to be possible to eradicate scab, even on a fully stocked run, and I advise "W.," when he intends passing the "point," instead of bothering with "microscopic observations," to have his sheep dipped "off the shears" in a sheepwash of 10lbs of good manufactured tobacco and 10lbs of sulphur, to 100 gallons of water; dip again immediately the sheep are all shorn, in a sheepwash of the above strength ... " <i>(Dec 16, 1865,Otago Witness,3</i>)

>  We have enquiries for half-bred hoggetts [sic] and wethers off the shears. <i>(Oct 2, 1876,North Otago Times,2</i>)

>  .. a woolgrower's hoggets proved to be larger off the shears ... <i>(Sep 1, 1925,NZ Farmer,1340</i>)

>  <b>STACEY</b> By very careful mustering and dipping 'off the shears' it was made obvious which sheep had been dipped. <i>(1996,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,147</i>)

>  The inspector replied that the law was altered to meet that; sheep had not sufficient wool on them when dipped off the shears to retain the dip, and whether dipped off the shears or not they must also be dipped between the dates mentioned. <i>(May 14, 1896,Otago Witness,31</i>)

>  <b>EVANS</b> Seventy or more newly shorn sheep lost off shears in the rain. <i>(Feb 17, 1868,Letter,242</i>) [NOTES: in VANCE High Endeavour (1965)]

>  Wanted, 3,000 Hogget Merino wethers, off the shears. <i>(Nov 29, 1872,Hawke's Bay Herald,1</i>)

>  A & P Conference iv Numbers have been found dying off the shears in many cases ... [of blood poisoning.] <i>(Aug 1900,NZ Farmer,Supplement</i>)

>  It would be decidedly a good plan to dip both ewes and lambs off the shears with a good non-poisonous dip. <i>(Nov 20, 1915,NZ Journal of Agriculture,456</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> He found that he used to lose hundreds of them when he drove them over Porter's Pass off the shears if he had bad weather. After that he had no losses off the shears ... <i>(1930,Early Canterbury Runs,241</i>)

>  The new season's two-tooth wethers came off the shears in good order and made 16/- to 16/6. <i>(Nov 2, 1939,NZ Farmer,34</i>)

>  Forward-conditioned two-tooths off the shears have reached 63/4 ... <i>(Oct 25, 1951,NZ Farmer,34</i>)

>  <b>MAF</b> Off-shears: newly-shorn sheep. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,51</i>)

>  They [the sheep] should be 3 to 6 weeks off the shears. <i>(Dec 21, 1988,NZ Farmer,7</i>)

>  The big entry of prime sheep, with 100 lots being auctioned saw values ease by $2 especially for autumn shorn and off-shear lots. [off-shears; off shears] <i>(Aug 22, 1996,NZ Farmer,35</i>)

>  All hands who have recently tried dipping off shears now swear by that system. <i>(Apr 12, 1900,Otago Witness,5</i>)

>  <b>REES</b> Flocks of sheep - `off the shears' -->off the shears -->of sheep, newly shorn; (cf OFF THE BLADES) -- <i>(1924,April's Sowing,162</i>)

>  ... the twice-shorn sheep came off the shears in October in a very much better condition than the once-shorn. <i>(1929,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,10/326</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> The machines shear too near the skin and when the almost naked sheep go out into the high tops `off the shears,' the first cold storm which comes sweeping down, sends them cowering under every ledge of rock. <i>(1951,High Country,26</i>)

>  <b>MILLS</b> ... they were coming [58] `off the shears' with that yellow, `doughy' appearance which every shearer knows all too unfavourably. <i>(1960,Sheep-O,57</i>)

>  <i>(phrase)</i>. sheep that have just been shorn. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),624</i>)

>  <i>shears, off the; off the wool (Phr.) </i>sheep newly shorn. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),17</i>)

>  <b>BRADSHAW</b> NZ of Today <i>(1888,New Zealand Of Today,110</i>)



## off the spurs
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [mustering] having completed a job or not working.

>  Cowhands, cooks, clerks and sorters joined the musterers off the spurs and with rabbiters, yardmen and packmen streaked towards the bedlam in the shed. <i>(May 1, 1945,NZ Dairy Exporter,85</i>)



## off the turnips
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural, derogatory) from the countryside

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> My first steamrollered week at Riwaka School, hot off the turnips, included: 'Wanna try an electric shock?' <i>(1983,Down from Marble Mountain,83</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> turnips, straight off the. Applied to one who is a country bumpkin or very green: New Zealanders' (-1932) <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,918</i>)

>  <b>BARDSLEY</b> .. she remembers walking along the street when a WAAF laughed at the Land Service uniform and remarked 'off the turnips.' <i>(2000,Land Girls, The,74</i>)

## off-course substitute
 
 <i>n.</i> [horse racing 195? R.Mason (fide N.M. Adams) When a bettor through the T.A.B. bets on a double and the horse on the second leg of the double is scratched after the bet is laid the favourite at the tote on the course is substituted in the bet for the scratched horse. This substituted horse is an `off course substitute']

>  [Hdg] Off-Course Substitute. Now thatthe T.A.B. is well established, I suggestthey cut out the off-course substitute... The off-course substitute is the horsefavoured most in second-leg wagering bybackers using the T.A.B. The decision toemploy this method of transferring thewinning first-leg tickets when thepunters' nominated second leg does notstart was arrived at after matureconsideration. <i>(Jun 2, 1954,NZ Truth,3</i>)



## off-farm
 
 <i>adv.</i> off the farm

>  Perhaps it is operating as a farm-stay to earn a few dollars, or one spouse is working off-farm to service the family's debt... <i>Ibid.</i> 21 Then you've got another group who are getting a significant proportion of their revenue off-farm. <i>(Mar 22, 1997,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)



## off-graze
 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to graze stock off a property when feed is low.

>  Our policy of off-grazing ram hoggets for 6 months .. lets us carry more breeding ewes. <i>(2002,Internet - see Notes,0</i>) [NOTES: weblink.co.nz/Twin Farm]

>  In Southland most farmers off-graze their stock during winter to avoid damaging pasture with soil pugging. <i>(Feb 2001,Waikato Times,08</i>)



## off-paddock
 
 <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of stock ] on stand-off or wintering pads.

>  Many deer farms winter weaners in off-paddock systems .. We then put the off-paddock wintered weaners back onto pasture for spring ... <i>(Jul 2002,NZ Deer Farmer,12</i>)



## off-season
 
 <i>n.</i> In NZ various specific applications espec. farming) freezing works (opposed to killing season), ? oyster dredging, as distinct from close(d) season [[Is this entry necessary?]]

>  This is a seasonal job and gives rise to phrases like: `Chaps winter at a certain job (e.g. shearing) in the off-season, and come back regularly. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: p.c. Colin Gordon]



## off-shears
 
 <i>adj.</i> of sheep newly-shorn [auctioneers']

>  Off-shears: Newly-shorn sheep. A sale term. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,51</i>)

>  off-shears NZ & Austral. (of sheep) recently shorn. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,520</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]

>  Sheep may be dipped straight "off-shears". <i>(Jan 1960,NZ Dairy Exporter,20</i>)



## off-sort
 
 <i>n.</i> a fleece which, when classed or graded, does not fit the established lines and is binned [woolgrowers']

>  Off-sort: Fleece which, when classing or grading, does not fit the established lines and isbinned. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,57</i>)



## office
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  *Office.-A small patch of grass high up in an almost perpendicular rock wall, which offers only one way in and out for a few sheep or even a dog. The word was in general use among the genuine high country men of 40 or 50 years ago, but may (a correspondent says) not be much used now, as too many modern musterers are conveniently blind to an o. <i>(Jan 27, 1934,Press, The,15</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [high country] a small patch of grass on a ledge, barely accessible to stock.

>  Office.- A small patch of grass high up in an almost perpendicular rock wall, which offers only one way in and out for a few sheep or even a dog. <i>(Jan 27, 1934,Press, The  (Acland Glossary),15</i>)

>  An office is a challenge - a small ledge on a face where a sheep can be confined. <i>(2001,Questionnaire Respondents (DB)</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> to give a warning (signal) [(obs criminal slang)]

>  Sullivan, from his spot, gave me `office', that Mr. Ken was not alone. <i>(1866,Maungatapu Murders,71</i>)



## Office of Treaty Settlements
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  For almost seven years research has been gathered and submissions made to the Waitangi Tribunal and Office of Treaty Settlements. <i>(Dec 1996,North & South,35</i>)

>  The Office of Treaty Settlements negotiates with the claimants to settle the claim. <i>(Dec 13, 2003,Dominion Post,E2</i>)



## Official Assignee
 
 <i>n.</i> a state official who holds and administers the property of bankrupts, and in some cases acts as the liquidator of insolvent companies. [Insolvency Act 1967, s.15]



## Official Cash Rate
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  What's the Official Cash Rate? The interest rate, set by the Reserve Bank, at which banks lend money to each other... He raised the Official Cash Rate, the rate at which banks lend to each other, from 4.5 percent to 5 percent. <i>(Nov 17, 1999,Evening Post,1</i>)



## offside
 
 <i>adj.</i> of a bullock, the right-hand side facing the front



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to work the offsiders in a bullock team.

>  My husband was trying to teach me to off-side but I'm afraid I was not as brave as he would have liked. <i>(Sep 13, 1951,NZ Farmer,62</i>)

## offside with
 
 <i>phr.</i> on the wrong side of, in bad with [OED no transf uses, only literal sporting, from Assoc. Football; so W3: nf P. [NZ use coloured by Rugby Union]

>  <b>SIMPSON</b> To be `Off-side' with the Maori residents is to be deprived of even these unreliable services. <i>(1950,Chatham Exiles,149</i>)

>  <b>COMBS</b> Harris began to put himself off-side with some of the leading citizens. <i>(1947,Half-Lengths,8</i>)

>  <b>HASCOMBE</b> As well as ... putting us `ofside' with neighbours, ... [he] is now a canine luxury par excellence. <i>(1969,Down and Almost Under,32</i>)

>  Our 'moonlighters' are making themselves heard this last few nights, plenty 'offside.' <i>(Jul 28, 1894,Observer (Auckland),21</i>)

>  No, Bay, you are offside and it will be the wish of every sport in the Dominion, in or out of Hawkes Bay, that there will be some strict "referees" at Wellington on April 23. <i>(Apr 18, 1925,NZ Truth,11</i>)



## offsider
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>REED</b> The cleared land would then be ploughed with one or two pairs of bullocks, and though the ploughman would not have the assistance of a driver ... the offsider would not put a foot out of the furrow from end to end. <i>(1953,Story of the Kauri, The,184</i>) [NOTES: (D.J.McLeod)]



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  And up lumbered the heavily-laden waggon with its driver, "offsider," and twelve horses. <i>(Dec 24, 1897,Bruce Herald,6</i>)

>  They went as what is called 'offsiders,' to drive spare bullocks, round up the bullocks straying from camp, flog up any lagging offside bullock, and generally to be useful in forming camp, cooking, and chopping wood, and drawing water - work such as the scarecrow boy from your hedgerow could do just as well. <i>(Aug 1, 1899,Star, The,2</i>)

>  The bullocky's offsider was the chap who helped the bullock driver, and the origin of the name `offsider' came about this way; very often when a team is operating it is necessary for someone to run round on the off side and push or force point bullocks over quickly ... for instance when going round a corner or avoiding some obstacle ... also to give general help. <i>(1951,Radio NZ / National Radio</i>) [NOTES: [a1914] 1951 H.S.McCarroll The Days of the Kauri Bushmen [Radio Talk 3: TS]]

>  <b>REED</b> The bullock-driver usually had an assistant called an offsider. ... At one time I engaged an intelligent young Maori, a likeable, willing fellow, as off-sider. <i>(1953,Story of the Kauri, The,184</i>) [NOTES: (D.J. McLeod)]

>  <b>MOORE</b> [Northland c 1920] The team was hitched to the rear of the waggon, and the Maori `offsider', a somewhat irresponsible [43] youth was instructed to guide the two polers .... <i>(1969,From Forest to Farm,42</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [sp. use] an assistant to a station or shearing gang cook.

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> At Waimate there were about 50 men and boys employed for the shearing .. 1 cook's mate, generally known as the offsider, or 'slushy.' <i>(1940,Te Waimate,127</i>)

>  <b>HAMILTON</b> If there were more than a certain number of men to be provided for, the cook was given an assistant, generally known as his 'off-sider.' <i>(1952,History of Northern Southland,45</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Both the old packie and the cook's 'off-sider' were well past cricketing days. <i>(1949,High Country Days,59</i>)

>  <b>MCLEOD</b> One classer, two rollers .. make up the shed contingent and the cook and 'offsider' complete the total. <i>(1951,NZ High Country,27</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (See [slushy](../S#slushy))

>  <b>SMYTH</b> The Maori boy who acted as the cook's `offsider'. <i>(1929,Girl from Mason Creek, The,206</i>)

>  Off-sider.- Assistant. Now mostly applied to the slushy, or cook's mate, but the term may have originated with the old bullock drivers, who drove their teams from the near side. I have oftenhelped the Glentanner bullock driver across the Tasman, by riding on the off side with a whip to keep the bullocks up stream; and he always asked if I would `off-side' for him. I have heard a bullocky speak of his dog as his o.s. <i>(Jan 27, 1934,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> Many of the old [c 1880s] station cooks, with an offsider (mate) .. <i>Ibid.</i> 91 Many of the old station cooks, with the assistance of an `offsider' (mate) found no difficulty in cooking for fifty or sixty men. ... it has to be remembered that ... bread cost only 6d. or 7d. a 4lb. loaf, and tea (post and rails) could be bought for 1/- per lb. Some of these contract cooks were great fossickers and managed to find quite a lot of green stuff for vegetables ... <i>Ibid.</i> 127 At Waimate there were about 50 men and boys employed for the shearing - 22 shearers, 1 sheep-oh, 4 fleece-pickers, 4 wool-rollers, 1 brander, 1 wool-classer, 1 classer's boy, 3 pressers, 1 waggoner, 1 cook, 1 cook's mate, generally known as the offsider or `slushy' 8 shepherds; and last, but not least the shed boss, ... [fn] In the early days the manager acted as shed boss. Later, when the agricultural work increased, a special shed boss was engaged. <i>(1940,Te Waimate,75</i>)

>  <b>BRUNO</b> [The cook] hearing of the unexpected arrival of a number of reinstouchments snarled to his offsiders. <i>(1944,Desert Daze,49</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> OFFSIDER: A man employed to help the cook. He does the dishes, prepares vegetables, etc., and in time may graduate to a cook's job. <i>(1949,High Country Days,96</i>)

>  <b>REED</b> After the cook had washed the dishes and his offsider had cut firewood ... and there would likely be a midday meal to prepare for a few men working nearer the shanty. <i>(1953,Story of the Kauri, The,261</i>)

>  <b>SHARPE</b> A contract shearing gang is complete, right down to the cook's offsider. <i>(1962,Country Occasions,36</i>)

>  <b>FERNANDEZ</b> The only people left working were the cook and her two off-siders, cleaning up after the meal. <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,16</i>)

>  <b>AGNEW</b> I pointed to the pile of dirty dishes. `Do you want a hand to clean up?' ... `That's right,' agreed his [the cook's] offsider, who threw me a towel. `Get stuck in, young fellow. There's plenty of them.' <i>(1974,Loner, The,64</i>)

>  <b>OWEN & PERKINS</b> The cook always had an offsider whose business it was to keep the fire going. <i>(1986,Speaking for Ourselves,153</i>)

>  WANTED - Offsider, for Nelson Bros.' cook-house; wages GBP2 per week. <i>(Nov 29, 1913,Poverty Bay Herald,1</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> an assistant, `side-kick', crony, a partner, friend

>  At 3.20 on Sunday morning, however, the music ceased abruptly, as the result of a row between the piano player and certain of his offsiders and some local doubting Thomases who remained in the room overnight to keep a viligant watch on Montecino. <i>(Dec 20, 1924,NZ Truth,2</i>)

>  They told my offsider who was doing the catering that they walked across the road every time there was a service for afternoon tea... <i>(Feb 4, 2006,Dominion Post,A3</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> He will get you on as an offsider. <i>(1922,Happy Wanderer,8</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> His offsider said `Give a man a fair go', so I gave him a bit of kidney pie and there was nothing lousy about him, but he said he had to be careful because the other fellow was the trump of the dump. <i>Ibid.</i> 288 It's the offsider I'm talking about because he winked at me when the trump of the dump asked him if he had barbered the spuds for tomorrow's breakfast <i>(1935,Slang Today,287</i>) [NOTES: A.E. Strong WW1 scene in Partridge Slang Today]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Offsider. An assistant: Australians' and New Zealanders': C. 20. Orig. <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,580</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> One classer, two rollers, two fleece-pickers, two pressers, one penner up, make up the shed contingent and the cook and `offsider' complete the total. <i>(1951,High Country,27</i>)

>  <b>RHODES</b> He said it wouldn't have mattered if it had been his half-witted cowman, but Johnny Holden and that off-sider of his are two of the best musterers in the Gorge. <i>(1952,Fly Away Peter,122</i>)

>  <b>SCRYMGEOUR</b> One day, Marco took out ... the steam engine, having previously told an offsider to fire up and get a good head of steam on the big 16 h.p. traction engine. <i>(1960,Memories of Maoriland,65</i>)

>  <b>SYDER & HODGETTS</b> An assistant. .in 4;`He's my offsider - there's too much for one man to do; he does all thefetching and carrying for me.' <i>(1974,Australian & NZ English [TS],788,1962-63 ed.</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> Marginally colloquial are the words from phrases no-hoper `useless person' and offsider `horse harnessed at the right of a main horse in a team', `helper', `companion' generally. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,46</i>)

>  off-sider n. personal assistant associate. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621</i>)

>  off-sider n. personal assistant, associate. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)

>  <b>MOORE</b> My `offsider' is Ralph, a young man who does not know much about bush work. <i>(1969,From Forest to Farm,88</i>)

>  <b>DAVIN</b> <i>(1970,Not Here, Not Now,273</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> But his offsider was doubtful. <i>Ibid.</i> 73 Tups and his offsider were fortunate enough to be much informed by the young orderly on duty at the morgue ... <i>(1976,Sunset Village,48</i>)

>  (9.25 p.m.) [policeman who had axe thrown through windscreen of patrol car says] I wondered whether my offsider had been [hit] [=mate: policemen who was my mate] off-sider n. Austral. & N.Z. a partner or assistant. <i>(Aug 26, 1984,TV1</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> offsider assistant, hanger-on, friend, partner, all or some of these; originally assistant to bullock driver, walking on the offside of the team; eg `I see Hart's offsider is now coaching the team.'NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,78</i>)

## Oh One
 
 <i>n.</i> a skinhead

When written in capitals OI OI (for example, as in the case of tattoos) it loooks like the numerals 'zero one' or 'oh one'. <i>(Oct 2000,NZWords,2(2): 2</i>)

## ohoho
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [Ma.] a nectar-bearing native tree or bush, valued by early apiarists [poss. ongaonga, Urticaceae.]

>  There are [sic] a number of native trees in blossom at the same time as pukapuka, including puriri, tea-tree, 'bush-lawyer,' clematis, cabbage-tree, mingi and ohoho. Of these, 'bush-lawyer', cabbage tree and ohoho bloom only at the same time as pukapuka and all three yield more nectar than pukapuka does. <i>(Jul 1911,NZ Farmer,765</i>)



## ohu
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a government-sponsored rural commune, usually on marginal land.

>  <b>GORDON & DEVERSON</b> .. the word ohu was prominent for a while in the 1970s when the government supported the setting up of rural communes ... <i>(1985,NZ English (Gordon & Deverson),36</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Some went into communes - remember the ohu movement of the Kirk Government, of which Amos was a minister? <i>(Sep 27, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),24</i>)

>  <b>HAYWARD</b> [fn. to a 1.12.73 entry on Mr Kirk's interest in supporting alternative lifestyles] * The Listener in 1979 published a feature on the kibbutz or ohu scheme which described it as originating from a`chance remark by Norman Kirk.' <i>(1981,Diary of the Kirk Years,183fn</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> formerly, a state-sponsored, rural commune. -->a kibbutz-type of landed-community introduced by the Labour Government in the 1970s; a communal work group [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  <b>KOHERE</b> The work was performed by what was calledan "ohu" or working-bee. Only in thiscase the whole community or sub-tribeformed the "ohu." <i>(1949,Story of a Maori Chief, The,28</i>)

>  The pioneering spirit. It stops not to ask if that wilderness actually belongs to someone else, or whether one's 60s girlfriend really wants to live in a teepee and wash nappies in the river on some ohu. <i>(Feb 12, 1994,Listener (New Zealand Listener),63</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Ohu ... n. company of volunteer workers. <i>(1871,Dictionary of the Maori Language,100,3rd ed.</i>)

>  A Colac Bay native narrated that the late Rawiri te Awha had been one of a party of Maoris living on the shores of Te Anau some eighty or ninety years ago. Under the custom of ohu the men were assembled assisting a man to amke a garden at some distance from the whare (houses). <i>(Apr 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/88</i>) [NOTES: Beattie]

>  The impression I gathered by personal inspection of some of the lands and from representations made at crowded meetings in seven settlements, where we met some 3000 of the northern tribes, was that they were doing more than any other section of the race - not excepting the East Coast - for themselves. Road-making on a 50/50 basis with the local bodies, ohu or mahi-apu [ed fn: A group of volunteer workers] in relation to scrub-cutting, bush felling, splitting posts and battens, gave ample evidence of self-help. <i>(May 22, 1930,Na To Hoa Aroha,2/34,1987 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Gnata]

>  <b>HAYWARD</b> Arthur Faulkner, still Acting Minister of Lands, has announced that the proposed communes will be called `ohu' - a Maori word meaning to achieve something `by means of friendly help and work.' <i>(Mar 7, 1974,Diary of the Kirk Years,223,1981 ed.</i>)

>  THREE OHUS There are three ohus on Crown land with a total area of 375 hectares, ... <i>(Nov 2, 1977,Evening Post,2/7</i>)

>  Ohu is a Maori term which refers to either a communal or volunteer work group. ...The main objective of the settlement was to offer an alternative way of life, and the Government decided tolease Crown land to groups. At the outset 38 groups were approved as eligible but only three have established themselves on Crown land. <i>(1981,Dominion,13</i>)

>  <b>KING</b> [caption] The ohu, or working bee, which cleared and sowed the Panguru block. By 1932, groups like this were bringing Maori land into production all over the North Island. <i>(1988,Apirana Ngata</i>)

>  [Ahu Ahu on the Wanganui River] was founded only 13 years ago, back in those heady Kirk years when the late Prime Minister gave the flower-power mob the land they yearned t return to.There they could build their communes. Ohu, he called them. This was one of eight ohu established and remains as one of only two that have seen the distance. <i>(Dec 30, 1988,Dominion,11</i>)

>  They went there in 1972, at the time newly elected Labour Prime Minister Norman Kirk was promoting his plan for State-sponsored communes or ohus, with the idea of finding a suitable property and dropping out for a while. <i>(May 31, 2001,Evening Post,5</i>)

>  Norm Kirk's reforming newly-elected Labour government's policies included support for communes, called ohu in the new-found enthusiasm for the native language. <i>(Jan 7, 2003,Dominion Post,C1</i>)

>  New Zealand's last remaining Ohu - a commune of alternative lifestylers established in the 70s, on state land given away to the flower power generation by the Norman Kirk Labour Government - survives further downstream. <i>(Oct 2, 2000,Evening Post,13</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR & HARREX</b> The Labour Government of 1972-5 assisted groups of people to form ohu - communes which were supposed to express Maori co-operative values. <i>(1978,Looking Back,67</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a working bee.

>  <b>ATKINSON</b> In evg. Went up to the foot of Totaraiahua to an ohu of Tutanekai who are digging potatoes there. <i>(Mar 18, 1862,BLANK ENTRY,755</i>) [NOTES: in Scholefield (1960) Richmond-Atkinson Papers I]



## oi
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [muttonbird (1947)](../M#muttonbird-(1947)))

>  The Maori name oi comes from the high-pitched call the birds make during flight. <i>(Mar 2005,NZ Geographic,72/</i>)

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 127. PUFFINUS TRISTIS.Forst. Mutton Bird. Oii. <i>Nectris amaurosoma</i>. COUES <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,45</i>)

>  <i>Observations on Gould's Petrel (Hutton)</i>, Procellaria gouldi (Ohi), <i>their Habits and Habitats.</i> ... These Petrels are common on the coast of New Zealand. ... [91] After sunset, thick clouds of these Petrels swarm round the cliff, uttering the melancholy sound<i> `ohi! ohi!'</i> from which the Natives named it <i> `Ohi.' </i> <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/90</i>) [NOTES: (Reischek)]

>  <b>BULLER</b> PROCELLARIA {MAJAQUEUS} GOULDI. (GREY-FACED PETREL.) ... <i>Native name.- Oii. </i> <i>(1888,Birds of New Zealand,2/245</i>)

>  The tuatara lives in the same burrows as are used by the oii, the titi and the toanui. All three of the petrels are included in the term mutton-birds. <i>(Sep 11, 1922,Quick March,15</i>)

>  32. <i>Pterodroma macroptera gouldi </i>(Hutton, 1869) <i>Grey-faced Petrel</i> (Oi) <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,21,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Grey-faced Petrel Pterodroma macroptera gouldi</i> (HUTTON, 1869) ... OTHER NAMES: <i>Oi, muttonbird</i>. ... Around the northern part of the North Island, the grey-faced petrel is the most common breeding petrel. [A North Island bird] <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,72</i>)

>  The grey-faced petrel, or sombre shearwater - ornithological name Puffinus tristus, has the Maori names oi, oioi and oii; Cook's petrel (Procellaria Cooki), is titi; brown petrel (Procellaria parkinsoni) is taiko; Gould's petrel (Procellaria Gouldi) is ohi; rain bird (Puffinus gavius) is hokoakoa; (Puffinus assimilis) is totorore. The most common name in the south is titi, or oii; the others are seldom used. <i>(Apr 20, 1903,Southland Times,2</i>)



## oia
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Caesioperca lepidoptera</i>, red perch, (See [butterfly perch](../B#butterfly-perch))

## OIA
 
 <i>abbr.</i> Official Information Act

>  If they (WCC) have information, they should assess it under the terms of the OIA. Contracting out is not a ground (for witholding information) under the OIA. <i>(Feb 18, 2003,Independent Herald (Wellington),1</i>)

>  It does not matter that its own legislation advises otherwise; that the Official Information Act aims at openness in the interests of enhancing respect for the law and promoting good government. When the OIA says information should be made available unless there is good reason for withholding it, why, public servants can think of a hundred, a thousand good reasons... Public agencies and ministerial offices routinely put OIA requests on ooze control. <i>(Feb 24, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),5</i>)

## OIC

>  In the past five years the OIC, in decisions made public, has approved 1380 applications and refused just 29. "The Overseas Investment Commission's work could be done by a monkey using a rubber stamp," Mr Horton says. <i>(Aug 10, 2002,Dominion Post,A10</i>)

>  The blame does not lie with the vendor or the purchaser but with the system and successive governments and OIC. <i>(Aug 11, 2002,Sunday Star Times,A6</i>)

>  To buy land here without OIC permission all you need is a resident's permit. <i>(Oct 9, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),18</i>)

## oil
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) useful information, the truth; somebody or something excellent in its class; the real thing. [1961 Stage I University Student's Essay : She's the oil]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> The oil. 4. Hence in New Zealand c. (- 1932), it = information. <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,581</i>)

>  [In local criminal slang]. . . `oil' is significant information. <i>(Apr 2, 1938,Press, The,18</i>) [NOTES: McNab NZ Slang]

>  <b>FULLARTON</b> `What's the oil, Noel?' `Yes, spill it' <i>Ibid.</i> 112 What's the oil, Sir? <i>(1944,Troop Target,18</i>)

>  <b>COOZE</b> I'll give you the oil according to Hoyl. <i>(1946,Ten Bob Each Way,22</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> The sense`information' is australian (dinkum oil) and New Zealand s. <i>(1949,Dictionary of the Underworld,479,1961 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Oil. 4. By 1946, more Australian than New Zealand: Sidney J. Baker, letter. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang Addenda,1123</i>)

>  `Wait on, mate give us the oil,' yelled Giant after him. <i>(1946,Watersiders,22</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> oil, the the truth, the information you need, an excellent person or thing; eg `Phil had the oil all right on Brierleys. Sold all he had the day before the bottom fell out of the market.' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,78</i>)

>  oil ... 10. Austral. & N.Z. sl. facts or news. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,783</i>) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 521]]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> the real truth

>  Before "Pro Bono Publico" is moved to an exhortation on practical patriotism I'll give you the dinkum oil. <i>(Jun 20, 1925,NZ Truth,6</i>)

>  <b>McCALLUM</b> The `right oil' at the races cost me plenty ... `What about you driving me to the races on Saturday because I can get the good oil from a mate of mine?' I tell him that I don't need the oil since the old V8 has had the rebore. He say `Crikey, I don't mean that sort of oil. What I mean is the dinkum oil, the straight griffen and the low-down about the horses.' <i>(1963,Half-Gallon Jar,21</i>)

>  The Dinkum Oil [Name of a student Musical review <i>(1939,Cappicade,27</i>)

>  <b>FULLARTON</b> Anyway there's no dinkum oil. Only latrinograms ... it may be all hooey. <i>Ibid.</i> 24 He was a mine of information - both `latrinograms' and the `dinkum oil.' <i>(1944,Troop Target,18</i>)

>  <b>SOLJAK</b> Following are examples of colloquialisms common to New Zealand and Australian English, with their English or American equivalent ... dinkum: genuine; the dinkum oil: the real McCoy <i>(1946,New Zealand (various authors/titles),115</i>)

>  `Unless I'm doing new things all the time I feel I'm going loopy. ... Dinkum. Dinkum oil.' <i>(May 1952,Here & Now,34</i>)

>  <b>ROGERS</b> ... slanting his glance down at the Scotch and soda on the tray. `The dinkum oil, eh?' <i>(1960,Long White Cloud, The</i>)

>  <b>McCALLUM</b> [see OIL n for full quot] What I mean is the dinkum oil, the straight griffen and the low-down about the horses. <i>(1963,Half-Gallon Jar,21</i>)

>  dinkum oil, the real truth [Lincolnshire dialect]. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),7</i>)

>  dinkum adj. Austral. & N.Z. inf. 1. genuine or right: a fair dinkum offer. 2. dinkum oil. the truth. [C19: < E dialect: work, < ?] <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,314</i>) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 210; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 135]]

>  <b>OVENDEN</b> Journalists! Think you know the lot! Always drilling for the dinkum oil. <i>(1984,Ratatui,189</i>)

>  <b>GOODING</b> You told her you could give background information only; the dinkum oil would have to come from the challenge bosses themselves. <i>(1987,KZ7,26</i>)

>  <b>NORGRAVE</b> `Is this straight up, Blue?' Charlie was wearing his sceptical look. `Dinkum oil, Charlie.' <i>(1987,Shoestring Sailors,42</i>)

>  I find I have merely exchanged the shrewd guys for the Wise Men of the East who.. give you the dinkum oil in Star of the Mouski or Pearl of Sweet Water Canal. <i>(Jan 26, 1942,NZEF Times,I/31 : 6</i>)

>  This is the second news - 1.30 - red hot - dinkum oil. <i>(Sep 20, 1943,NZEF Times,III/117 : 10</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  good oil, the (slang) reliable information. See also dinkum oil. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),616</i>)

>  good oil, the n. (Sl.) reliable information. See also dinkum oil. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),9</i>)

>  <b>PFAHLERT & WATSON</b> He was an ex-horse trainer and had all the right connections for the `good oil' on hot favourites for Saturdays. <i>(1987,Some Must Die,28</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

## oinker
 
 <i>n.</i> a policeman

>  <b>MARRINER</b> "Shit, the oinkers must've broken it up already.' <i>Ibid.</i> 160 In the darkness, what I can make of the oinker's face beneath his peaked hat only goads my alarm. Some oinkers have hearts - seriously - are open to interpreting the law a little eccentrically when it's in the common good. <i>Ibid.</i> 162 I can read this guy's history like a timeline: straight C student;... lost cheery at age nineteen (to a twenty stone boiler);.. first beer and 'parties' at oinker school...... when dealing with obstinate oinkers, the dude tends to get conciliated-out pretty quickly. <i>Ibid.</i> 171 Every set of lights in the rear-view looked like oinkers. <i>(2001,Stonedogs,41</i>)



## oinkering
 
 <i>n.</i> policing, detective work

>  <b>MARRINER</b> Fuck <i>Me</i>, that's first-rate oinkering. <i>(2001,Stonedogs,331</i>)



## oioi
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) a jointed wire rush plant, Apodasmia similis. [ORIGIN: Maori, Formerly classified Leptocarpus similus]

>  <i>Leptocarpus simplex</i>, Br. ... [266] Nat. name, `Oioi' (shaking), <i>Col. </i> <i>(1853,Flora NZ,I Flowering Plants 265</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Leptocarpus similis</i> JOINTED WIRE RUSH ... Oioi ... The Maori name oioi also means to shake or agitate; watch the beds of jointed wire rush when waves wash into them. <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,324</i>)

>  <b>CROWE</b> The jointed zigzag stems of oioi were gathered by early Maori for use as an outer thatching for homes... Oioi means 'to shake gently', which derives from the swaying of the stems as the wind blows through them. <i>(1995,Which Coastal Plant?,51</i>)

>  Oioi is an attractive and unusual plant with cylindrical stems and leaves that form a short sheath at intervals along the stem creating noticeable joints - hence the common name, jointed wire rush. <i>(Aug 5, 2006,Dominion Post Indulgence,19</i>)



## OK
 
 <i>adj.</i> as an adjective attested early in NZ (1865, Dunedin) and has remained a common feature of NZ speech; also in various forms espec. among children (OKE, OKIE, OKIE-DOKIE, OKIE-DOKE, DOKE). `She'll be doke' would be more likely to be heard than `She'll be OK.', as a by-form of `She'll be right'. spg also okay

>  The measures you have taken for lining the public purse are quite O.K. <i>(Nov 11, 1865,Dunedin Punch,168</i>)

>  I would include among New Zealand English words: O.K. <i>(May 6, 1952,VUC First Year English Class</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> just the thing

>  [Two-up's] a real open air game - just the O.K. for a race-course. OEDS 1839 [derivation from orl korrect; thence Old Kinderhook 1840] <i>(Mar 1912,Magpie, The</i>)
