## Okareka Ash
 
 <i>n.</i> Geol. definition

>  The base of the section comprises ridges of pumice sand gravel mantled by Okareka Ash, Rerewhakaaitu Ash and Rotorua Ash now truncated by lake waters when the lake level was at an elevation of 284 m as against the present day elevation of 279.7 m. <i>(May 1975,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,39/35</i>)



## Okarito brown kiwi
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Young Okarito brown kiwi remain with their parents for at least nine months. <i>(Jan 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 45/91</i>)

>  [Operation Nest Egg] was conceived with the Okarito brown kiwi in mind, but because its population was so low - estimated to be around 150 in the early 1990s - it was decided to trial ONE on the more common North Island brown. <i>(Jul 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 64/51</i>)

>  Okarito brown kiwi are New Zealand's rarest kiwi and are on the nationally critical list. <i>(Jan 10, 2007,NZ Herald,A7</i>)

>  In 2002, the government decided to keep most of the remaining unsold land at Okarito in public hands, winning an extra habitat for one of New Zealand's rarest birds, the Okarito brown kiwi, and a sanctuary for a delicate ecology. <i>(Feb 21, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26</i>)



## Okarito forest gecko
 
 <i>n.</i> brownish gecko with orange blotches, seldom encountered, restricted to small area in south Westland. [<i>Hoplodactylus "Okarito forest gecko"</i> aka Okarito gecko]

>  The confirmed lizard fauna of the Franz Josef/Waiau area comprises just fourspecies.the forest gecko, the Okarito forest gecko (presently confirmed from asingle specimen), the speckled skink, and the common skink. <i>(2004,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.doc.org.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/WClizards.pdf]

>  Okarito Forest Gecko . . . A new species of <i>Hoplodactylus granulatus</i> first seen in the 1990s and recently confirmed as a distinct species using DNA sequencing but still to be described. <i>(2009,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.doc.govt.nz/conservation/native-animals/reptiles-and-frogs/species-information/atlas-of-the-amphibians-and-reptiles-of-nz/electronic-atlas/atlas-details/?SpeciesID=13252]



## Okataina Tephra
 
 <i>n.</i> Geol. definition

>  On field appearance and stratigraphy, the thin tephra was considered not to be an Okataina tephra [sic, small 't'] (Kaiharoa or Whakatane Tephra) but perhaps a tephra from Taupo or the basaltic Rotokawau Ash. <i>(Jan 1984,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,66/18</i>)



## okay, lah
 
 <i>exclam.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> okay, lah intensified agreement, army slang using Malay `lah', a suffix supplying emphasis; eg `You boys stick with your friendly neighbourhood seargent, this'll be a piece of piss. Okay? `Yeh, Sarge. Okay, lah!' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,78</i>)



## okeoke
 
 <i>n.</i> dogfish

>  <b>LEE & KENDA;;</b> O'ke |oke, s. A certain fish. <i>(1820,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,141</i>)

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Squalus fernandinus</i> Molina. SPINED DOGFISH; OKEOKE or KOINGA. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,9,1971 ed.</i>)

>  SPINY DOGFISH. <i>Squalus fernandinus</i> , Molina. The spiny dogfish (<i>Squalus fernandinus</i> ) is one of those species of shark which has attained importance as an economic species. ... It is generally called oke-oke, or kapeta, by [Maori]; the term pioke is applied by some tribes to this species, while to other tribes pioke is any small species of shark. <i>(1929,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,11/223</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps]

>  <i>Squalus fernandinus</i> Molina. Spined dogfish. Okeoke. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/401</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> SPINED DOGFISH (KOINGA OR OKEOKE) <i>Koinga lebruni</i> (Vailliant) ...Spined Dogfish is so named from the single spine in front of each dorsal fin, and Dogfish probably because of its supposed carnivorous habits. There are several species of small sharks that are known as Dogfish. [<i>ibid.</i> 82]. The Maori name for Spined Dogfish is Koinga or Okeoke, and the fish is used by them as food, usually being hung outside and dried, when it will keep for long periods. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,80,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> NORTHERN DOGFISH ... <i>Other names: Squalus blainvillii</i>; Griffin's dogfish, common spined dogfish, brown dogfish; pioke, okeoke (Maori). <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,186</i>)

It's popular country use probably springs from the traditional Englishman's `How are you old boy?' ... No New Zealand farmer would ever speak in this way. But he retains that friendly, goodwill connotation of the word. <i>(1985,Gumboots and Goalposts,142</i>)

## Old Bucky
 
 <i>n.</i> [the Christchurch earthquakes personified]

>  The end of the tether is there in the grey faces of the men who continue against all odds to service this broken city, painstakingly blasting the water-pipes free of silt, digging the hideous liquefaction out of roads and properties, endlessly plugging and repairing the roads, and scaling cliffs and tall buidings - trying to put Christchurch back together with what seems like vinegar and brown paper, as Old Bucky sniggers with contempt and swings back for another go. <i>(Jun 20, 2011,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  <b>Jane Bowron</b> It's the best thing to come out of Old Bucky, but the guitarist, who's a chippie, is disturbed by what's happening with the rebuild or non-re-build. <i>(Apr 25, 2011,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

## old chum
 
 <i>n.</i> [AND old 2. obs 1832-1915a person with colonial experience; =OLD HAND (never as common as OLD HAND; possibly a new formation on NEW CHUM: cf NEW HAND) -->1863 Butler First Year ch iv 55 I was anxious to become an old chum, as colonial dialect calls asettler - thereby proving my new chumship most satisfactorily. [Morris 1846 `not in common use'; AC 1846]

>  <b>HOYLE</b> My fellow passengers [were] both `old chums'. Zealand Scenery') Then there's what we call a gully, which of course we take to mean/ Just a small and narrow valley, in which bush is sometimes seen;/ Bushy gully suits me better, serves my purpose just as well;/ ... Bush, too, means the native forest; you will never, I'm afraid,/ Hear a self-respecting bushman call a bush a leafy glade,/ ... <i>(1868,Fragments of a Journal,23</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Old Chum ... Not in common use; the opposite to a new chum.[1846] <i>(1898,Austral English,327</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE</b> In an incredibly short time, ... the `new chum' was quite able to sit a stock-horse, no easy task even for an `old chum', ... <i>(1930,NZ Memories,159</i>)

>  Old Chum. - Man who has a long experience of the Colony. Not used except as opposite of new chum. `Never was a freshman at Cambridge more anxious to be mistaken for a third year man, than I was anxious to become an old chum, as the Colonial dialect calls a settler--thereby proving my new chumship most satisfactorily.'--Butler A First Year in Canterbury Settlement], p. 54. ([[1898 Morris Austral-English] <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  old chum n. used in 1870s for a person who had been in N.Z. for some time. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  THE reports of riches outshining our best finds in the Driving Creek, drew me at last to this neighbourhood, to ascertain personally how much of "new chum" and "old chum" gold could be seen here. <i>(Dec 16, 1862,Daily Southern Cross,3</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an early settler.

>  "As you see, I am still here, although it is hard to have to stay here when every New Zealand paper we read tells of an old chum wounded or gone ..." <i>(Oct 30, 1915,Poverty Bay Herald,6</i>)

>  Evan Graham, an old chum, falls desperately in love, and is loved in return. <i>(Dec 30, 1921,Ashburton Guardian,2</i>)

>  We copy the following sketch .. depicting the labour and trials of a zealous but rather impractical clergyman, who, on arriving in his new parish, in a rural district of England, has to wage war, not only with all the whims and prejudices of his "old chum" parishioners, but with the coarse unbelievers of the village. <i>(Aug 15, 1857,Daily Southern Cross,7</i>)

>  I was anxious to become an old chum, as the colonial dialect calls a settler - thereby proving my newchumship most satisfactorily. <i>(1860,BLANK ENTRY,52</i>) [NOTES: in BUTLER First Year (1863)]

>  The servant, therefore, retains his place, not because he is wanted, but because there is a difficulty felt in providing a successor; originating in a doubt which obtrudes itself constantly on the mind, whether the "new hand" will turn out any better than the "old chum". <i>(Nov 2, 1859,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,4</i>)

>  <b>BARKER</b> .. I asked an 'old chum' what I was to do with my chimney. <i>(1873,Station Amusements in NZ,199</i>)



## Old Country
 
 <i>n.</i> Britain

>  Drink, crime, poverty are not less evident here than they are in the Old Country. <i>(Nov 16, 1905,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  A decision to send a delegation to the Old Country was arrived at. ...What is the delegation going Home for? What is it to say to the Home producer of like food-stuffs? Some ass has said that it is a delgation of good-will to lay the position of the poor Antipodean dairy-man before his relative and talk to him of the `Empire' and `loyalty'! <i>(1988,Na To Hoa Aroha,3/145</i>) [NOTES: 1934 Mar 25 in 1988 Na To Hoa Aroha (Ngata-Buck Correspondence) 3/145 (Ngata)]

>  <b>GUTHRIE</b> If there is one desire common to those adventurers among the people of the bight ... it is to see what they still call the Old Country... <i>(1935,Little Country, The,1,1937 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ADAMS</b> But straight before us lay our land of promise ... the land where the losses and errors of the old country were to be retrieved. <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,16</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [AND 1834] constr THE: Britain; one's MOTHER COUNTRY (cf. Home, (the) Old Dart, Old Land -->(Never as frequent as HOME: tends to be have been used by immigrants) [(obsolescent)]

>  Everyone in New Zealand speaks of England as "Home", even those who were born in the colony and have never visited the old country. <i>(Sep 10, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,7</i>)

>  The mortality amongst the Native team that journeyed to the Old Country has been remarkable. <i>(Oct 8, 1904,NZ Observer,5</i>)

>  Some relative in the Old Country died and left him a lot of money. <i>(Nov 16, 1938,Auckland Weekly News,96</i>)

>  <b>BALLANTYNE</b> Some saying it was awful how a man with connections in the Old Country could go to the pack like this. <i>(1948,Cunninghams, The,75</i>) [NOTES: Page 67 in the 1963(1976) edition; chapter 14 : Night of Dec. 24]

>  <b>POLACK</b> If [the emigrant] expects to become emancipated from the thraldom of the aristocracy, he is soon relieved from his mistake by discovering that the invidious order is usurped by a clique, formerly, in the `old country', (as it is feelingly termed) on a par with himself. <i>(1840,Manners & Customs of the NZers,2/216</i>)

>  On the road was invited into the house by a Scotch lassie to take a drink of new milk, and a slice of cake, a gossip about the `auld countrie' included. <i>(Dec 17, 1848,Letters from Otago,23,1978 ed.</i>)

>  [prospectus of the Otago Witness] The objects of the proprietors are ... to supply the settlement with the means of advertising, and with the news from home and other quarters; and, secondly, to circulate such accurate statistics, and solid information in regard to the state and progress of the settlement, as shall be useful to the Settlers themselves, and of abounding interest to their friends and well wishers in the Old Country. <i>(Mar 15, 1851,Lyttelton Times,5</i>)

>  <b>FULLER</b> <i>(1859,Five Years' Residence in New Zealand</i>) [NOTES: [see chron notes]]

>  <b>KENNAWAY</b> The crops grown were just those raised in the old country. <i>Ibid.</i> 232 And though he [the colonist] may not stand upon the very ground of his nativecountry, yet at least he will live among his fellow-countrymen; and even if at times he feels inclined to regret for the pleasant places at home, he may recollect that life, even in the old country, is not altogether clear of difficulties ... <i>(1874,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,16</i>)

>  <b>HOPEFUL</b> Yes, the young [77] Colonist would see a great deal to make him open his eyes on these august occasions, so often to be seen in the old country. <i>(1887,Taken In,76</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> `Why, mother, poor old mother,' said Harry, ... `who would have expected to find you here [in New Zealand]? I thought you were still in the Old Country.' [88] ... Annie, who was married just before we left the old country, and Tom, who is working on his own hook now, but not far from here. <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,87</i>)

>  pass. ... the Old Country ... the Motherland [frequently used] <i>(Aug 12, 1905,NZ Truth,8/2/8</i>)

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> Visitors from our oversea dominions, when they come Home, invariably swear that they have in their own little State something better than anything in the same line to be seen in the Old Country. <i>(1912,Impressions of a NZ Pastoralist on Tour,123</i>)

>  <b>BARRY</b> In April 1921, the people of Brydone gave a social welcome to Mr. and Mrs. A.W. Armstrong and family who had recently arrived from the `old country'. <i>(1966,In the Lee of the Hokonuis,243</i>) [NOTES: 1921 April in 1966 Barry In the Lee of the Hokonuis]

>  Old Country. - England, not usually including Scotland; but sometimes Irishmen use the word to signify Ireland. `The crops grown were just those raised in the old country.'--[1874 Kennaway Crusts], p. 16. <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>SCANLAN</b> We've got to stand by the Old Country. I'd be off to-morrow if I were a young man. <i>(1933,Tides of Youth,240</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> ... the other burnt to death by her dress catching fire at a wedding in the Old Country. <i>Ibid.</i> 139 It was very interesting for me who had been all my life in New Zealand to meet these young fellows who had come out from the Old Country to make their way in life. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,53</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> Old country and old land are close enough to standard English not to require comment. -->Siegfried Democracy in NZ -->Reid Book NZ 301 <i>(1941,NZ Slang,44</i>)

>  <b>MUIR</b> `Some of you jokers could do with a stir-up,' Fred said in his slow, part-Midlands, part-Kiwi accent. He knew the men still regarded him as a Homey, though it was well over thirty years since he'd left the Old Country. <i>(1960,Word for Word,181</i>)

 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MAY</b> False bottoms fooled even seasoned diggers, particularly at Kaniere. Claims considered worked out were frequently re-worked at a different level. This explains the many small rushes which occurred on `old ground'. <i> Ibid.</i> 527 OLD GROUND: ground worked over or mined. <i>(1967,West Coast Gold Rushes,195,1962 ed.</i>)

## old hat
 
 <i>n.</i> a form of shore erosion [`Old Hat' type of shore erosion was first described by Prof J.A. Bartrum A.U.C. in a paper read before Auckland Institute and Museum 20/10/1915]

>  the name comes from the island in the Bay of Islands locally known as `the Old Hat' from its shape. This island exemplifies the type of erosion. <i>(1916,TrNZI,48/132</i>)



## old identity
 
 <i>n.</i> [(Otago and non-Otago)]

>  House flies first appeared in Dunedin .... entirely supplanting .... the genuine old-identity blow-fly. <i>Ibid.</i>321 I know that this statement will be questioned by many who have never believed that there were genuine old-identity wild dogs in New Zealand before Europeans brought them here, .... <i>(1877,TrNZI,10/310</i>) [NOTES: (Gillies)]

>  <b>MANTELL</b> `One of the locals thought so, too. ... Probably wouldn't have moved if it hadn't been for the old identity competition.' <i>(1978,Murder in Fancydress,67</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a Scotch settler in Otago - non-collective

>  <b>THATCHER</b> Mister Monson in the Customs / Is a most efficient chap; / His dad's an Old Identity - / I once gave him a rap; <i>(1862,Dunedin Songster,No.3/11</i>) [NOTES: The Great Customs Seizure]

>  <b>THATCHER</b> Many an Old Identity/ Had been imbibing rather free;/ <i>Ibid. </i> 8 Full many [an Immigrant] resembled/ An Old Identity./ Their speech was very similar -/ For one his boxes missed,/ And he told a fellow-passenger,/ `Eh, mon, I dinna see ma kist.' <i>(1865,Otago Songster,7</i>)

>  ACT II. SCENE I. - A SLY GROG-SHANTY. ... [Enter New Chum and Old Identity] <i>(Jul 8, 1876,Saturday Advertiser,5</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Otago, obs.) BARRACOUTA as a Dunedin boarding-house dish (cf BARRACOUTIAN)

>  <b>MPFFATT</b> There seemed to be a plethora of bread and barracouta [at Dunedin], the principle [sic] cry being `Here you are, a loaf and an old identity (barracouta) for a shilling.' <i>(1896,Adventures by Sea and Land,64,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> The barracouta was the fish ... as it was cheap was often served by the [Dunedin] boarding house-keepers [c1860s], and I remember a fish-hawker while selling it calling out: `Here you are: two yards of old identity for a bob!' <i>(1925,Random Recollections,17</i>)

>  <b>LYON</b> In early Dunedin large shoals of barracouta were easily caught and sold at sixpence or less for a fish ... No wonder the old Dunedinite of a former generation referred to them, those wide-jawed and projecting toothed long fish, as `Old Identities'. <i>(1952,Faring South,131</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> in general (non-Otago) use, occasionally with some development of meaning or application. The first element of the collocation, `old', is not always felt as inseparable from the second, allowing a descriptive term (usually a local or place-name) to be inserted. -->a. a person long identified with a place, position, institution, etc; an oldinhabitant

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> To `blow' is, in the colonies, to brag or boast; this is graphic if not elegant; but one of the most curious expressions, based on an entirely errroneous derivation, is `an old identity', used for a person long `identified with a place' - in fact an old inhabitant, or one whose interests have long been bound up in it. This expression is unfortunately now universal even in newspapers. <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,171</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> A mixed gathering, containing some members of the class who in this country are known as `OldIdentities', but whom I heard an aged man designate, with a lapsus linguae, that had in it much unconscious irony: `Old Nonentities'. <i>(1894,In the Land of the Tui,256</i>) [NOTES: 1892 Jan 16 [Wakatipu] in 1894 Wilson Land of the Tui]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Old Identity phrase denoting a person well known in a place. A term invented in Dunedin, New Zealand, in 1862, in a popular topical song, by Mr. R. Thatcher, an improvisator. In the song the `Old Identity,' the former resident of Dunedin, was distinguished from the `New Iniquity,' as the people were termed who came from Australia. [1879. W. J. Barry, `Up and Down,' p. 197:] <i>(1898,Austral English,209</i>)

 <i>n.</i> sometimes used with a sense of `colourful (local)character', `dag' [e.g. ca1940's He was a bit of an idenity round town.]

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> Like all small places, Queenstown had its quota of old identities, interesting personalities, some with very odd and often amusing characteristics. <i>(1964,Doctor in the Mountains,75,1974 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HUNT</b> Reggie was seventy-seven, ... a well known character in the Bay. The locals would ask, `Have you seen Reggie yet? He's a real old identity.' <i>(1981,Speaking a Silence,58</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a long-time resident of a particular place. [(hist) with THE the original Presbyterian puritan inhabitants of Dunedin (as seen originally by ? Thatcher ? Cargill or by their critics) -->(It is uncertain whether Cargill or Thatcher originated the term. Possibly Cargill used the expression first, and Thatcher, with his acute sense of the locally ridiculous, popularised it. That it was apt is certain from its originally enthusiastic, and later wide (and current) use) (collective sg)] (See [also identity](../A#also-identity))

>  I was at the Old Identities' picnicyesterday and spent a fair time among the"young iniquities" and "old immensities"as Texas Jack's irresponsible brothercalls the Old Identities. <i>(Feb 25, 1903,Otago Witness,68</i>)

>  <b>STEWART</b> He had been a Borough Councillor, President of the Old Identities' Association, and had coached hundreds of youngsters in sport. <i>(1973,Days of Fortune,73</i>)

>  <b>BOWMAN</b> An Old Identities Association, to include the settlers of their descendants, who arrived in the province before 1870, was formed in 1913. <i>(1948,Port Chalmers,201</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> "Old Identities". The phrase.. is on familiar in the ears of all Otagans as household words... Otago has every reason to be proud of her "old identities". <i>(1874,Colonial Experiences,25</i>)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> Mr Cargill in the Council, / Made such a funny speech; - / He got up and he stated / That it devolved on each / Of all the early dwellers / To preserve safe as could be, / Amid the Victorian influx, / The Old Identity. / Still cherish barbarism - stick to your Dunedin mud, / Andwith your eight-years' leases chouse [? clense] the Victorian blood /. Still dress your ancient Postman in the style we daily see-/ His costume is essential to the Old Identity. / [The refrain the Old Identity is repeated at the end of every stanza]. <i>(1862,Dunedin Songster,No. 1/1</i>)

>  The curse of Dunedin is the `leasing system' and the exclusive spirit of the `old identity' <i>(1862,Otago: Its Goldfields and Resources,9</i>)

>  <b>WEKEY</b> fn `Old Identity' is an entirely Otagonian idiom. It is meant to designate the early settlers of the province and their adherents. <i>(1862,Otago As It Is,76</i>)

>  <b>ALEXANDER</b> The gold discoveries had caused a large influx of active and enterprising men [to Dunedin] to overpower `the old identity' or original free kirk settlers. <i>(1863,Incidents of the Maori War,399</i>)

>  <b>HEYWOOD</b> The Scotch settlers are now called `The Old Identity', and seem almost sent to the wall by the new arrivals. <i>(1863,Vacation Tour at the Antipodes, A,167</i>)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> (The Sessions) The jurymen, as far as I could see,/ Were mostly of the Old Identity:/ <i>Ibid.</i> 11 (The Maoris and the Old Identity) Thinks I unto myself to-day/ What an analogy,/ There is between the Maoris and/ The Old Identity!/ For they view us poor Victorians/ With the same dislike and fear,/ And they tell us plump and plain - Eh mon!/ We dinna want you here./ <i>(1864,Invercargill Minstrel,3</i>)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> But all the Old identity/ Will clap their hands with wond'rous glee/.../ Yes, thrang as bumbees there they'll be,/ At a Caledonian Gathering./ <i>(1865,Otago Songster,6</i>)

>  ... eyes of some of the old identity. <i>(1865,Evening Post,1/47/2</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> [Old Identity] was the name applied by the people of Victoria, who flocked hither in thousands on the discovery of gold, to those who had been in the province before that time. Those honest folks, the tenour of whose ways had previously been very even, were greatly started by this immense and sudden influx of population. 16,000 persons landed in three months, and everythig went up to worse than famine prices. It is alleged that one minister actually prayed that the stream of rogues and vagabonds which was flowing into the country might be stayed, but this I believe to have been a base invention of the `new iniquity', as the new-comer were nicknamed. The term `old identities' took its origin from an expression in a speech made by one of the members of the Provincial Council, Mr. E.B. Cargill, who, in speaking of the new arrivals, said that the early settlers should endeavour to preserve their old identity. The strangers, who were inclined to laugh at the aboriginals as a set of old stagers, caught up the phrase, and dubbed them `old identities'. Acomic singer helped to perpetuate the name by writing a song the refrain of which was something in this style - `Touch not that shaky jetty, / Let that old post-office be, / That's one way to preserve / Your old identity'. So that anything effete came to be styled a regular `old identity'. So much did the name come to be a term of reproach among the Australian diggers that the barracouta 'thyrsites atum), a rather coarse fish, very abundant at certain seasons, and which was consequently very cheap, and a great favourite with boarding-house keepers, was christened by them `the old identity' <i>(1874,Colonial Experiences,25</i>)

>  <b>REEVES</b> The canny Scotch `old identity' was swallowed up in the `new iniquity', as they mutually styled each other. <i>(1892,Homeward Bound,3</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The origin of the descriptive term `Old Identities' has been involved in some doubt. Not only is it a convenient one, but it is now widely used throughout the Colonies to [202] denote the oldest inhabitants, as distinguished from the newcomers, or `new iniquities.' Mr. Charles R. Thatcher, who was the son of a natural history collector at Brighton, and was about his father's business, came to Dunedin with the great rush in February, 1862, and gave vocal entertainments which were highly relished. He was a clever improviser, and nightly sang in rough verse to delighted audiences the events of the day, which then, though trivial, were numerous enough. Mr. E. B. Cargill was seeking a seat in the Council, and addressing the electors, regretted that the old settlers were being thrown into the shade, and that to prevent this they must make a strong and united effort. This was food for Thatcher, who thereupon brought down the house with a witty song of eight verses, each of which terminated with the refrain of `The Old Identity.' Thus:- `Does he wish each brother Scotchman/ To come out in a kilt - / To kittle up the chanter,/ And go in for a lilt?/ With bare legs in Otago/ How very cold 'twould be!/ But it's one way of preserving/ The Old Identity.' Yes, it did seem as though the `new iniquity' were, like a flowing tide, to obliterate the `old identity.' But no such catastrophe happened. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,201</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> Dunedin became the largest town in the country, while, to the horror of the `Old Identity', saloons, billiard rooms, gambling dens and dance halls sprang up to attract the gold of the `New Iniquity'. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,104</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Examples of slang and colloquial expressions of local origin are old identity, on which hangs a tale,. . . <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/680</i>) [NOTES: (Wall NZ Speech)]

>  <b>BARR</b> There are in Princes-street certain Old Identity places of business <i>(1879,Old Identities, The,317</i>)

>  Our gallant little steamer had bought down.. the "old identities" of Otago, in the person of Governor Gore Brown and his good Lady. <i>(Sep 25, 1890,Otago Witness,36</i>)

>  It is, therefore, with no particular astonishment that we notice that this phrase - "the old identity" - is being already adopted by the new comers to express a sort of contemptuous feeling towards those who were in the country before them. <i>(Apr 12, 1862,Otago Witness,5</i>)

>  There was a large attendance atPortobello on Saturday when the annualpicnic of the Port Chalmers OldIdentities' Association was held. <i>(Feb 24, 1919,Otago Witness,5</i>)

>  May we never have occasion to set foot again in this glorious land of brause and whisky, the vaunted boast of Old Identity. <i>(Jun 13, 1863,Otago Witness,5</i>)

>  Get, if you can, speech with an "old identity." <i>(Nov 11, 1924,Otago Witness,68</i>)

>  We publish a report of proceedings at a crowded meeting held in the theatre at Dunedin, where old Otago settlers ("the old identity" as they are called) and new arrivals from Victoria appear together to demand separation from the Northern Island. <i>(Jun 6, 1862,Daily Southern Cross,4</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> an early inhabitant of a settlement other than Otago.

>  In our celebrations of the arrival of theOld Identities, we overlook the all-important fact that had it not been forthe advent of the digger, that "newiniquity".. our Dominion would not sospeedily have attained the forwardposition she now occupies. <i>(Dec 30, 1909,Otago Daily Times,8</i>)

## old lag(ger)

## old man
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  `Murder on the old sergeant-major, and the bottom line goes for the old man.' <i>(1917,New Zealand At the Front,106</i>)

>  `Come on me lucky lads, you pick 'em. Hop in, digs, and have a fly with the old man. Come on, thick and heavy, for I'm here today and gone tomorrow.' <i>(Aug 10, 1921,Quick March,27</i>)

>  Diggers' Rules of Crown and Anchor. - When the limit is laid on the spade, club, heart, diamond, and anchor, and the banker throws three crowns, he shall forthwith cry in a loud, clear voice : `The old man smiles again,' and folding his tent like the Arabs, silently beat it. <i>(Oct 1, 1918,Quick March,3</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i> long-lasting, persistent, or in some way large or outstanding of its kind: `old man couch grass'; `an old man southerly'; `an old man snapper'; `an old man flood'. (occas. spg old-man; occ. caps.) superior, large, remarkable (of its kind) (cf GRANDFATHER) esp. withnatural objects se also OLD MAN FLOOD,

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> (Letters, (ed) Harrop 1935) [check this] We found the boar well protected in the rear by an overhanging `old-man fern'. <i>(1870,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,97</i>)

>  ... I feel confident that any one who has seen a Westland river in an `old man' flood would credit the actual upheaval of any sized boulder. <i>(1896,Pioneer Work in the Alps of NZ,134</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Old Man, adj large, or bigger than usual. Compare the next two words. [1845] <i>(1898,Austral English,328</i>)

>  <b>BARRY</b> The `old man' Rimu that took five hundred or more years to grow is laid low in the space of a few minutes by the woodmen's axe. <i>(1966,In the Lee of the Hokonuis,149</i>) [NOTES: 1926 Prospectus of the Waitane Saw-milling and Afforestation Ltd in 1966 Barry In the Lee of the Hokonuis]

>  old man a.-old' man n. a full-grown male kangaroo; hence, old'-man a. unusually large; of goodsize. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,8</i>)

>  <b>DEVANNY</b> Old Man rata. <i>Ibid.</i> -17 [He] had oncetaken to an Old Man pig with a slasher. <i>(1930,Bushman Burke,9</i>)

>  Old man.- Slang. Striking, big, remarkable, e.g. an o.m. rock, flood, nor'-wester, cabbage tree, etc. Hence the o.m is a common name for paddocks, though some hills are probably so called from the fancied resemblance to man's face. (C.) <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>HYDE</b> You've got to be cunning as a fish; there's nothing to look for but a little bump in the mud, and that's old man flounder. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,144</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> Old Man Blackberry Farm <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,253</i>)

>  ... an `Old Man' southerly. <i>(Jan 31, 1951,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  <b>BAXTER</b> A cord of old man manuka,/ I cut in a day. <i>Ibid.</i> 277 Standing above the clump of old man manuka <i>(1979,Collected Poems,241,(1953-61) ed.</i>)

>  <b>ASHTON-WARNER</b> He appears across the playground beneath the Old Man Totara. <i>(1964,Bell Call,63</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> old man a rural object of striking or large appearance; eg old man paddock or old man rock. NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of plants and other aspects of nature] large with an impact. (See [old man gorse](../O#old-man-gorse))

>  <b>MCGILL</b> old man: a rural object of striking or large appearance; eg old man paddock or old man rock. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> We found the boar well protected in the rear by an overhanging 'old-man fern' ... <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,97</i>)

>  First floods, and then an 'old man nor'wester.' <i>(Feb 20, 1930,NZ Dairy Exporter,37</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> old man: a kind of extra-superlative adverbial or adjectival phrase, often used to describe something particularly pestilential or annoying ( eg old-man couch grass or a real old-man nor'wester but it can also indicate something of great age (an old man totara). <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,57</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> 'Old man' scrub like this probably took over after fertility depletion of country which could not hold vigorous pasture ... [old-man] <i>(1988,From the Hills With Vision,32</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i>

>  Whenever you touch hills, there you are on "Old man". <i>(1896,AJHR,D.-4/244</i>)



## Old Man Bottom
 
 <i>n.</i> [goldminers']

>  In Westland these ancient gravels [Montere Gravels] form the true Old Man Bottom of the gold-miners. <i>(1908,NZ Geol. Survey Bulletin,No 6 (N.S.)/113</i>) [NOTES: Morgan, Mikonui]

>  ... it was shown that it is posisble to differentiate between a lead formed of less consolidated gravels, if imbedded in more consolidated gravel,locally known as `Old Man' bottom. [Westland] <i>(1938,NZ Geol. Memoir,No 4/49</i>) [NOTES: Modriniak and Marsden]

>  An old Bendigo miner, who worked in this very locality in 65, says that the "old man" bottom was followed right down as far as the flat, the wash giving out at that point. <i>(Oct 28, 1892,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

## old man flood
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Experiencing a real `old man' flood ... <i>(1933,NZ Alpine Journal,V/20/180</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> This was the famous Old Man Flood [of the Shotover in 1863]; it was the biggest I ever knew ... <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,173</i>)

>  <b>HISLOP</b> It was in 1878 that the Old man flood, as [the Otago miners] called it, happened and swept all the bridges away between Cromwell and Balclutha. <i>(1943,Pure Gold,19</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The `Old Man Flood' of 1863 on the Shotover, Arrow, Kawarau, and Clutha rivers is said to have drowned many diggers, ... <i>(1947,Early Runholding in Otago,61</i>)

>  <b>MOORE</b> ... but a real `old man' flood in the autumn ultimately swept the tangle away and cleared the bed of the stream. <i>(1969,From Forest to Farm,45</i>)

>  The town has had the "Old Man Flood," the "Jubilee Flood," the "Record Reign Flood," but none of them equaled the one that swept the town yesterday. <i>(Jun 26, 1905,Evening Post,5</i>)

>  Then, with the aid of practical engineering talent they could have made a straight cut for the Grey River from Jerusalem point to the beach direct through Coal Creek Flat and the gorge and as straight out to sea as circumstances would permit, the spoil meanwhile being utilized to reclaim all the river frontage on the north side to a height sufficient to defy the biggest "old man flood" ever knwn [sic] and every cubic foot of which would by now have been of immense value. <i>(Aug 14, 1912,Grey River Argus,6</i>)

>  Under the most unfavourable circumstances, the upper portion of the line is not likely to give any trouble, but it is quite possible and not at all unlikely that the lower part may be submerged for a few hours during heavy floods; but it will be more than ordinary, or an "old man" flood that will do this. <i>(Aug 10, 1885,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  The "old man" flood which threatened Hawkes Bay last week has, like Massey's "State Advances Bill", passed safely and the week of anxiety now over, the people are ready, "aye ready!" to discuss the ways and means of BRINGING ABOUT THE DOWNFALL of the great Wellingtonians. <i>(Jul 28, 1923,NZ Truth,9</i>)

>  The Shotover River was fully ten feet higher than during the "old man" flood. <i>(Oct 5, 1878,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  That fickle stream, the Molyneux, rose to a great height, and a second "Old Man" flood was expected. <i>(Jul 6, 1866,Otago Witness,3</i>)



## old man gorse
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a very large gorse bush, difficult to eradicate.

>  In the case of 'old man' gorse, Mr Brodie recommended burning ... <i>(Oct 2, 1950,NZ Dairy Exporter,42</i>)

>  Trials have shown generally poor control with 2,45-T formulations applied to 'old man' gorse ... <i>(Sep 1, 1951,NZ Dairy Exporter,29</i>)

>  'Old man' gorse eradication presented no problem where land could be cultivated either by the plough or giant discs ... <i>(Sep 1, 1951,NZ Dairy Exporter,31</i>)

>  Now, however, a bulldozer makes much easier the initial task of grubbing out the 'old man' gorse. <i>(Sep 27, 1951,NZ Farmer,12</i>)

>  Gorse is from the small to genuine 'old man' stuff. <i>(Mar 1987,NZ Dairy Exporter,25</i>)

>  <b>ADDENBROOKE</b> I trapped several patches of bush on different farms as well as patches of heavy old-man gorse. [old-man gorse] <i>(1992,Home from the Hill,102</i>)



## old man gravel
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>DENNIS</b> Great thicknesses of these sediments were laid down ..., some two thousand metres of them still lie beneath the Old Man Gravels of the Grey River valley. ... More recent and perhaps better known are the Pliocene Old Man Gravels of the Grey-Inangahua Valley, a conglomerate that is part of the large [140] sheets of aggradation gravels deposited both east and west of the ... Southern Alps early in the Kaikoura Orogeny. <i>(1981,Paparoas Guide,139</i>)

>  Tunnels 6ft by 5ft in "Old Man" gravel, "Tapa," and soft sandstone. <i>(Jul 5, 1916,Grey River Argus,1</i>)

>  <b>EASTWOOD</b> There were both a sandstone bottom and a blue-pug bottom on top of what they called "hard old-man" gravels. <i>(1982,Brief History of Gold Mining, A,135</i>)

>  Erosion of rocks overlying the granite produced thick mantles of gravel in the form of a 'piedmont plain'; the series of low hills between Ikamatua and Reefton Saddle are the only remnants of this plain and are called the 'Old man' and 'Moutere' gravels. <i>(1990,Tramping in South Island Forest Parks,121</i>)

>  A little gold in the surface drift, fair dredging prospects in the wash, the richest gravels being on the bottom; total depth, 12ft 6in; bottom, the soft sandstone of the Old Man gravels. <i>(Feb 27, 1901,Otago Witness,21</i>)

## old man snapper
 
 <i>n.</i> [[used with fish freq.]] (See [snapper](../S#snapper))

>  Calculus is a fine upstanding son of Trenton and Rosemary, with the most pronounced Roman nose I have ever seen an animal display; in fact it forcibly put me in mind of an old man schnapper. <i>(Oct 27, 1892,Otago Witness,28</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Many large Snapper are caught with a peculiar hump on the head which is caused by a thickening of the frontal bone. When a fisherman lands such a fish he immediately states: `I've caught an old man Snapper' under the erroneous impression it is an old male fish. I have often dissected these `old man' Snappers and found that there were as many female as male fish with this conspicuous bony knob. The name `old man Snapper' comes from the likeness to the face of an old man with bulbous red nose, a short clipped moustache and tight upper lip, which can be seen in the illustration. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,245</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `Old man snapper' are those fish which develop a hump on top of the head as they become older; often the fleshy upper lip also increases in size. This condition may affect females as well as males. The term `old man may also be applied to old, slabby fish in poor condition, whether they have developed a hump or not. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,227</i>)

>  <i>old man snapper </i> <i>(Feb 1981,Avondale College Slang Words in Use</i>) [NOTES: (Goldie Brown Collection)]

## old man's beard
 
 <i>n.</i> a rampant, troublesome introduced clematis, a strangler of trees and shrubs, whose flowers are replaced in autumn by dense grey-white down. [(cf OLD MAN'S WHISKER(S); TRAVELLERS' JOY) (old man's whisker is also WILD FENNEL)]

>  Old man's beard is a member of the clematis family, and was introduced from Europe as a decorative plant... "This is a good time to look out for old man's beard. It tends to spill out of hedges at this time of year, or you might see it flowering in the tops of trees or along roadsides." <i>(Mar 5, 2002,Independent Herald (Wellington),17</i>)

>  Waving his hands about like David Bellamy stumbling unexpectedly upon a fresh crop of old man's beard, he passes on a fresh secret of business success. <i>(May 12, 1995,Listener (New Zealand Listener),64</i>)

>  a wild clematis in its winter form, clothing hedgerows. <i>(1979,Heinemann NZ Dictionary,746</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> TRAVELLER'S JOY (<i>Clematis vitalba</i>) ... In autumn dense down takes the place of flowers . . ., giving the alternative name `old man's beard'. ... Traveller's joy was introduced from Europe but extends into native forest. <i>(1981,Weeds of Roadsides,149</i>)

>  The fast-growing introduced vine Old Man's Beard is smothering native bush in many parts of the city and surrounding hills, ... Also known as Clematis vitalba, Traveller's Joy and Mile-a-Minute, the climber spreads rapidly. <i>(May 8, 1984,Karori and Western Suburbs News,8</i>)

>  <b>5</b> Old man's beard is raging through native bush and may be stopped only by public concern. -->The Department of Lands and Survey is holding an old man's beard seminar in Wellington in November, detailing the widespread destruction the vine has caused in native bush. <i>(Sep 13, 1982,Evening Post</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Clematis vitalba</i> - TRAVELLER'S JOY or OLD MAN'S BEARD - has innumerable, small, creamy white flowers in late summer ... <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,38</i>)

>  This small area is a conservationist's nightmare, with thick infestations of Lonicera japonica (honeysuckle), Passiflora caerulea (blue passionflower) Tradescantia flumenensis (wandering Willie), garlands of Clematis vitalba (old man's beard), patches of hemlock ready to seed and enough nodding thistle (Carduus nutans) to keep Eeyore the donkey gloom-free for weeks. <i>(Feb 14, 2004,Dominion Post,E10</i>)

>  The control of noxious weed old man's beard on private property in Wellington is being hailed a success by the regional council. <i>(Aug 4, 2000,Evening Post,18</i>)

>  Wellington Regional Council has hunted down old man's beard to the point of coming onto people's properties. <i>(Feb 15, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26</i>)

>  Saturday.. was a warm and sunny day for the 28 volunteers (plus nine apologies) who turned up at the Taihape Kindergarten to cut Old Man's Beard on Mt Stewart. <i>(Jan 26, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),12</i>) [NOTES: Original in Central District Times 20/10/01]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Usnea</i> a lichen that grows on tree trunks and branches. [cf OLD MAORI'S BEARD; cf]

>  <b>HAAST</b> ... the beech forest ... now had become dwarfish and covered with <i>Usnea barbata</i> (that peculiar pendant greenish white lichen) ...] <i>(1879,Geology of the Provinces of Canterbury & Westland,109</i>)

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> ... from the branches hang bunches of that wonderful lichen, `old man's beard', and slender lance-wood emerges ... from the convolvulus-leaved but thorny `lawyers' and the low manuca scrub. <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,152</i>)

>  Of these are some of our larger ... tree-lichens, such as ... <i>Usnea barbata</i>, Fries (`old man's beard'), several varieties. <i>(1893,TrNZI,25/316</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> ... the pendent `old man's [145] beard' that, yards in length, swings from the tawa boughs. <i>(1927,Birds of New Zealand,144</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> [when the rimu] tree becomes old these splendid leafs [sic] become smaller and grow in tufts ... giving it the appearance of being dead, or `covered with old Mauries beards' rather than natural leafs. [Editor's Note 10, p. 117 says: Usually [sic] known as Old Man's Beard, a lichen (<i>Usnea</i>).] <i>(1956,NZ Journal,117</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> Foliaceous Lichens - mainly <i>Sticta fossulata</i>, and tufts of Old Man's Beard (<i>Usnea</i>), on dead tree branches in Stewart Island forest <i>(1961,Flora NZ,51, fig. 120,1929 ed.</i>)

>  Recent reports about the threat posed to some North Island bush by an invasive exotic clematis confused some people, says a reader, because the popular name, `old man's beard,' applied to the clematis has also been applied to a native lichen. <i>(Oct 9, 1982,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Sphaerophorus tener Vegetable wool</i> ... Woolly clumps of intricately branching, fine white stems, sometimes stained pale pink. ... It can look similar to OLD MAN'S BEARD LICHENS, <i>Usnea</i> spp., which drape dead branches, shrubs and rocks. <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,486</i>)

## old settler
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  After some further desultory discussion, the meeting was called to order, when Mr MacAndrew said that, although it was customary on occasions like the present for candidates to expound their political sentiments, in his case this might, perhaps, to some extent be dispensed with, seeing that he was now an old stager in Otago politics... <i>(May 6, 1863,Otago Witness,6</i>)

## old woman from Botany Bay
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> The Dialogue game of Caricature includes The Old Lady from Botany bay. In this game the children must not laugh at the old lady although she does her best to provoke them to do so. It has been known as Diggley Bones (Auckland [19]00), An Old Man from B.B. (Palmerston [19]10), An Old Lady from Poverty bay (Wellington [19]00). It is widely reported in the last twenty years. The Old Lady comes up to the children and says `Here comes the Old Lady from Botany Bay, and what [433]have you got to give her today. You must neither laugh nor smile but say ----'. Then the child would say something funny but try not to laugh when doing so. ... (Nelson [19]49). <i>(1953,Unorganized Games,2/432</i>)

## oldest white inhabitant
 
 <i>n.</i> A NZ twist to the well-known English village personality.

>  The gentleman referred to was one of the first brokers in Reefton - the "oldest white inhabitant" of the local share market, and he was a whale. He could sse more shares for more money to fewer people in less time than anything that ever walked - in Reefton. <i>(Nov 3, 1888,West Coast Times,2</i>)

>  <b>HOLLOWAY</b> That much abused authority `the oldest white inhabitant'. <i>(Jun 18, 1874,Journal of a Visit,117</i>)

>  She was one of the oldest white inhabitants of New Zealand, having resided in the Bay since 1815, and leaves nearly 80 descendants. <i>(May 2, 1867,Daily Southern Cross,8</i>)

>  I had all along the line been directed to visit this gentleman, who is a celebrity in Taumaranui, owing to his being the oldest white inhabitant. <i>(Jul 24, 1907,Otago Witness,90</i>)

>  The oldest white inhabitant could scarcely credit that [the Fijian snake] was an indigenous product. <i>(Oct 21, 1897,Otago Witness,13</i>)

## olive
 
 <i>n.</i> a tree and its fruit

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Maire, (ii). 1. Olea Cunninghamii </i>and other species of olive. [so 1957/168] <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,195</i>)

>  If the olive proper was grafted on to the New Zealand olive, Sir James [Hector] said, the trees would bear fruit in 12 months... <i>(Nov 23, 1898,Evening Post,4</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>POWELL</b> SOUTHERN OLIVE (<i>Baryspira australis</i>). A handsome polished shell up to 1 1/2 inches in height. It is bluish slate around the middle and dark brown both on the spire and on the base. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,29</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>LOCKLEY</b> ... plump brown wallabies peering at us tamely from among the stunted pohutukawas, native olive (olearia), and manuka, <i>(1980,House Above the Sea,95</i>)

>  <b>DAWSON</b> Three of the four species of <i>Nestegis</i> or `native olives' have narrow adult leaves. In the case of two species, black maire (<i>N. cunninghamii</i>) and white maire (<i>N. lanceolata</i>), the juveniles are much narrower than the adults ... <i>(1988,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,42</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i> Olea</i>

>  <b>HUDSON</b> <i> Olea apetala </i> <i>(1898,NZ Moths & Butterflies</i>)

>  Among the ... plants on or near sea cliffs ... may be mentioned ... the tawapou (<i>Sideroxyloncostatum</i>), sometimes called the New Zealand olive, from the resemblance of its fruit to that of the olive (the true New Zealand olive is the maire -<i> Olea</i>). <i>(1911,TrNZI,43/200</i>)

>  <i>Olea apetala </i> ....<i>Sideroxylon novo-zelandicum </i> <i>(1911,TrNZI,43 :</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Olea</i>sp. <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <i>old man's beard [=] traveller's joy [s] </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,54</i>)

## Omahina tephra
 
 <i>n.</i> Geol. defn.

>  Another widespread silicic airall tephra reported from southern North Island is the Rangitawa Pumice and its possible correlatives, the Omahina Tephra (Pillans 1979), the Lower Finnie Road Ash (Seward 1974 ; Pillans & Kohn 1981), and the Mangaroa Ash (Neeser et al. 1980), all with fission-track dates in the two-standard deviation age range of 370 +- 70 ky BP (Pillans & Kohn 1981; Kyle & Seward 1984; Kohl 1986, oral presentation). <i>(Nov 1988,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,82/36</i>)



## Ombudsman
 
 <i>n.</i> an officer of Parliament appointed by the Legislature to investigate complaints by individuals against the state or public services. [Swedish, legal representative]

>  <b>TURNER</b> In July 1961 he was to be called the `Parliamentary Commissioner for Investigation'.... The office was modelled on the Scandinavian ombudsman and this word was used in the meantime ... . The Plural ombudsmen appeared, and a man checking hotel measures was called a drinker'sombudsman In 1962 the name ombudsman was officially adopted. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,174</i>)

>  <i>(Jun 8, 1966,Weekly News,10/2</i>) [NOTES: OEDS]

>  <i>(Sep 28, 1966,NZ News,3/2</i>) [NOTES: OEDS]

>  <b>HILL</b> Although the Ombudsman has the power to be self-activating, nearly all of his investigations are initiated by citizens' complaints. Hence, it is scarcely an exaggeration to say that the Ombudsman's role is largely determined by his complaints. <i>(1976,Model Ombudsman,79</i>)

>  It has recommended a small change in the title from Parliamentary Commissioner for Investigations Bill to Parliamentary Commissioner (Ombudsman) Bill. The reason is that the public is not talking about the Parliamentary Commissioner for Investigations but about the appointment of an ombudsman. <i>(1962,NZ Parliamentary Debates,331/1758</i>)

>  I have no proposals to bring before the government for the appointment of a consumers ' ombudsman. <i>(1972,NZ Parliamentary Debates,381/3558</i>)

>  It is proposed to extend the jurisdiction of the Ombudsman to all local authorities. <i>(1973,NZ Parliamentary Debates,382/10</i>)

>  The term "ombudsman" has general acceptance and has passed into common usage. The reservations that existed 12 years ago over using an unfamiliar and foreign term are no longer supportable. <i>(1974,NZ Parliamentary Debates,395/5735</i>)

>  There will be a Chief Ombudsman who will be the prime Ombudsman. <i>(1974,NZ Parliamentary Debates,395/5737</i>)

>  There shall be appointed, as an officer of Parliament, a Commissioner for investigations, to be called the Ombudsman. <i>(1962,NZ Statutes,10/107</i>)

>  It shall be a function of the Ombudsman to investigate any decision or recommendation made.. relating to a matter of administration and affecting any person or body of persons in his or its personal capacity, in or by any of the Departments or organisations named or specified in.. the First Schedule of this Act. <i>(1975,NZ Statutes,9/98</i>)



## Omega lamb
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a young lamb under 22 pounds destined for the Greek market.

>  <b>KERR</b> They watched any changes in the grading of beef; and the killing sheets of their lamb grades were, and are, a puzzle - 'P', 'PM' 'M' - though they know what an Omega lamb is. I didn't, but I discovered that those lambs under 22lbs (Omegas) are sent, only in cuts, to the market in Greece; but since March 1976, their export as carcases is permitted. <i>(1976,From the Beginning: chronicles of a county,350</i>)

## on compo
 
 <i>phr.</i> absent from work receiving compensation

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Men off work on compo with a strained back and lugging heavy furniture on to the moving-van at home. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,225</i>)

>  <i>compo ... n. </i>workman's compensation for injury incurred while at work [Abbrev. of <i>compensation</i>]; whence<i> off on compo, to go on compo.</i> <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),6</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>compo</i> compensation for lost wages from work-related injury, formalised as Accident Compensation. <i>- on compo</i> in receipt of such. <i>- compo king</i> malingerer or self-mutilator, with the aim of getting compo. All NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,30</i>)

>  I am now entering my second week on compo. <i>(Oct 2, 1923,Letter,00</i>) [NOTES: MS letter from "Jack" (a seaman) to F.P. Tuohy (F.P. Walsh) 2 Oct.]

>  <b>Carr</b> [caption to photograph of a dog] Tiger throwing a "sickie" - on compo after that dunking in the dark. <i>(1986,Diary of a Pig Hunter,171</i>)



## on drop/on the drop
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [ewe or cow] about to lamb or calve.

>  He turns his cows out in the winter on indifferent pasture and brings them home, generally 'on the drop' in low condition and in a weak state. <i>(Oct 29, 1927,NZ Dairy Exporter,26</i>)

>  Heifers on drop have been worth GBP14 to GBP18 10/-. <i>(Sep 1, 1949,NZ Farmer,24</i>)

>  Cows on drop were worth up to GBP22, heifers on drop GBP18 to GBP23. <i>(Oct 25, 1951,NZ Farmer,34</i>)

>  <b>MAF</b> On-the-drop: Animal about to give birth. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,13</i>)

>  A small lot of autumn shorn ewes 'on drop' sold at $53.50. <i>(Aug 1, 1996,NZ Farmer,42</i>)

>  You'll notice that ewes heavy in lamb and 'on-the-drop' slow up a lot. <i>(2002,Lifestyle Farmer,June/July 54</i>)

>  <b>MAF</b> On-the-drop: Animal about to give birth. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,13</i>)

>  Lambing has gone well with Nelson farmers reporting the highest drop ever. b. on-the-drop, of a farm animal, about to give birth. <i>(Sep 20, 1986,Radio NZ / National Radio</i>) [NOTES: Landline Agric. Programme 2YA National Radio Programme]

>  <b>Special Ad</b> On the drop ewes. <i>(Jul 8, 2006,Hawke's Bay Today,9,Weekend Market ed.</i>)



## on gold
 
 <i>phr.</i> working a payable claim.

>  <b>PFAFF</b> [We were] getting excellent gold. From that time until May 1867, we were always `on it'. <i>(1914,Diggers' Story, The,66</i>)

>  ... but after all there are only 11 claims on gold ... <i>(Jan 9, 1869,Grey River Argus,3</i>)

>  <b>PYKE</b> They've struck a big old placer somewhere, you bet. They are on heavy gold, too, for I saw some of the stuff that they traded away ... <i>Ibid.</i> Bk III ch ii 77 But they're on gold anyhow, ... And there's a whole mob of fellows shepherding them [to discover their claim]. <i>(1873,Wild Will Enderby,3/i/75,1889, 1974 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> The Chinese, with their frugal habits when not `on-gold' ... and their capacity for earnest co-operation were making [the claim] pay handsomely. <i>(1974,Colonial Experiences,129</i>)

>  <b>WOOD</b> We got on gold soon, but all the money we made we sent to Polly; ... <i>(1875,Waiting for the Mail,34</i>)

>  <b>PRESHAW</b> Nearly all [the diggings] were on gold, but no pile claims. <i>(1888,Banking Under Difficulties,157</i>)

>  It was first stated that every claim was on gold; the poorest paying [pounds] 40 per week,... <i>(Mar 2, 1867,West Coast Times</i>) [NOTES: 1867 Mar 2 West Coast Times in 1985 Bromby Eye Witness History 49]

>  They've struck a big old placer somewhere, you bet. They are on heavy gold too. <i>(1873,Wild Will Enderby,Bk III ch i 75,1889 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MILLER</b> I was on gold all the time. I had sunk my first prospect hole at the lower end of a shallow depression ... and had struck the deepest part of the lead. It was a narrow gutter, no more than afoot wide at the most, and rich in heavy gold. <i>(1946,There Was Gold,69</i>)

## on it
 
 <i>phr.</i> (informal) drinking heavily

>  <b>SLATTER</b> The news soon got around that we were on it. Horns peremptory at the gate, blokes coming up the drive every night, some lumping cartons of beer, some carrying jars of draught, many of them with nothing but a nose for parties. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,14</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> on it drinking alcohol; eg `I'm sick of footy practice. Let's get on it instead, eh?' NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> be + adj. + on it

>  <b>SARGESON</b> He looked pretty crook on it. <i>(1946,That Summer,96</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> You don't look too good on it what's wrong? <i>(1976,Sunset Village,35</i>)



## on one's ace
 
 <i>phr.</i> [AND 1904: f. a punning euphemism for arse with ace, a singleton.] alone, on one's own; on one's pat (Pat Malone).

>  What was `Arry's lotion? Did he have `a handle' of beer every time ... And was he on his ace, drinking with the flies, or did he shout for the boys like a toff? <i>(May 29, 1909,NZ Truth,205/7/3</i>)

>  Not Understood! Yer meant t' 'elp that neddy by givin' 'im a dose before the race; the stooards cop yer, and yer tale ain't ready; so 'ome yer go upon yer bloomin' ace. <i>(Feb 8, 1913,NZ Bulletin,16</i>)

>  When men are picked around me,/ And I'm left upon my ace. <i>(1926,Transport Workers' Song Book,66</i>)

>  <b>NEWBOLD</b> ACE (n) 2. On your - . Alone, on your own. <i>(1982,Big Huey, The,244</i>) [NOTES: nf OEDS, EDD, P]

## on one's hammer

## on one's own hook
 
 <i>phr.</i> on one's own account, for oneself

>  <b>BELL</b> [`one one's own hook', `for oneself'] `Why, mother, poor old mother,' said Harry, ... `who would have expected to find you here [in New Zealand]? I thought you were still in the Old Country.' [88] ... Annie, who was married just before we left the old country, and Tom, who is working on his own hook now, but not far from here. <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,87</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i>

## on one's pat
 
 <i>phr.</i> on one's own, alone

>  <b>ANTHONY</b> ... a boy along to help Gus on the stack, but he said `No', a man should be able to build a round stack on his pat, and he didn't want any boy standing about in the way while he was working. <i>(1927,Me and Gus,73</i>) [NOTES: a1927 Anthony Me and Gus (1977 Gus Tomlins ed. Sturm) 73]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>Pat (or Pat), on one's</i>. Alone; single-handed: Australian and New Zealand: C. 20. C. J. Dennis, 1916. Ex <i>Pat Malone</i>, q.v. .. <i>Pat.</i> 2. (Gen. pl.) A Chinaman: New Zealand c. (- 1932). Prob. suggested by the relevant sense of <i>John. </i> <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,609</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> `It's tough if he's got a wife and kids somewhere.' `Why? If he was enjoying himself on his pat, why wasn't that just as hard?' <i>Ibid.</i> 187 She [the river] ain't used to it [sc. dam-building]. She's been going along on her pat for a long time now. <i>Ibid.</i> 214 The fact is, I'm a stranger here and on my pat. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,124</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> But why not stay the night, Dave? Mr Anderson went on. Don't leave a man all on his pat. <i>(1949,I Saw in My Dream,216,1974 ed. Rhodes ed.</i>)

>  <b>SYDER & HODGETTS</b> (said, `ON ME PAT';) Alone. .in 4;`I can travel faster by myself - I'll be on me pat today'. <i>(1974,Australian & NZ English [TS],813</i>) [NOTES: (1962-63)1974 Syder and Hodgetts Aust. and NZ English [TS] 813]

>  <b>SARGESON</b> pat: <i>all on my pat</i>, by myself. <i>(1964,Collected Stories,301</i>)

>  <i>pat, on one's (colloq. phrase).</i> alone, on one's own, shortened form of `on one's Pat Malone.' <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),622</i>)

>  <i>pat, on one's (Coll.phr.)</i> alone [Rhyming slang Pat Malone]. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),15</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Good old Johnny ... Remember him getting two tries all on his pat at Castelraimodo? <i>(1970,On the Ball,135</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>pat</i>, phr. <i>on one's pat</i> single-handed or alone, short for rhyming slang `on one's Pat Malone/alone' or on your `own'; eg `Since Mabel passed on, Ron's been on his pat for too much. Time we went round and jollied him up, fellas. Agree?' ANZ. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,82</i>)

>  I'll stick on me belt and gas bag, and buzz off to the village on me pat. <i>(Jan 2, 1918,Chronicles NZEF,III/34: 13</i>)



## on one's Pat Malone
 
 <i>phr.</i> on one's own, alone (See [also on one's pat](../A#also-on-one's-pat))

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>Pat Malone</i>. Alone: Australian and New Zealand rhyming s.: C. 20. Gen <i>do a thing, go, on one's Pat Malone</i>, hence <i>on one's Pat</i> (hence <i>pat</i>). <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,609</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> usually shortened to on one's pat; alone: Pacific coast: C. 20. M and B, 1944. Rhyming slang introduced by Australians. <i>(1949,Dictionary of the Underworld,498</i>)

>  <b>BEATON</b> DI: Scared you might spend the Festive Season on Your Pat Malone? <i>Ibid</i>. Glossary 110 <i>to be alone. </i> <i>(1984,Outside In,79</i>)

>  <b>OWEN & PERKINS</b> Different things brought people together to swag. There was I, on my Pat Malone, starting off on my first experience at humping a swag. <i>(1986,Speaking for Ourselves,68</i>) [NOTES: (Bob Edwards, bushman)]



## on one's reputation
 
 <i>phr.</i> Construction: to do, perform, accomplish, etc. something on one's reputation, to do the `near impossible', to accomplish without visible means, etc. against expectation or `the odds' ca1953 Christmas Charlie Buchanan, rabbiter, Bluff Station, Clarence Valley, remarks of trampers who have used a difficult and treacherous track: `You came round there on your reputation'.



## on protection
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>BEATON</b> MA: Screws said you went on protection.... DI: I've never been scared of you girls and you know it. Me? Ask to go on protection? Never! I copped the lot. <i>Ibid.</i> 110 <i>Go on protection</i>: to request solitary confinement as a protection other inmates <i>(1984,Outside In,82</i>)



## on shares
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sp. use] sharemilking.

>  Wanted, to milk on shares, between 40 and 50 good cows. <i>(Oct 2, 1902,Hawera Star,1</i>)

>  Wanted, milking on shares, two men, first-class milkers. <i>(Oct 3, 1902,Hawera Star,3</i>)

>  Wanted, by a family of six milkers from Canterbury, from 60 to 80 cows on shares. <i>(Oct 6, 1903,Hawera Star,01</i>)



## on special
 
 <i>phr.</i> an item offered for a short time at a reduced price

>  "My old man once bought a ton of Speights. It was on special in Dunedin." <i>(Jul 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 64/34</i>)



## on terms
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sheep] a long term grazing arrangement where sheepowner and landowner share profit of wool sales.

>  <b>HOBHOUSE</b> Flocks are often let on Terms ... <i>(Nov 4, 1859,Letter</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> In 1856 Porter took the run and sheep on term ... <i>(1946,Early Canterbury Runs,102</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> Sheep may be placed out in the same manner, on terms ... <i>(1857,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,46</i>)

>  Everyone suggests different investments. One says buy sheep and put them out on terms. <i>(1860,BLANK ENTRY,39</i>) [NOTES: in BUTLER First Year (1863)]

>  <b>BALFOUR</b> Under this arrangement, a landless man, who had enough capital to buy sheep, could graze them on another man's property. Usually, the grazier took one-half of the annual wool clip of the 'terms' sheep and one-third of the total increase at the end of the period. J D Ormond, before his purchase of Wallingford, grazed sheep 'on terms' or 'thirds' on the property of Richard Collins, a Wairarapa sheepfarmer. <i>(1885,Balfour Diaries,75</i>)

>  <b>CRAWFORD</b> Putting 'sheep on terms' was a very common custom in the early days. <i>(1949,Sheep and Sheepmen of Canterbury,37</i>)



## on the bash
 
 <i>n.</i> on the spree, on the booze. [1953 15 M A21 Thames DHS 31 BASH (DRINKING = ON THE BASH)]

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> `But dinkum, I mean it. And when you get back, we'll ... go on a dirty great bash together, won't we, Eddie?' <i>(1945,Gunner Inglorious,157</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> We go on the bash. <i>(1950,Brave Company,8</i>)

>  `I know,' I said, `but if you go on the bash and start abusing these people, they'll lock you in the cooler ...' <i>(1978,Listener Short Stories,2/20</i>) [NOTES: 1951 Young in 1978 Listener Short Stories Vol 2/20]

>  I would include among New Zealand English words: <i>on the bash </i> <i>(May 6, 1952,VUC First Year English Class</i>)

>  Ya been on the bash for three days <i>(Sep 1953,Here & Now,3/II/35</i>)

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> Jack was my cobber in the timber mill. Jack and I went on the bash every Saturday. ... Drink all day ... <i>(1959,New Zealanders, The,156,1986 ed.</i>) [NOTES: (Knock on Yesterday's Door)]

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>on the bash</i> an alcoholic drinking bout, particularly in New Zealand and Scotland; eg `I feel ghastly. I was on the bash most of the night.' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)



## on the battens
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sheep or shearers] in a woolshed pen.

>  <b>ACTON-ADAMS</b> Allow no men in the shed after working hours, no pressing of scoured wool by candlelight, and no smoking on the battens. <i>(Dec 13, 1901,Letter</i>) [NOTES: Letter to manager]

>  The hoof should be well-dressed with a mixture of Stockholm tar and powdered bluestone, and the sheep left on the battens overnight. <i>(Aug 9, 1944,Weekly News,29</i>)



## on the beach
 
 <i>phr.</i> broke; unemployed [.[HWO Has this been extended from the seamen's idiom, to be on shore out of work or waiting to sign on a ship: modern seamen's<i> to be on the corner</i>.]] (See [cf. beached](../B#beached))

>  <b>SARGESON</b> I'm on the beach myself, I said, but I can make it a deener. <i>(1946,That Summer,77</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> beach: <i>on the beach</i>, broke. <i>(1964,Collected Stories,297</i>)



## on the bed
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) the dressing of a carcass in a special cradle.

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> bed, on the: method of dressing carcasses as they lie in a special cradle. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,6</i>)



## on the blind
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [of sheepdogs] out of sight of sheep being worked.

>  <b>LILICO</b> In training to run on the blind, open country without fences or landmarks of any kind should be chosen ... <i>(1919,Breeding and Training of Sheep Dogs,19</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> The dog of course was 'running on the blind' ... <i>(1964,Straggle Muster,16</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> Blind, run on the: A dog is 'on the blind' when he is is running out of sight of his sheep. [running on the blind] <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,7</i>)

>  Championship to include running on the blind for not less than ten sheep in two separate lots. <i>(Aug 1, 1919,NZ Farmer,986</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> It had been at least a two mile head run on the blind and for the greater part the little dog had been out of sight of his master. <i>(1949,High Country Days,31</i>)

>  <b>MILLS ET AL</b> Hold him [the sheepdog] until he does [sight the sheep] and he will then go away freely with the right direction and cast instead of running 'on the blind,' Running 'blind' often leads a dog into the bad habit of loitering out until he sights his sheep and is therefore to be avoided. <i>(1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,32</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> A hard beat is usually one of the middle beats and very often includes bluffs and also much need to run dogs on the blind. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,6</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> without prior information or proper sight or foresight

>  <b>LYON</b> As the quartz mines began to decline in output and could no longer pay a large wage sheet, it became the custom to let them on tribute, by which parties of men were given the right to mine and raise quartz from the resultant crushing, of which a proportionate share of the gold went to the company. This system might also apply to old workings of mines in actual work, but in most of these cases the tributors followed a thin but valuable lead, which might not be much thicker than a finger, but so rich as to be worth tunnelling or working as a tribute, in which the golden lead was pursued on an upward slope. It used to be rather shrewdly suspected that tributors were not `working on the blind' but had as former employees of the company marked a lead and covered the same by a piece of mine timber or rubble till a later date give them the chance to profit by their knowledge. Many of the tributors did exceedingly well; they had nothing except their labour to lose, and the company got some profit on a good lead, and so `where's the harm?' <i>(1952,Faring South,168</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> ... the short staple of wool on the Merino allows a shearer to see and handle his work more easily. With the long wools, quite a lot of hand-work is done `on the blind'. <i>(1953,Golden Bush, The,175,1963 ed.</i>)

>  <b>TUCKER</b> [?horseracing] It was a question of backing on the blind because nobody was supposed to know in advance which mule carried which saddlecloth [indicating the number carried in the race]. <i>(1978,Thoroughbreds Are My Life,26</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> (working) out of sight of the shepherd (of a sheepdog); often run on the blind

>  <b>LILICO</b> ... though he was very perfect on sheep within sight of him, he was no use at running on the blind in rough ground. <i>(1934,Sheep Dog Memoirs,6</i>)

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> <i>Casting `On the Blind</i>' ... A good opportunity is often presented when driving a mob through a paddock. A few will go off on a side track over a ridge. The trainer is certain of their whereabouts but it is a run `on the blind' to the young dogs. . <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,25</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> <i>On the blind</i>: A dog running where he cannot see his sheep. <i>(1972,Mountain World,310</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> RUNNING ON THE BLIND: A dog which is running out with no sheep in sight is `running on the blind.' <i>(1949,High Country Days,197,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> <i>Running on the blind</i>: When a dog is being sent after sheep which are out of his sight he is running on the blind. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,189</i>)



## on the block
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) on the hill sections of a station.

>  <b>HALL</b> A typical tailing yard, built on the block in just half an hour. There are two good reasons for tailing on the block. One is that it is difficult moving uneducated lambs - by that I mean ones that aren't used to being worked by shepherds and dogs. <i>(1987,Shepherd's Year,  A,29</i>)



## on the bludge

## on the board
 
 <i>phr.</i> working as shearers, shearing

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> ... 34 shearers being on the board, ... <i>(1940,Te Waimate,210,1954 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SCRYMGEOUR</b> Perhaps a week had passed, 26 men on the board, mostly Australians, who came over in the off-season making a good cheque on the soft cutting New Zealand flocks, then hired their professional ability back to the land of the kangaroo, ... <i>(1960,Memories of Maoriland,48</i>) [NOTES: [the collection of shearers]]

>  `Do you have open shed or contract shearing?' `I prefer the contract gang, but you have to help your local shearers; some wives are on the table, and we're often on the board too.' <i>(Oct 11, 1986,Radio NZ / National Radio,Landline Farming Programme</i>)



## on the board/s
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sheep] in the process of being shorn.

<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) at work on a shearing floor.

## on the cross
 
 <i>phr.</i> engaged in dishonest practice.

>  If not that he is occasionally found navvying, and there he is boss, and when he is not in that line he is found eking out an existence on the goldfields, and it is strange, passing strange, that a Chow on a deserted goldfield can always show color[sic], and if it is not on a deserted field, the Chow, too, can always be found on the `cross' (viz.) buying crook stuff. <i>(Sep 21, 1907,NZ Truth,118/5/6</i>)



## on the crutch
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) using a crutch to plunge a sheep's head under the dipping fluid at a pot or swim dip.

>  <b>STRONACH</b> We had two men on the 'crutch' pushing the sheep's heads under. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,55</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> And although the irrespressible Brownie was screaming at the man on the crutch to duck him again, he was hauled, cursing and spluttering, to safety. <i>(1949,High Country Days,104</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> The only person who was busy was the man on the crutch doing the plunging. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,132</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> The man on the crutch was the only person who had an easy job as no matter what you did the sheep never came out very fast. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,165</i>)



## on the cuff, a bit
 
 <i>phr.</i> beyond what is normal; excessive; severe, etc.: Aust. and NZ: since ca1930.

>  ... a bit on the cuff, that sort of thing. <i>(Nov 16, 1942,Johnny Enzed in the Middle East,8</i>)

>  <b>FULLARTON</b> That's a bit on the cuff, Dig. <i>(1944,Troop Target,85</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>Bit on the cuff, a</i>. Rather `thick' - rather excessive, <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang Addenda,991</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>cuff</i> phr. <i>on the cuff </i>excessive or unfair or inappropriate; perhaps rhyming collocation `a bit rough', suggests OEDS; eg `Steady on, old boy, that language is a bit on the cuff.' Late C19. NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,32</i>)

## on the flats
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) level, closely-settled areas of land [usually used for dairying and cropping.]

>  At one time maize for grain was an important cash crop on the flats. Stores are brought down off the hill runs to be finished off on the luscious paspalum-white clover pastures on the flats. <i>(Jan 15, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,15</i>)

>  There are never sufficient pigs on the flats to cope with the skim milk in the spring. <i>(Jan 15, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,17</i>)

>  <b>BLAKE</b> Corletts were running a few dry cows on the flats, and Roy being a townie dog had more than likely never seen a cattle beast before. <i>(1999,Trappers, Dogs 'n' Deer,70</i>)

 <i>phr.</i> on a drinking bout

 <i>phr.</i> about to happen or in progress

>  <b>SARGESON</b> go: in a few sidebets on the go as well, in progress; I didn't go much on, I didn't care much for, wasn't attracted by; would go down well, would be very acceptable; went on a treat, carried on terribly. <i>(1964,Collected Stories,300</i>) [NOTES: Pearson Glossary]
