## on the hill
 
 <i>phr.</i> iup the mountain

>  And the Auckland man.. is still revelling in a fantastic day on the hill with his 12-year-old son. <i>(Oct 8, 2001,Evening Post,19</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [high country] on a mustering job.

>  A good duff is good indeed and a slice kept for lunch next day is a very sustaining food on the hill but a bad duff is terrible. <i>(Oct 27, 1938,NZ Farmer,10</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Old Bill, who had had his day on the hill and was slowing up, made a practice of carrying a stick like a shepherd's crook ... <i>(1947,Wayleggo,74</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. one of the most capable and efficient musterers I have met in my twenty years on the hill. <i>(1947,Wayleggo,109</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> A musterer could be 8000 feet up, scrambling through a rocky bluff 'as steep as a hen's face' but in his phlegmatic parlance, he is simply 'on the hill.' <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,42</i>)

>  <b>HALL</b> The young dog had never been run on the hill before. <i>(1987,Shepherd's Year,  A,83</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Allan was the logical choice for this beat as he knew the country and was a first class man on the hill. <i>(1949,High Country Days,27</i>)

>  <b>BROSNAHAN</b> The hill man didn't do anything on the flat and flat guys didn't go on the hills. <i>(1998,Glenaray 1898-1998,71</i>)



## on the hoof
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) used with reference to live animals in contests, exhibitions, or for grading.

>  <b>TAIT</b> Every year there are one or more clients who are in the first four prizes, either 'on hoof' or 'on hooks.' <i>(1956,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol I,20</i>)

>  From a survey of lambs 'on the hoof' it appeared that the overall standard was very good ... <i>(Jan 2, 1941,NZ Farmer,7</i>)

>  After winning the chiller class on the hoof and the Borthwick Cup for the best ... <i>(Apr 12, 1951,NZ Farmer,15</i>)

>  Their winning entry in the lightweight national class was also placed second on the hoof by judges ... <i>(Jun 1, 1994,NZ Farmer,26</i>)

>  <b>HINDMARSH</b> .. there was mutton galore on the hoof ... <i>(1997,Come Be a Pioneer,47</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [animals] walking rather than being transported.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> These [lambs] went to Fairfield on the hoof and killed out at the incredible average of over 42 pounds. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,239</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> But by 1939 a change was beginning to take place in the movement of stock. No longer did all stock arrive on the hoof. <i>(1989,Pioneer's Life, A,80</i>)

>  They paid higher prices on the hoof than were justified. <i>(Sep 1, 1925,NZ Farmer,1426</i>)

>  In the early days all transport of stock was on the hoof, and it often took a runholder several weeks to reach his holding with his newly-acquired flocks. <i>(May 15, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,461</i>)

>  <b>MEEK</b> The swagger was a pioneer of transport on the hoof. <i>(1952,Station Days in Maoriland,24</i>)

>  <b>FOSTER & WRIGHT</b> By the time this herd is up to full strength there'll be, estimates a stock agent, up to a million dollars' worth of cattle on the hoof. <i>(1983,Stockman Country,38</i>)

>  <b>DAHM</b> In those days all the stock had to be moved on the hoof. <i>(2000,People of the Axe, The,46</i>)



## on the hooks
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [butchery] used with reference to a slaughtered animal's final dressed carcass or its weight.

>  .. those present were able to witness the whole process from the time the lambs were killed until they were judged on the hooks ... <i>(Jan 2, 1941,NZ Farmer,7</i>)

>  Two classes of fat lambs which were judged on the hoof at the Manawatu spring show were judged on the hooks at the Longburn freezing works ... <i>(Dec 19, 1940,NZ Farmer,18</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> Hooks, on the: a term used in referring to the final dressed weight of a carcass. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,43</i>)

>  I couldn't see any point in running a cattle operation where I paid more per kilogram liveweight than the equivalent of what I eventually got paid for them on the hooks. <i>(Sep 1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,08</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> (of slaughtered carcasses)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> Quick-maturing, well covered [145] with meat, and hanging up attractively `on the hooks', the Southdown has long been regarded as the premier meat breed of New Zealand. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,144</i>)



## on the horse's back
 
 <i>phr.</i> on a winner

>  [Advt] On the Horse's Back. <i>(Apr 2004,NZ Memories,No. 47/69</i>)



## on the kivvy
 
 <i>phr.</i> on the look out (qui vive)

>  <b>WHITWORTH</b> I was down there with Old Ashmore's lot, doing the trampoline, and so on; but things was very quizby. Everything went crooked somehow, and the gorger had a hawful cramp in his kick. Well, ramp was the word, and vamose we did, the lot on us; some here, some there. I was reg'lar beached, and I tell you when a cove 'as to spout his smish it's about time to look out. However, I got a job at a livery stable, but although that kep' me in grub, and I slep' in a spare stall, the billet was very multy, and I kep' on the kivvy for something better. [fn] 1. [trampoline] Vaulter. 2. [quizby] Bad. 3. [crooked] Wrong. 4. [cramp in his kick] Emptiness in his pockets. 5. [ramp] Clear out. 6. [vamose] Go. 7. [beached] Hard up. 8. [smish] Shirt. 9. [multy] No good. 10. [kivvy] Look out (qui vive.) <i>(1879,Spangles and Sawdust,5</i>)



## on the knocker
 
 <i>phr.</i> immediately payable , esp. cash on the knocker. [ca1949 Havelock store: `No tick; cash on the knocker.']

>  <i>knocker ... 4. on the knocker. Austral. & N.Z. inf. </i>promptly:<i> you pay on the knocker here. </i> <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,622</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1986 NZPOD; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 273]]

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i> knocker,</i> phr.<i> on the knocker </i>punctual; originally `cash on the knocker', meaning cash on demand, prompt payment required; eg `Crombie is invariably here on the knocker for council meetings, no matter how busy he might be.' NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,66</i>)



## on the land
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) being an active farmer [not merely living in a rural area.]

>  Their days on the land are over - their time of making do with second-hand gear now in the dim, distant past. <i>(2002,Lifestyle Farmer,Oct/Nov 31</i>)

>  Girls on the Land: Excellent Work Done. <i>(Aug 12, 1942,Weekly News,6</i>)

>  Girls on the Land: Successful Solution of Farm Labour Problem. <i>(Sep 16, 1942,Weekly News,29</i>)

## on the nod
 
 <i>phr.</i> [drug users']

>  It is hard to control a cigarette when you are `on the nod'. ... When a person injects heroin, the body responses shut down.Heroin is an anaesthetic. When a person is `on the nod' which does not necessarily follow from a shot, they appear asleep, but are still conscious of what is going on around them.... And the effects of homebake are about the same as for other narcotics - an initial rush of euphoria, followed by more long-lasting euphoric feelings of contentment and detachment (called `being on the nod'). <i>(Apr 5, 1986,Evening Post,19</i>)



## on the nose
 
 <i>phr.</i> of a person or thing, objectionable, having gone too far [AND distasteful 1941] (cf. on the cuff, snorer, snout)

>  You're on the nose, aren't you, pinching a man's letter to his popsie. <i>(1946,Johnny Enzed in Italy,9</i>)

>  <b>HELMER</b> 'Aren't you going to look for what's causing the stink?'... `It is a bit on the nose, isn't it?' Crump observed. <i>(1964,Stag Party,48</i>)

>  a bit on the nose: unfair; smelly <i>(Feb 1981,Avondale College Slang Words in Use</i>) [NOTES: Goldie Brown Collection]

>  <b>McGILL</b> nose, phr. a bit on the nose protest at unfairness; eg `Hey, no spitting, okay? Come on, that's a bit on the nose. Fair dos, eh?' NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,77</i>)

 <i>phr.</i> out of favour, neglected; penniless [ca1951 Wellington Nash's really on the outer with the Unions now.]

>  outer ... 5. on the outer. Austral. & N.Z. excluded or neglected. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,801</i>)

>  2 n. on the outer NZ & Austral. sl. neglected, unpopular, <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,532</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> outer phr. on the outer out of favour, or penniless; from the outer enclosure at a racecourse; eg `After Dick was seen in the big anti-apartheid march on telly, he was on the outer at the rugby club.' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,80</i>)



## on the pig's back
 
 <i>phr.</i> successful, in a comfortable position, well off (in money, situation, etc.) cf. high on the hog [ca1945 He was on the pig's back for the rest of his life when his father left all that money.]

>  It's a hot line, give and take On the pig's back, a piece of cake, Keep your shirt on, - jump in the lake She'll be Jake, you're past your use-by date. <i>(Dec 2004,NZ Memories,p18</i>)

>  <b>COOZE</b> If one had the courage ... one would be on the pig's back <i>(1946,Ten Bob Each Way,27</i>)

>  <b>DAVIN</b> Otherwise I'd have been all right, on the pig's back as they say over here. <i>(1959,No Remittance,28</i>)

>  <b>WALLIS</b> He stuck with it right up till the end of the war and then aerial topdressing came in and they were on the pig's back. <i>(1963,Point of Origin,11</i>)

>  and why should they doubt it as sure to last forever?/ their province astride the colony's pig's back? <i>(Aug 1986,Islands,37/51</i>) [NOTES: (Ireland)]

>  <i>on the pig's back NZ & Austral. sl.</i> in a fortunate position. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,568</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 366]]

>  <b>LELAND</b> <i>on the pig's back:</i> perhaps because pork is New Zealand's most expensive domestic meat, to `live on the pig's back' is to LIVE very WELL indeed. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,72</i>)

>  If one-third of the yarns that are now in circulation about the size and quantity of the mica deposit in this district, have any foundation in fact the Greymouth syndicates are on the pig's back. <i>(Aug 26, 1890,West Coast Times,2</i>)

## on the turps

## on the vag
 
 <i>phr.</i> on the road, on the swag (seeking work) [on the vag, vag] (See [vag](../V#vag))

>  I've shivered and starved in vain,/And been honest for months in rag,/So if I'm convicted again,/I think it won't be on the vag. <i>(Sep 3, 1887,Tuapeka Times,1</i>)

>  A middle-aged married woman named Mary Burgoyne, who was found with the men in a drunken condition in the railway carriage was charged with drunkenness, with being a rogue and "on the vag.," and with puncturing her prohibition order. <i>(Nov 27, 1920,NZ Truth,6</i>)

## on the wallaby
 
 <i>phr.</i> on the swag (cf. wallaby track; wallaby trot) OED <i>wallaby-track,</i> and hence <i>on the wallaby</i>: Aust: wandering about on foot: 1869; AC 1861; Morris [orig. and usually Australian, and mainly station and outdoors usage in NZ]

>  <b>BODELL</b> He had the appearance of a fighting man or a dog fancier a brutish countenance and he had often been on the Wallaby (or tramp) about the various Gold Fields and Squatters Stations. [ed fn] A `dog fancier' was a receiver of stolen dogs who would return them to their owners for a fee. <i>(1890,Soldier's View of Empire, A,141,1982 ed.</i>)

>  <b>McHUTCHESON</b> He determined to see ... the interior of the country ..., and to that end he spent his first eighteen months or two years `on the wallaby' ... `humping his swag' in true Colonial fashion. <i>(1892,Camp-Life in Fiordland,10  [?101]</i>)

>  <b>WRIGHT</b> (`Swagger') For them that's on the wallaby there's little to be had. <i>(1897,Station Ballads,30</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> .. an' he'd be d-----d if he wad hump ony mair whusky to Swinborough on Shanks's powny. ... He micht juist as weel be on the wallaby amang the stations again - ... <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,233</i>)

>  Therefore, Woollcombe, get thee hence; go home thou wowser on the wallaby; hump bluey, carry Matilda, pad the hoof, thou parsonical howler, thou Wowseristic whaler Woollcombe. <i>(Nov 6, 1909,NZ Truth,228/1/3</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> [cf P (B1942) 2. on an urban drinking-bout: Aust: ca1890-1910 3. penniless: Aust: 20c. the Red Funnel citation is probably mainly Australian in reference]

>  [From an article`On the Wallaby' by Helen F. Hunt about wallaby-hunting in Australia being what gum-digging is in NZ] As the hunter was of necessity [?out] a good deal at night, `on the wallaby' came by degrees to mean - according to the natural tendency of language to degenerate, when associated with low ideas - `making a night of it', having a `spree', being drunk; ... But to the rest of us `on the wallaby' means taking off the harness, and letting ourselves run free a bit; putting aside the mask and coming down from the stage; loosening the grip of fashion and returning for a while to the simplicity of life ... We crave to go somewhere `on the wallaby'. <i>(1906,Red Funnel,3/147</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [used in Aus.] [itinerant worker] looking for work.

>  <b>MARTIN</b> These men were probably simply 'on the wallaby' in search of work. <i>(1990,Forgotten Worker, The,29</i>)

>  I never saw half as many men 'on the wallaby' at this time of year before. <i>(Feb 21, 1880,Otago Witness,5</i>)

>  <b>ELKINGTON</b> I wanted to go on the wallaby, as I had read so much about it. <i>(1906,Adrift in NZ,24</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> The swaggers who moved around on the roads and tracks in search of work were described as being 'on the wallaby.' <i>(1990,Forgotten Worker, The,33</i>)



## on the weekend
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  I get caught out on small tricks of language. I'll say <i>on</i> the weekend, which is perfectly acceptable New Zealand usage, but in England it's <i>at, over</i> or <i>during</i> the weekend, but never <i>on</i>. <i>(Jul 16, 2000,Sunday Star Times,F2</i>)



## on the/via hobnail express
 
 <i>phr.</i> on foot

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Having stowed all gear away and put a few personal effects in our packs, as well as our rifles, we set off via hobnail express down the road. We had walked from seven to eight miles when our friend returned to take us home. <i>(1952,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,173</i>)

>  <b>PINNEY</b> Into every corner the shepherds had to go on the hobnail express, often under conditions where a slip meant death ... <i>(1981,Early Northern Otago Runs,38</i>)



## on thirds
 
 <i>n.</i> a system of flock-sharing where an owner put his sheep into the care of a station owner who received a third of the wool and lambs in lieu of rent for grazing



 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) a sheep-grazing arrangement where a grazier is allotted a proportion of the wool and offspring of the flock. [rural, usually sheep farming; occas thirds]

>  <b>PAUL</b> Many of our settlers have flocks of sheep placed out 'on thirds' as it is called ... <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,21</i>)

>  Mr. Brook will be happy to take (Cattle) on Thirds, or other terms. <i>(Mar 31, 1843,New Zealand Colonist & Port Nicholson Advertiser,1</i>)

>  WANTED to Let 1400 acres, on Castle Rock Run. Terms liberal - small quantity of stock (sheep if preferred), will be given on thirds. <i>(Feb 11, 1867,Southland Times,1</i>)

>  Reginald Alexander, farmer and stock dealer, Hikutaia, said he was farming on thirds with his father and a soldier brother (returned). <i>(Feb 21, 1917,Ohinemuri Gazette,2</i>)

>  Is sharemilker on thirds entitled to his proportion on the gross or on the net butter-fat profit? <i>(Jan 6, 1923,NZ Truth,12</i>)

>  Stafford renewed his offer to me of looking after his sheep on thirds, ... <i>(Aug 23, 1849,Torlesse Papers,99,1958 ed.</i>)

>  <b>GODLEY</b> It is customary here, & sometimes very profitable, to have sheep 'on thirds'; which means to send them to the station, & allow the owner of it half the wool, & 1/3d. of the increase for the trouble & expense of looking after them - the owner receives half the wool, & 2/3ds. of the increase, as profit on his capital. <i>(Jul 8, 1850,Letter</i>) [NOTES: 1850 July 8 Godley letter in 1971 Speeches and Documents 28]

>  <b>ROCHFORT</b> A settler will generally take sheep on thirds; that is, he takes the sheep on his run, and attends to them, finding labour: the party to whom the sheep belong gives him annually one-third of the increase and half the wool, besides paying for material for dressing in case of scab, &c. <i>(1853,Adventures of a Surveyor,27</i>)

>  <b>ADAMS</b> or place his sheep `on thirds', as it is called; that is to say, may enter into an arrangement with the owner of some large sheep farm, to receive a certain proportion of the wool and of the increase, in lieu of rent. <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,68</i>) [NOTES: (Griffiths Collection)]

>  ... sheep on thirds <i>(1854,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,21</i>) [NOTES: 1854 Paul Canterbury Settlement 21]

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> His best plan is to get acquainted with some practical flock-master in the neighbourhood, and to make the arrangement of `thirds'. <i>(1857,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/398</i>)

>  <b>JOLLIE</b> Watt, who was starting a sheep-farm in the Wairau Valley [before 1850 was] on `Thirds' for three years. This meant one third of the wool and lambs each year to Watt and I have two thirds. <i>(1878,Reminiscences,17</i>)

>  <i>`Thirds.' </i>- All endowment lands leased under the [Land] Act are subject to the `thirds' clause, which gives one-third of the rent to the local bodies for roads for access. <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,188</i>)

>  <i>(1933,Press, The</i>) [NOTES: Acland [check???]]

>  <b>AYSON</b> Mr. Shaw took on what was called thirds at lamb marking time, and put his own earmark on every third lamb [of Brugh's flock running with his]. <i>(1937,Pioneering in Otago,94</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Johnny Jones saw that Trotter was a reliable man and gave him a start [in the 1850s] with a flock of 1000 sheep on thirds - that is to say, Jones got the wool and two-thirds of the lambs while Trotter got one-third for his work. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,100</i>)

>  <b>ADAMS</b> .. and as his means may permit, either rent and stock a large run, or place his sheep 'on thirds' as it is called. <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,68</i>)

>  Mr Fannin is prepared to receive sheep on thirds on his run, Rua Taniwha plains, Ahuriri. <i>(Feb 20, 1858,Hawke's Bay Herald,1</i>)

>  Wanted: Sheep on thirds. Apply to WR Ferguson, Clive. <i>(May 27, 1859,Hawke's Bay Herald,1</i>)

>  <b>BALFOUR</b> Under this arrangement, a landless man, who had enough capital to buy sheep, could graze them on another man's property. Usually the grazier took one-half of the annual wool clip of the 'terms' sheep and one-third of the total increase at the end of the period. J D Ormond before his purchase of Wallingford, grazed sheep 'on terms' or 'thirds' on the property of Richard Collins, a Wairarapa sheepfarmer. <i>(1885,Balfour Diaries,75</i>)



## on with
 
 <i>phr.</i> keen on, amorously involved with, ON A FUSS WITH [AND 1903] -->BE, GET ON WITH, to have a crush on; also as a negative express. not to go much on, not to care much for.

## on-cue feeding
 
 <i>n.</i> alternative term for demand feeding

>  Another term currently hot on the baby jargon scene is "on-cue feeding." Whereas demand feeding ran along the lines of feeding the baby up to 94 times a day (but hey, who's counting?), on cue feeding runs along the lines of feeding the baby up to 94 times a day. but in a much more co-operative cued-on sort of way. <i>(Jul 29, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),38</i>)

## on-off grazing
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a form of rotational grazing [where stock are offered pasture or crop for only three to four hours each day and are fed on a wintering-pad or sacrifice paddock for the other twenty hours.]

>  Using humps and hollows, on-off grazing, being attuned to the conditions and seeing problems before you get to them are the ways the O'Briens cope with the wet. <i>(Sep 4, 1997,NZ Farmer,24</i>)

>  If you find that you cannot get density in your sward .. you are thrown back to on-off grazing systems... <i>(Aug 1972,NZ Dairy Exporter,9</i>)

>  <b>WHITE & HODGSON</b> On/off grazing is where animals are offered their daily pasture allowance (three to four hours' grazing) and then removed from the pasture to a hard stand-off area, wintering pad or sacrifice area. <i>(1999,NZ Pasture and Crop Science,156</i>)



## on-refer
 
 <i>v.</i> refer on

>  Others we can on-refer. <i>(Sep 26, 2000,Radio NZ / National Radio,Merepeka Raukawa-Tait on</i>)



## on/off the hill
 
 <i>phr.</i> the hill, the high-country mustering beat; ?in gen. the high country itself; be on the hill, to be out mustering; be off the hill, to be finished mustering. [(musterers')]

>  No shepherd or musterer speaks of mountains. He may be mustering the side of Mount Cook, but he is, still on the h. (C.) <i>(Oct 28, 1933,Press, The,17</i>)

>  I was two beats from the top, and there was shingle all the way. It slid continuously, and all the time I had to bear up hill, to keep a level track. However, we got off `the hill' quite early, put the sheep across Cat Creek at Tarndale, and raced for the homestead. <i>Ibid.</i> 29 But we got our mail, and were glad to be for a day or so `off the hill'. <i>Ibid.</i> 46 Dogs often start work down country at about nine months - sometimes under. That is too young to start a dog on the hill. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,18</i>)

>  <b>SHARPE</b> That is typical of what a high-country man calls a good day on the hill. <i>(1962,Country Occasions,123</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Mustering Stick: When on the hill, high-country musterers carry a long walking-stick. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,188</i>)

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> Watty took my swag and I went up to meet the musterers as they came off the hill. <i>(1968,Remembered Trails,37</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Old Jack Halliday often used to talk of his days `on the hill'. I used to lap up these stories of mustering and droving ... <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,20</i>)

>  ...Billy Dunbar, reckoned by many as the greatest walker ever to go on the hill;... <i>(1975,Sixty Thousand on the Hoof</i>)



## once a day
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [dairying] milking cows once daily before drying off or to restrict milking to once daily for animal husbandry reasons.

>  Traditionally the shift to once a day is generally used as a signal to the herd that drying off time is not too far off. The expectation is that once a day will initiate a reduction in milk production. However, there is also the obvious positive side to once a day. Once a day will increase cow condition (or decrease condition loss.) <i>(Apr 4, 2001,Fencepost.com,Expert Farming</i>)

>  Cow condition is no longer a concern under his once-a-day milking regime. When we went to once-a-day milking they very quickly picked up condition. <i>(Jul 26, 2002,Dominion,C7</i>)

>  Mrs Tong said that because the once-a-day approach required a slightly bigger herd, it did take longer to milk ...Our once-a-day cows were at least half a condition score above the twice-a-day cows throughout the year, so they are heavier. [once-a-day] <i>(Sep 20, 2002,Taranaki Daily News,Farming Supplement 5</i>)

## oncer
 
 <i>n.</i> something done once only, once a lifetime (cf ONEFER GOFER)

>  Buying engagement rings is a oncer - a once a lifetime thrill. <i>(Jan 28, 1953,2ZB</i>)

>  ONCER This may be purely local ... A oncer is a man (or purchaser) who is not likely to have occasion to buy the firm's goods more than once a lifetime so can be fobbed off with poor quality stuff. My guess is that it could also have been used on the West Coast (South Island) ... (timber trade.) <i>(Nov 22, 1957,Letter</i>) [NOTES: Muriel Dalton letter (Upper Hutt)]

>  <b>SLATTER</b> You've got to win a oncer game... I want to tell you about a oncer game long ago. ... [133] he fed it out perfectly and three backs handled it and we scored in the corner and won the game. that was a oncer game and it was Tank Tarrant that won it. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,132</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> oncer: something that is NOT going TO BE REPEATED. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,73</i>)

## one out of the box

>  As old Te Whiti, the Parihaka Maori Prophet said in 1881, "the potato is cooked," so I say Stewart is down and out, without a leg to stand on. <i>(Aug 21, 1920,NZ Truth,5</i>)

## one-man farm
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a farm managed by one worker full-time for most of the year, with occasional seasonal help.

>  Good one-man farms.. <i>(Mar 15, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,141</i>)

>  On one-man farms where an area of grain is grown but increasing difficulty is found in obtaining labour to sew bags, the bulk header makes one-man harvesting possible. <i>(Nov 1963,NZ Journal of Agriculture,375</i>)

>  Half share-milking on a one-man farm with a non-strip herd and a tractor is not such a hard job for a strong man. <i>(Oct 2, 1950,NZ Dairy Exporter,89</i>)

>  <b>TAIT</b> The shed is equipped with automatic counters, thus avoiding the necessity for counting out pens - a big help on a 'one-man' farm. <i>(1961,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol III,155</i>)

>  <b>MILLS ET AL</b> Successfully handling sheep through the race on a one-man farm should next occupy our attention. <i>(1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,76</i>)

>  One-family or one-man farms are still well represented. <i>(1977,NZ Dairy Industry Dairy Board,41</i>)



## one-man saw

## one-man unit
 
 <i>n.</i> a farm which can be run effectively by one farmer working full-time

>  <b>Army Education</b> It is important to note that the farms discussed in Chapter I to VI are 'one-man-units,' this type of farm having been used as it is most generally appropriate to the requirements of the returned servicemen with limited capital.<i>Ibid</i>28 A one man unit of this type will carry six hundred to a thousand ewes according to the suitability of the country for the breeding of replacements. <i>(1945,How to Start Farming,Preface</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a farm that can be run effectively by one farmer working full-time, with occasional seasonal help.

>  <b>ARMY EDUCATION</b> It is important to note that the farms discussed in Chapter I to VI are 'one-man-units' ... <i>(1945,How to Start Farming,Preface</i>)

>  <b>FEARON</b> Many small blocks of land have been amalgamated into larger more economic units. Several properties which used to employ staff are now one man units. <i>(1980,Te Wharau,xv</i>)

>  <b>ARMY EDUCATION</b> A one man unit of this type will carry six hundred to a thousand ewes according to the suitability of the country for the breeding of replacements. <i>(1945,How to Start Farming,28</i>)

>  Apart from the odd casual labour this farm has been worked as a one-man unit. <i>(Jan 1965,NZ Journal of Agriculture,45</i>)

## one-way
 
 <i>adj.</i> [Jim writes : Dictionaries define <i>one-way</i> in relation to traffic as allowing travel in one direction only. This does not cover the common NZ one-way bridge, across which a vehicle may travel in either direction unless there is opposing traffic. That is, it can be crossed only one way at a time. Although the distinction is a fine one, it seems real. Possibly the term <i>one-way bridge</i> is itself distinctive NZE. The latest NZ Road Code uses the term <i>one-lane bridge</i>.]

## Onehunga weed
 
 <i>n.</i> a lawn weed noted for its sharp-spined seeds in early summer. (Soliva sessilis), or <i>Hydrocotyle</i> sp. ? =JO JO a prickly lawn-grass substitute

>  There on the lawn was a wild half acre of hyperactive clover, buttercups, onehunga weed, tree rocks, pieces of ornamental pond, bicycle parts, Action Man limbs and lost tools. <i>(Dec 28, 2001,Evening Post,15</i>)

>  <b>HILGENDORF & CALDER</b> ONEHUNGA WEED (Soliva sessilis and S. valdiviana), also called Jo Jo weed, is a small spreading weed with trailing stems and much divided leaves. ... The weed was introducedfrom Chile and is now common in waste places and grasslands chiefly in the northern half of the North Island, but also in Nelson and Marlborough. <i>(1967,Weeds of New Zealand,192,nf 1926 ed.</i>)

>  Onehunga weed [s] [4 Soliva spp.] <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,54</i>)

>  a lawn weed noted for its sharp-spined seeds in early summer. <i>(1979,Heinemann NZ Dictionary,748</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> ONEHUNGA WEED (<i>Soliva</i> spp.). Several similar South American species go by this name. ... The common name is derived from the Auckland suburb where the weed came under early notice. Onehunga weed is common in many districts of the North Island and is spreading fast in the South Island,. . . <i>(1980,Weeds of Crops,69</i>)

>  This is the last chance this year to prevent seed setting on Onehunga weed (prickle weed)... If moss is a problem, also pray. <i>(Nov 26, 1988,Listener (New Zealand Listener),13</i>) [NOTES: Original in Wairarapa Times-Age, 3/11/1988]

## ones
 
 <i>n.</i> [(child marble players')]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Some of the typical terms used in marbles referred to the act of shooting and the movement of the marbles. Those related to ways of shooting marbles were cribbing, firsts, fudging, funks, funnicks, funnigans, flunks, forces, knee-high, knuckle-down-stiff, knuckles, and lasts. Termsconnected with the movement of the marbles were: cannons, clears, dead-stick, double-hits, fires, funs, gees, haystacks, in smug, keeps, kills, kisses, lay-ups, manyes, ones, pink, ringer, rolls, scat, screws, skinned, skun, spiders, sported-off, stays, stops, tracks, throughs. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,175</i>)



## onewoa
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>POLACK</b> ... a dark-gray and black-coloured granite, termed onewoa by the natives was made use of in its stead, or the green talc, poeanamu[a2-acute] and pieces of red jasper chipped off adroitly, resembling in shape our common gun-flints. <i>(1840,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/194</i>)



## ongaonga
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Really bad onga-onga stings provoke feverish sickness. <i>(Sep 9, 1924,Otago Witness,6</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a tree nettle

>  <b>LEE & KANDALL</b> O'nga |onga. A certain shrub. <i>(1820,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,142</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> This morning I was showed a real stinging nettle which the natives assured me as it grew turned into a tree & loses its stinging quality the onga onga, which though they affirmed I confess I very much doubt. <i>(Nov 23, 1843,Journal,18</i>)

>  <b>HOOKER</b> <i>Urtica ferox</i>, Forst. ... Nat. name, `Onga Onga', <i>Col. </i> <i>(1853,Flora NZ,I Flowering Plants 225</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> <i>Ongaonga</i>, sea nettle, a mollusca. <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui,415</i>)

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Onga-onga, <i>Col[enso]. Urtica ferox</i>, and others. <i>(1867,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766</i>)

>  Ongaonga. Urtica incisa, Poiret ... Ongaonga. Urtica ferox, Forst. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/48</i>) [NOTES: Buchanan Botany Otago]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> [141] <i>Urtica ferox (The Fierce Nettle) </i>... Maori name, Onga-onga. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,13,1940 ed.</i>)

>  That plant, from which all of us suffered for some days, was the native stinging nettle, the ongaonga, which botanists have christened urtica ferox. <i>(1916,Bush Explorers,7</i>)

>  <i>ongaonga</i>, a plant (Urtica ferox), nettle... <i>onga-onga</i>, a plant (Urtica ferox), nettle, when used as a place-name Ongaonga it usually has other meanings; a houhere (ribbon-wood) with to in the place-name; ... <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/154</i>) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  <i>ongaonga [=] tree nettle [s] </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,54</i>)

>  <b>MOORE & IRWIN</b> <i>URTICA FEROX, ongaonga.</i> The tree nettle can form great thickets taller than a man. Pale stinging hairs on leaves and stems are poisonous, a real danger to horses and dogs. <i>(1978,Oxford Book of NZ Plants,82</i>)

>  It is possible to proceed ..., but beware of the native tree nettle, ongaonga! Here [at Nugget Point, South Otago] is the habitat of the world's rarest penguin: the yellow-eyed penguin. <i>(1988,Pacific Way,48</i>)

>  Urtica ferox Ongonga <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/123</i>) [NOTES: TrNZI(Armstrong Botany Canterbury]

>  The <i>ongaonga</i> (nettle shrub) has no leaves now (August). <i>(1903,TrNZI,35/111</i>)

>  Nettles are well known for this [sc. stinging] and the most dangerous is the native tree nettle or ongaonga. <i>(Jun 1989,NZ Gardener,11</i>)

>  <b>CHAPPLE</b> [I] pushed my way along a rough trail, through bush growth and beds of stinging ongaonga in the gullies. <i>(2002,Te Araroa: the New Zealand Trail,191</i>)

>  Only gorse, Darwin's barberry and ongaonga were doing well. <i>(2000,Salient,No. 19 : 19</i>)

>  The onga-onga is a tall shrub armed with stinging hairs. <i>(Sep 9, 1924,Otago Witness,6</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [Ma.] nettle (Urtica ferox).

>  <b>DAVIS</b> Our hobnails slithered on the smooth mud, spiked ongaonga whipped across our faces, and once my uncle lost his balance altogether. <i>(1962,Mutton on the Menu,157</i>)

>  <b>MOORE & IRWIN</b> Urtica ferox, ongaonga. The tree nettle can form great thickets taller than a man .. a real danger to horses and dogs. <i>(1978,Oxford Book of NZ Plants,82</i>)

>  Here we found, growing plentifully, that most formidable of the whole New Zealand flora - the <i>Urtica ferox</i>, the tree nettle, or ongaonga of the Maoris, an innocent-looking plant, with dark green leaves, covered with numerous white spines, a slight touch from which will give cause to remember the plant for many days. <i>(Jan 13, 1872,Otago Witness,6</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>COWAN</b> .. the white and Maori explorers experimented with such onga-onga poison antidotes as their bushcraft suggested. <i>(1916,Bush Explorers,8</i>)

## onion
 
 <i>n.</i> 1. a wild variety of onion with masses of attractive white flowers. 2. for various plants: see also Maori onion; ONION PLANT, FLOWER, WEED; GRASS; a local weed also called (WILD) GARLIC q.v. 1. NATIVE ONION (see also MAORI ONION) cit. at Native Leek. See Leek. 2. WILD ONION spp. -->[AND =onion weed 1864, 1901 Bulbinera sp] WILD GARLIC, =CROW GARLIC =ONION FLOWER,=ONION WEED etc. (See [Maori onion](../M#maori-onion))

>  Captain Wellby, a young cavalry officer, recently spent a furlough in trudging 2000 miles through Tibet, from Leb to Pekin. For 14 weeks he and his party did not meet a single human being, and rarely saw any vegetation higher than a wild onion. <i>(Dec 15, 1898,Otago Witness,58</i>)

>  The specimen you send is wild onion, sweet scented garlic, (<i>allium fragrans</i>). <i>(Oct 3, 1899,Hawera & Normanby Star,4</i>)

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> WILD ONION (<i>Allium vineale</i>), is also called wild garlic and crow garlic ... [It is a bulbous perennial with tall cylindrical stem, bearing at its apex a cluster of white to reddish flowers ... ] <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,55,1967 ed.</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> <i>Wild onion</i>, Allium <i>spp.</i> ...wild garlic (<i>Allium vineale</i>). <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/593</i>)

>  <i>wild onion [=] three-cornered garlic [s] ... wild onion [s] Allium vineale L. </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,54, 86</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> WILD ONION (<i>Allium vineale</i>) European perennial, ... a weed on a few farms in northern New Zealand only. <i>(1981,Weeds of Lawns,133</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> [? Australian bikies only)]

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> Tilt from a bottle. Bucko said, `Let's move - / There's gonna be an onion up the beach ...'/ His voice like a scratched match, `You wanna watch?'/ `Onion' she knew to be a bikie term/ For when one of the girls took several men/ In frolic - or by force - the onion being/ A vegetable with many layers. <i>(1972,Onion</i>)

## onion weed
 
 <i>n.</i> onion plant, onion weed -->also called onion flower,wild (three-cornered) garlic, wild onion a wild variety of onion with masses of attractive white flowers. <i>Allium triquetrum</i>, see also GARLIC, THREE-CORNERED, =WILD ONION [AND Asphodelus fistulosus (introduced) 1909; Nothoscordum inodorum f. S. Amer; Bulbiul family see WILD ONION] (See [also Maori onion](../A#also-maori-onion))

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,294</i>)

## onkus
 
 <i>adj.</i> good [(obsolescent)]

>  But `Critic' asks the said gentleman to submit the following sentence to any school boy or a semi-intelligent adult: `The Opposition are whipping the cat because they've got Buckley'schance of pulling the leg of the farmer with an onkus [sic] freehold lurk.' <i>(Jun 11, 1910,NZ Truth,259/1/3</i>)

>  New Zealanders have their curiously sounding slang. ... `ongkus' (good), ... <i>(Apr 2, 1938,Press, The,18</i>) [NOTES: McNab NZ Slang]



 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Onkus. (Of food) good; (of a place) passable: New Zealanders': from ca. 1914, chiefly among the soldiers. <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,589</i>)

>  onkus adj. (slang) bad, unpromising, no good [opposite of dinkum (q.v.)]. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621</i>)

>  onkus ... adj. (Sl.) bad, unpraising, no good (opposite of dinkum q.v.). <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>BRUNO</b> Now Auld Jock and Bluey, having successfully partnered a crown-and-anchor board in their Naafi, and having `done up' the feloose A.W.L. in Cairo Y.M.C.A.'s, had arrived back at Maadi decidedly `onkus' <i>(1944,Desert Daze,55</i>)



 
<b>4</b> <i>n.</i> I have heard (before 1939?) the expression be on one's onkus = `be on the bones of one's arse, broke'; or `be one's last legs' I had always thought of the word as `honkers', probably arbitrarily seeing in it `hunkers' or `haunches', euphemism for `arse', as the concrete expressions were `get up off your onkus', `get up off you behind (and start working etc',) ... `sitting around on your onkus'. [....:.]. However, this meaning is not otherwise known.

## ons

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Action terms related to the movements of the marbles are, Throughs and No Throughs, Tracks and Clears, Stops and Rolls, Lay-Up, Manyies, Kiss, Ons, Gees, Haystacks, Stays, Spiders Can't Cross a River etc. Action Terms related to taking an opponent's marbles are Fires, Double-Hits and Forces. <i>(1953,Unorganized Games,2/769</i>)

## oonst
 
 <i>n.</i> electronic dance music? [An Oonst Oonst Oonst That Could Not Be Heard: The Secret History of Electronic Music, techno was actually invented in 1962 by Herman Poltswalski, a shoe salesman and dedicated electronics audio hobbyist from Eau Claire, Wisconsin. He called the beat-driven music that he coaxed from his home-made synthesizers and drum machines "indestructible polka" despite the fact that it generally followed a 4/4 beat and exceeded the speed of the fastest polkas by about 30 bpm. (From www.livejournal.com]

## opah
 
 <i>n.</i> moonfish

>  <i>Lampris Pelagicus</i> ... Opah. <i>(1911,Records of the Canterbury Museum,1/186</i>) [NOTES: Waite]

>  <i> On the Occurrence of the Opah, or Moonfish, in New Zealand Waters</i> ... The only record I can find of the occurrence of this rare pelagic fish ... is that by Hector in 1884, who states that a specimen of <i>Lampris luna</i> Risso was washed ashore ... near ... River Manawatu <i>(1922,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/316</i>) [NOTES: Bentham]

>  This handsome species, the Opah or Moon Fish, is known to the Maoris as Aro kura. ... plate 16 <i>Lampris guttatus</i> Brunnich: The Opah, Moon Fish, or Red Fish. <i>(1929,TrNZI,61/146</i>) [NOTES: Young]

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>MOONFISH</i> (Opah) <i>Lampris guttatus</i> The moonfish is a huge, almost disc-shaped fish ranging in size from 1m to over 1.8m. ... It is interesting to note that the Maoris considered these fishes good eating ... <i>(1982,Collins Guide,187,1984 ed.</i>)

## opalfish
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <i>Hemerocoetes acanthorhynchus </i>(Forster). Opal-fish. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/415</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> OPAL-FISH <i>Hemerocoetes monopterygius</i> (Bloch and Schneider) ... Both anglers and commercial fishermen of Port Chalmers knew this fish and had bestowed the common name ofOpal-fish prior to my having seen one. The opalescent display of colours left no doubt in my mind as to the appropriateness of the common name. The abovementioned anglers and fishermen had various names to distinguish this fish from Opal-fish - which are as follows: Bluey, Blue-cap, Blue-Bonnet, which I have used. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,316,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Opal Fish <i>Hemerocoetes monopterygius</i> (Bloch and Schn.) (Syn. <i>Callionymus acanthorhynchus </i>Bloch and Schn., <i>Hemerocoetes microps</i> Waite) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,410</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> OPALFISH ... Other names: <i>Hemerocoetes acanthorhynchus </i>; sandfish. <i>(1986,Sea Anglers' Guide,282</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> OPALFISH [<i>Hemerocoetes acanthorhynchus </i>] ... It is sometimes called sandfish. <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,46</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> Family TRICHONOTIDAE is the only family confined to New Zealand waters. The opal fish and blue bonnet are two handsome and colourful species of <i>Hemerocoetes</i>... <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,226</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> OPALFISH The exotically-coloured Opalfish is a fairly common offshore species being found in waters down to about 600 feet all around our coasts. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,170</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> OPALFISH <i>Hemerocoetes monopterygius</i> (<i>Hemerocoetes acanthorhynchus </i>) ... The opalfish is a slender round-bodied fish that grows to a maximum length of about 25cm. ... There are irridescent blue and orange markings on the head and flanks ... <i>(1982,Collins Guide,270,1984 ed.</i>)

>  [species name] opalfish .. [scientific name] <i>Hemerocoetes</i> sp. .. [other name(s)] -- <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Opalfishes </i>Family Percophidae. There are several New Zealand species, one of which is common in harbours and over the shelf. The opalfish, <i>Hemerocoetes monopterygius</i>, is so named from its irridescent striped and banded colouring of blue, orange... <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,116</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] opalfish 1789-95 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

## open
 
 <i>v.</i>

>  Those of Mokena's family who have signified their willingness to dispose of their share in the block say the akuhata's refusal to sign will not deter them from having the field thrown open for gold mining purposes and that the question of proclaiming the field is now in the hands of the authorities. <i>(Nov 18, 1880,Bay of Plenty Times,3</i>)

>  Within 12 months from date there should be a large area of auriferous land thrown open for gold mining, as it is anticipated that by then the Kelly's Terrace tail-race, now being driven under Government subsidy, will be completed. <i>(Sep 29, 1898,Otago Witness,18</i>)

>  In reference to opening the lands covered by the Barewood pastoral lease for mining purposes, the Prime Minister to-day stated that the matter had been considered by the Mines Department, when it was decided that it was not expedient to proclaim the land in question open for gold mining. <i>(Sep 16, 1908,Otago Witness,22</i>)

>  Some ...[sic] who wished to stop, intend proceeding to Auckland as they are frightened of bush land, and the block containing fern, which will be next opened, is hardly likely to be in the market for 6 months to come. <i>(Sep 24, 1853,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/131,1960 ed.</i>)

>  About the same time several gullies were opened in the Umbrella Ranges; and on the banks of the Waikaia ... [9] a permanent goldfield ... was also discovered. <i>(1887,Handbook NZ Mines,8</i>) [NOTES: 1863 V. Pyke's Report in 1887 Handbook NZ Mines]

>  He has sent a message to the King for assistance to drive off surveyors, as the Ngatimaru have openly declared their intention of throwing the block open for gold mining when surveyed. <i>(Sep 30, 1873,Star, The,2</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i> [1832 @open grassy forest' open aj2 of land, available for settlement: to throw open land 1830]]



 
 <i>adj.</i> (of land) with scattered trees without undergrowth, etc. 1829

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The prices varied according to the character of the land, whether clear or open, or hilly and wooded. Thus, for fifty-acre sections, the price [for cutting] ranged from 8d. to 1s.9d. per acre, ... <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,78</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>adj.</i> (?South Island) of land, without obstruction to title sc. not taken up by owners

>  <b>SCOTTER</b> In April, 1853, six months after his arrival in New Zealand, he bought 820 sheep in Nelson and drove them to Canterbury in search of land and it was only after he saw that all the open country was taken up ... that he ... decided to cross the Waitaki River. <i>Ibid.</i> 15 Yet, until the land was fenced stock had to be herded. There was also the unsold land open for grazing. Each year, therefore, those who wished to graze stock on this open country applied for a depasturing licence - .. <i>(1948,Run, Estate and Farm,1</i>)



## Open Bay Islands gecko
 
 <i>n.</i> a small brown gecko known only from Taumaka Island in the Open Bay Islands, south Westland. [<i>Hoplodactylus "Open Bay Islands gecko"</i>]

>  Other notable faunainclude the endemic Open Bay Islands leech and Open Bay Islands skink (<i>Oligosoma</i> "Open Bay Islands"), Open Bay Islands gecko (<i>Hoplodactylus</i> "Open Bay Islands"), and large numbers of fur seals (<i>Arctocephalus forsteri</i>). <i>(Sep 1997,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.doc.govt.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/Sfc057.pdf]

>  Taumaka is the only known location for three critically endangered species: the Open Bay Islands leech, the Open Bay Islands gecko, and the Open Bay Islands skink . . . <i>(Apr 2007,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.westmarine.org.nz/documents/WCMarineandCoastalEnvironment.pdf]



## Open Bay Islands skink
 
 <i>n.</i> Known only from a small population on Taumaka Island in the Open Bay Islands, South Westland the Open Bay Islands skink is slender, dark brown, with relatively large feet. [<i>Oligasoma taumakae</i> aka Taumaka skink.]

>  <b>Tony Whitaker and John Lyall</b> An undescribed taxon with unresolved relationships. .. This clearly requires furtherinvestigation as it would be hard to find two more disparate species withinOligosoma than the Open Bay Islands skink and the scree skink.not just inform, but also in habitat and behaviour. <i>(May 2004,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://doc.govt.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/WClizards.pdf Conservation of lizards in WestCoast/Tai Poutini ConservancyTony Whitaker and John LyallPub by DOC Wellington May 2004]

>  <b>Miller, Daugherty</b> The allozyme data revealed the existence of three undescribed taxa, whichwe have labelled: O. "Big Bay", O. " Grey Valley" and O . " Open Bay Islands". O."Open Bay Islands" remains undescribed due to a lack of collected materialfrom this species. The other two species are being formally described in apaper to be submitted to the New Zealand Journal of Zoology. <i>(2000,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.gen.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/casn291.pdf Identification of genetic diversity Conservation Advisory Science Notes No. 291 ,Department of Conservation, Wellington.for lizard conservationMiller, C.; Daugherty, C.H., 2000.]

## open country
 
 <i>n.</i> land without any obstruction to movement or use; spec. without (or with very few) trees 1849 country not covered with bush, cf OPEN LAND cf OPEN BUSH

>  There is much open or fern country <i>(1841,NZ Journal,2/48/286</i>) [NOTES: (G. Cutfield)]

>  <b>W GANN & Co</b> In the ... South Island there is a large and magnificent area of `open country'. [F.N.] I include in this term all tracts and districts which are not true or dense forest districts. <i>(1864,Handbook of New Zealand,95</i>)

>  In the term `open' I include plains and hill-sides in the low-lands covered with grass, fern, tutu, .... spaniards, wild Irishman and snow grasses. <i>(1871,TrNZI,4/73</i>) [NOTES: Haast]

>  <b>KENNAWAY</b> After crossing the steep range s which I have names as forming a rugged barrier between the harbour and the open country, we struggled up ... to the banks of the river. <i>(1874,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,11</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> His father, who owned a large farm ... in the open country, some fifty miles nearer the coast, had ... started him in life on his own account here in the bush, by purchasing this property of about five hundred acres, ... <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,10</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> see quot. BUSH n. 4a] <i>Ibid.</i> open land [see quot BUSHLAND] <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,81</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> - [possible distinction. open downs 1848; open paddock 1929 open plain]

>  <b>WOODHOUSE</b> Down the slopes we rushed them [sc. cattle] hell for leather,/ Towards the open country and the yard. <i>(1950,Farm & Station Verse</i>) [NOTES: 1938 E.C. Studholme `Cattle Mustering' in 1950 Woodhouse Farm and Station Verse [p?]]



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) unfenced land, usually in the high country.

>  <b>LILICO</b> In training to run on the blind, open country without fences or landmarks of any kind should be chosen ... <i>(1919,Breeding and Training of Sheep Dogs,19</i>)

>  <b>HALL</b> The blocks of fenced paddocks would end eventually and the wethers would be in open country with no fences to keep them in. <i>(1987,Shepherd's Year,  A,27</i>)

>  In the early '70s in common with the custom of that time, Mr McLeish possessed a number of head of cattle which roamed freely over the open country. The Provincial Government passed a law that no one could allow cattle to run on the ridges, and Mr McLeish bought a 100 acre farm owned by Mr John Wilson at the Aparima estuary. <i>(Jul 17, 1928,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,1</i>) [NOTES: No fences.]

>  <b>TAIT</b> To avoid boxing mobs, the ewes and docked lambs were held in open country until dark. <i>(1956,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol I,51</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> ... they had to be held on a fenced block - until such time as there was a sufficiently permanent winter snow boundary to enable them to be turned out on open country with comparative safety. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,39</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) land available for selection.

>  <b>SCOTTER</b> In April 1853 .. he bought 820 sheep in Nelson and drove them to Canterbury in search of land and it was only after he saw that all the open country was taken up .. that he .. decided to cross the Waitaki River. <i>(1948,Run, Estate and Farm,7</i>)

>  <b>SCOTTER</b> Each year, therefore, those who wished to graze stock on this open country applied for a depasturing licence. <i>(1948,Run, Estate and Farm,15</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) land that is not close-hilled, but open to the elements.

>  <b>THOMSON</b> In open country such as the high snow grass tops above the bush line ... <i>(1964,Deer Shooting Days,102</i>)

>  <b>MCCONOCHIE</b> This build-up of hinds brought in during the rut a greater number of mature stags, with the result that animals of better type were to be found in the forest than on the open country of the snow tops. <i>(1966,You'll Learn No Harm from the Hills,65</i>)

>  High open country with plenty of wind also reduces the likelihood of excessive moisture and fleece rot. <i>(Dec 14, 1988,NZ Farmer,31</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) land with no tracks or roads.

>  <b>SALE</b> Only one house is in sight - about a kilometre away - and the rest is open country filled, it seems, with skylarks. <i>(1987,Country Diary,110</i>)

>  <b>PULLEN</b> We gave the other drovers a few days help when they had to go through open country, which can mean going through big stations or just overland away from the main roads. <i>(1996,On the Road Again,10</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) land that does not carry trees or forest.

>  .. the number of sheep or cattle that may be fed on 100 acres of open country. <i>(Mar 14, 1843,New Zealand Colonist & Port Nicholson Advertiser,2</i>)

## open shed
 
 <i>n.</i> [(shearers') check SHED (v. contract shearing)]

>  <b>BARKER</b> Brown and Wetherby's was an `open shed', where any shearers that came were taken on until there were hands enough. <i>(1872,Xmas Cake in Four Quarters, A,Ch. III</i>)

>  Open shed.-S. where the shearers are not engaged beforehand, but roll up on the advertised day and take their chance of employment. I think the custom has quite gone out of use now. Anama in the late 'nineties was the last station I can remember to have seen advertising its starting day. <i>(Jan 27, 1934,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Clayton was an `open shed', that is, the shearers were not engaged beforehand, but turned up and took their chance of a pen on the advertised starting day. <i>(1946,Early Canterbury Runs,166</i>)

>  <b>TEMPLE</b> It's the last year Ben Griam's an open shed. Those picked this year can book their place for next. <i>(1979,Stations,93</i>)

>  `Do you have open shed or contract shearing?' `I prefer the contract gang, but you have to help your local shearers; some wives are on the table, and we're often on the board too.' <i>(Oct 11, 1986,Radio NZ / National Radio</i>) [NOTES: National Radio Landline Farming Programme]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a woolshed where shearers arrive and compete for a stand without prior reservation.

>  <b>VANCE</b> Sometimes shearers had to ford flooded rivers so as to be at an 'open shed' station on time. 'Open shed' stations picked their shearers from those present when the shed opened; there were no prior engagements and no jobs for latecomers. <i>(1965,High Endeavour,51</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> open shed: as distinct from a closed shed, this was one in which shearers used to be chosen from amongst those who were there in time when the annual shearing began. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,II 2</i>)

>  Shearing will commence at Benmore Station, on Friday, 11th November. An Open Shed. <i>(Oct 28, 1876,North Otago Times,3</i>)

>  Shearers wanted at Messrs, A and W Birch's, Oruamatua, Kereru. To commence on the 9th December. Open shed. <i>(Dec 3, 1872,Hawke's Bay Herald,1</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Another feature, which is unusual in a property of this size, is that this is an open shed - the shearers, all local men, just seem to be avilable when required. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,154</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> C G Tripp's Orari Gorge station in South Canterbury ... was a well-known open shed. <i>(1990,Forgotten Worker, The,37</i>)

## open slather
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) a free-for-all, a free rein; anything goes [(ca1920 p.c. R. Mason (1952)) compare DNZE earliest citation 1959]

>  However, Wellington Central MP and ACT New Zealand justice spokesperson Stephen Franks says in practice council's [<i>sic</i>]will have to pass special new by-laws, and until they do it is "open slather". <i>(Jul 1, 2003,Independent Herald (Wellington),1</i>)

>  It was our fate to witness the misfortune of the sinking of the Triumph near by, and when next morning the Majestic followed it made us want still more to 'Up and at them'. However, things will not and cannot be always in our favor, but it [is] hard to grin and bear it at times, especially when your side loses without a fair go, or what we call 'an open slather'. <i>(Jul 15, 1915,Poverty Bay Herald,8</i>) [NOTES: from a letter from Gunner Pat Duncan at Gallipoli to a friend in Gisborne]

>  <b>Llewellyn, S.P.</b> The petrol was discovered about six miles down the Amyntaion-Servia road and as soon as the lorries were loaded our drivers turned their attention to the ration section of the dump. They were shown where the luxuries were stacked and told to help themselves. It was their first experience of what was to be known later as an 'open slather' and many of them were pink with excitement as they scrambled among the stacks, breaking open cases of tinned fruit, tinned vegetables, pickles, jam, tobacco, cigarettes. You just helped yourself. It was a childhood dream come true and for a quarter of an hour they behaved like greedy small boys. They stuffed their blouses with tins and threw cases on top of the loads of petrol.Often, in the days that lay ahead, we were to be wet and cold, sick and sorry, tired and frightened-never hungry. Empty and half-empty tins of peas and beans and pears and raspberries were to mark the passage of our convoys. It is probably fair to say that from the day of the first 'open slather' until the day we left Greece most of us over-ate. <i>(1949,Journey Towards Christmas,51</i>)

>  It is open season all the year round for the shooting of bank clerks. Even the wild ducks get a sanctuary sometimes, but with the underpaid and responsibility-laden banker it is an open slather all the year round. <i>(Dec 6, 1924,NZ Truth,5</i>) [NOTES: Truth's 'Melbourne Rep.' is reporting on a spate of bank shootings there]

>  The last round was a 'trimmer'. Both boys let themselves go, and made it an open slather. The crowd was on its feet throughout. <i>(Jan 3, 1929,NZ Truth,8</i>)

>  If the poachers know all they have to be is staunch ... then it's open slather. <i>(Feb 13, 2004,Dominion Post,A3</i>)

>  Open Competition (heading) In the final of the open 'slather' Dellaway gained the verdict over Parker on the towel trick [?]. <i>(Oct 11, 1920,Grey River Argus,3</i>)

>  I think his [a boxer named Torrie's] will be one of the best professional fights the Association has ever put on and it will be open slather from the gong. I am sure this event will be worth the price of admission. <i>(Mar 26, 1918,Grey River Argus,4</i>)

>  <b>ASHTON-WARNER</b> Aren't you supposed to have anything at all if her kids can't have it? Or are you supposed to give them open slather, baby and all? Right, left and center [sic] ? <i>(1964,Bell Call,77</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>open slather </i>unconstrained and often riotous gathering available to allcomers; possibly from Irish `slighe', access; eg `It's open slather, I hear, at the golf clubrooms this Saturday. Coming?' NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>) [NOTES: the suggested etymology is erroneous; see slather n.]

>  There was an acrimonious discussion, and reference to an "open slather." <i>(Sep 27, 1932,Evening Post,10</i>)

>  This bliss had not lasted more than a week, however, when we were informed that the "increased" charge of 4d had been disallowed by the Price Tribunal, and that the price would again be 3 1/2d, with open slather as to weight. <i>(Feb 24, 1944,Evening Post,4</i>)



## open the books
 
 <i>phr.</i> a mainly political catchphrase popularised by the 1984- Lange Labour Administration

>  [Caption] Finance Minister RuthRichardson opens the books. <i>(Oct 6, 1993,Dominion,2</i>)

>  "This decision.. completely erodes theaccuracy of anything the Governmentchooses to present tomorrow," he said inreference to Finance Minister RuthRichardson's intention to "open thebooks" and explain the state of thecountry's finances. <i>(Oct 5, 1993,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  In the next three years, so the story inthe open book goes, unemployment "willremain around current levels." <i>(Oct 8, 1993,Dominion,7</i>)

>  The National Party opened its books to the writer, and made available its records and files. <i>(Nov 1, 1986,Dominion,5</i>)

>  <b>ROBERTS</b> The document was released as part of the pledge by the new Labour] Government to `open thebooks'. [footnoted to a reference to R.O. Douglas's Preface to Economic Management, The Treasury, 1984: p. 111] <i>(1987,Politicians, Public Servants & Public Enterprise,118</i>)

>  The advice I'm giving Jenny Shipley is to open the books. She has to be frank and point out just how revckless Mr Peters has been. <i>(Aug 29, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),21</i>)

>  Labour.. cannot release such detail until Labour has seen the books and the IMF report has been one of the books Labour has been demanding to be opened. <i>(Jul 11, 1984,Evening Post,60</i>)

>  Of late, however, in consequence of the blustering abuse with which we have been assailed, we have paid some attention to the Exodus, having made enquiry of shipping agents, who have obligingly opened their books to us. <i>(Jan 24, 1860,Daily Southern Cross,2</i>)

>  Why, if this were consistent, the ignorant public might start up and suggest that the New Zealand Parliament should cause Banks to open their books to legalised auditors, and pry into overdrafts, &c, &c, of a few founders, who have become practical millionaires thereby, in their own accounts, or perhaps require a set of directors to be ineligible for re-election, or under restriction, so as not to be playing the game of " one out the other come in " in rotation, for such a state of things would play " old gooseberry " with the manipulation of assets. <i>(Jun 13, 1878,Timaru Herald,4</i>)



## open up
 
 <i>v.</i>

>  This industry gives employment to a large number of persons, both directly to those engaged in breeding, shearing, freezing, and shipping, and indirectly to those who labour at bushfelling, grass-seed sowing, fencing, and otherwise opening up new country. <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,11</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> [(shearers')]

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> A big Maori was making the [shearing] pace; opening up in a scientific fashion with a clean-run cut over the ear-root <i>(1905,Sons o' Men,81</i>)

>  ... he leaned well over the sheep, placed the forelegs under his left arm, and opened up the right side of the belly with a few deft strokes. <i>(1981,Sunrise,45</i>)

>  open up: To shear wool from a certain area. An example is to `open up the neck'. <i>(1989,NZ English Newsletter,3/26</i>) [NOTES: Wallace Shearing Glossary]



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to begin a job, especially in shearing a sheep and harvesting crops.

>  <b>ARTHUR</b> Yesterday we had no spell, Rhody and I having to open up the other crop of oats with the binder, while tomorrow I am to go on cutting them while the others finish off the stacking <i>(1984,Waiouru: Land of the Tussock 1935-40,26</i>)

>  <b>WESTMACOTT</b> I had never been a shearer, but knew how to hold a sheep, open up, and work round the back ... <i>(1977,After-Breakfast Cigar, The,170</i>)

>  <b>BATES</b> The job shearing my sheep turned out a tough one, for the majority were Lincolns and they were hard to open up and very greasy. Pat and I would look over them in the pen before we caught one, hoping that this one was going to be easy and that his wool would open up good ... <i>(1982,Bridge to Nowhere, The,117</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i>

>  <b>PYKE</b> Other gullies and beaches further up the river were shortly afterwards opened up. <i>(1887,Handbook NZ Mines,11</i>) [NOTES: 1863 V. Pyke's Report in 1887 Handbook NZ Mines]



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to expose land to stock after it has been 'shut up'.

>  Several paddocks are then opened up at a time, so that the herd has a greater area to feed over ... <i>(Apr 16, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,350</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to develop or to tame land.

>  <b>TAIT</b> In the early 1880s up to 1,000 men were employed opening up the area and attending to 40,000 sheep. <i>(1956,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol I,32</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> The axe was followed by the match, and with the country opened up it looked as if this could well become one of the province's favoured farming districts. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,175</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to make tracks or roads through land.

>  <b>FORRESTER</b> Cullers explored and opened up the back country, pioneering tracks across alpine passes later to be followed by thousands of trampers, sports hunters, mountaineers and nature-lovers. <i>(1983,Chopper Boys, The,18</i>)

>  The new road to Karori, made by the New Zealand Company, is proceeding satisfactorily. It will be an excellent road, very nearly level, and will open up one of the finest districts in the neighbourhood of Port Nicholson... <i>(Apr 20, 1842,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2</i>)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> We heard of one runholder who had his station completely opened up where no roads had previously existed. <i>(1971,Historic Sheep Stations SI II,38</i>)

>  We trust, also, that some steps will shortly be taken to open up the new districts in the Hutt, and even to carry a road through to Wairarapa. <i>(Dec 16, 1842,New Zealand Colonist & Port Nicholson Advertiser,2</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> country or land for settlement (also OPEN)

>  The Chairman brought up the suggestion of opening up with relief labour of Crown land bush sections along the foot of the Longwoods, the idea being to clean approximately 50 acres on each section, relief labour being used; build a home similar to those provided under the Small Farm Scheme, and settle the property with a suitable relief worker, paying him relief wages, for a limited period, until he could sustain himself. <i>(Aug 16, 1932,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,4</i>)

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> `The country is now comparatively opened up,' Sir Julius Vogel was able to boast ... <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,233</i>)

>  This, however, arose from circumstances over which he had no control; as, in the first place, he was not fortunate enough to draw any very early choices, and secondly, the difficulties that have presented themselves to the surveying staff since their arrival in the Colony have been such as to have rendered it almost impossible for a company unaided even by the countenance of the [H]ome Government, to have the country opened up much more rapidly than they have done. <i>(Apr 19, 1841,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,1</i>)

>  Beyond accomodating the North Taieri settlers and a few miners, there is little call for a railway on the first twenty-five miles of the route - but from that point every mile opens up country fit for settlement, and calculated to support a large population. <i>(Aug 21, 1877,Bruce Herald,7</i>)

>  Mr. E. C. Goldsmith, Commissioner of Crown Lands, in an interview with a Herald reporter regarding the land hunger in this district, indicated that the great obstacles to the opening up of Crown lands were their inaccessibility, and the difficulty of securing the native lands that were suitable for settlement. <i>(May 16, 1911,Evening Post,3</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> to slit the stomach of an animal (freezingworkers')

>  Pelters open up (that is, slit guts) and if not careful they cut the paunch and .us;grass goes all over the place. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: Feilding Freezing Works terms p.c. Colin Gordon]



## open up pelters
 
 <i>phr.</i> to slit an animal's guts

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> PELTERS (Must also do Ring) <i>(1975,Log of a Superfluous Son, The,42</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> <i>Pelters open up</i> (that is, slit guts) and if not careful they cut the paunch and grass goes all over the place. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: Feilding Freezing Works terms]



## open winter
 
 <i>n.</i> [CC Ransom notes: But open winter is an old American term for a mild winter: we always thought of freedom from heavy snow as its chief characteristic: thus countryside is open for travel, movement of beasts, etc]

>  <b>NEDWTON</b> ~: In winter snow forms a boundary for stock. If the winter is mild this boundary may not be sufficient; the country is then open for sheep to get away back into dangerous areas - hence the term `open winter.' <i>(1949,High Country Days,196,1950 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Gloss.]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [high country] a relatively snow-free season that does not allow a clear boundary for safe sheep grazing.

>  Italian ryegrass has seldom been destroyed by frost, but it runs the risk of being rotted out by over luxuriance in an open winter. <i>(Apr 15, 1862,Wellington Independent,3</i>)

>  We had a very mild and open winter. <i>(Aug 16, 1873,Otago Witness,10</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Open winter: In winter snow forms a boundary for stock. If the winter is mild, this boundary may not be sufficient; the country is then open for sheep to get away back into dangerous areas - hence the term 'open winter.' <i>(1949,High Country Days,196</i>)

>  After an unusually long period of fine open winter weather - more like summer indeed - the rain, which has been threatening the last day or two, is now coming down in earnest - most acceptably for the farmers, who were feeling the want of seasonable moisture. <i>(Sep 3, 1859,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2</i>)

>  We are still enjoying a fine open winter, and farmers are taking advantage of the beautiful weather by pushing forward with sowing. <i>(Jul 25, 1889,Otago Witness,17</i>)

>  We have had a very open winter, having had more frost than the majority of us have seen here for some years past. <i>(Aug 9, 1894,Otago Witness,23</i>)

>  Farm work in the district is well forward, owing to the open winter we are having, and so far everything points to an early seed time. <i>(Jul 18, 1904,Southland Times,3</i>)

>  There is also a drop of 1/4d to 1/2d in frozen meat quotations just now, which does not tend to make the sheep market any livelier, also notwithstanding the very open winter we have experienced hitherto, there is an impression abroad that the turnip crops will hardly see the winter out. <i>(Aug 15, 1911,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,4</i>)

>  The open winter has certainly been responsible for a much-improved range of values for July than was anticipated, and with that month behind farmers have lost all fear of a shortage of feed. <i>(Jul 27, 1920,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,3</i>)

>  The exceptionally open winter we have had has, by bringing forward the grass, greatly increased our town meltings. <i>(May 18, 1872,Otago Witness,4</i>)



## open-faced
 
 <i>adj.</i> (of sheep) with heads relatively free of wool (See [clear-faced](../C#clear-faced),[clean-faced](../C#clean-faced))

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> Runholders later reduced this trouble [woolblindness] by using open faced rams. <i>(1940,Te Waimate,119</i>)

>  <b>TAIT</b> The founder of Blackford was after a reasonably open-faced and clean-legged type. <i>(1956,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol I,36</i>)

>  The Romneys are open-faced and heavy-woolled. <i>(Nov 6, 1963,Straight Furrow,46</i>)

>  <b>DU FAUR</b> Research has shown that the 'open-faced' ewes will consistently produce between 15 and 25% more lambs. Farmers should be prepared to pay a little more for the 'open-faced' ewes than for those that need eye wigging twice a year to prevent blindness. <i>(1966,Sheep Farming for Profit,140</i>)

>  They must be open-faced sheep with free-opening fleeces.. <i>(Aug 13, 1970,NZ Farmer,15</i>)

>  <b>BARTON</b> The [Romney Development] Group has already developed an open-faced, easy-care Romney.. [open faced] <i>(1971,Sheepfarming Annual 1971,69</i>)

[82] Each has agreed to a permanent open-space convenant for their special place, enacting conditions and prohibitions designed to keep the area in its natural space. <i>(Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/81</i>)

## Opo
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  [Heading] Opo the Gay Dolphin <i>(Jul 1958,Te Ao Hou,23: 22</i>)

>  <b>ALPERS</b> The dolphin had become generally known by the nickname 'Opo', having first been known as 'Opononi Jack'. <i>Ibid. 212 [Notice erected outside town] </i>WELCOME TO OPONONI BUT DON'T SHOOT OUR GAY DOLPHIN [sic]. <i>(1963,Dolphins,209</i>) [NOTES: 2nd edition]

>  <b>DOAK</b> With some cases, such as Opo in New Zealand and Donald in the United Kingdom, the dolphins have been described as 'social outcasts' or 'bulls ostracised from the herd.' <i>(1988,Encounters with Whales & Dolphins,59</i>)

>  North of Opononi, where Opo the dolphin frolicked with bathers in the 1950s (she is still remembered by a sculpture outside the pub) we head into the hills and go horse trekking on a wilderness farm. <i>(Apr 2002,AA Directions,No. 51 (Autumn) : 18</i>)

>  Opo ties all these things together in an event that is more than just a summer holiday, but is all the summer holidays you ever had in the 50s and 60s. <i>(Dec 3, 1994,Listener (New Zealand Listener),54</i>)

>  Opononi Jack, the man-loving mammal... "Opo's" antics and shows of delight in human company have already made him Opononi's celebrity No. 1. <i>(Feb 15, 1956,Weekly News,27</i>)

>  [Caption] Opo, the Opononi dolphin, who is already getting into the news overseas, is now showing what a good water-polo player he is. <i>(Feb 23, 1956,Weekly News,30</i>)

>  The untimely death of Opo George (who turned out to be Georgina) caused sadness in Opononi... Opo was touching the fringes of a world that was stranger than she could know. <i>(Mar 23, 1956,Listener (New Zealand Listener),4</i>)

>  The autopsy determined Opo's sex as feminine (so the name 'Opo Joe' sometimes used is a misnomer). <i>(1960,Greece & Rome,7/83</i>)

>  <b>GASKIN</b> 'Opo' was a partly grown female <i>Tursiops</i>.. believed to be the offspring of a female shot by a local youth. <i>(1972,Whales, Dolphins & Seals,131</i>)

>  Forty years ago, Opo the dolphin was found dead in a rock pool at Opononi in the Hokianga Harbour. <i>(Jun 1, 1996,Listener (New Zealand Listener),10</i>)

>  Forty years ago, Opo the dolphin was found dead in a rock pool at Opononi in the Hokianga Harbour. <i>(Jun 1, 1996,Listener (New Zealand Listener),10</i>)

>  <i>Opo, The Crazy Dolphin</i>, with vocals by Pat McMinn, was played incessantly in the summer of 1955-56, when the dolphin, now part of Northland lore, played with adoring holidaymakers at Opononi on the Hokianga Harbour. <i>(Mar 13, 2003,Dominion Post,B6</i>)

>  Captain Ahab had Moby Dick, the great white whale, in the 19th century novel about truth, nature and God. Last-century New Zealanders had Opo the dolphin, who for one magic summer persuaded people of the benevolence in nature. <i>(Jul 29, 2001,Sunday Star Times,A7</i>)

## opossum
 
 <i>n.</i> [Noun.]

>  I cannot fancy the massive form of the Maori darting up the stem of a slippery gum tree to cut out the opossum from his hole! <i>(Dec 8, 1852,New Zealand Spectator & Cook's Strait Guardian,3</i>)

>  If his recollection served him right, he added, it was about 25 years since the first pair of opossums were introduced into the district. <i>(Aug 22, 1908,Poverty Bay Herald,5</i>)

>  At the Magistrate's Court yesterday afternoon, before Mr T. E. Maunsell, S.M., two young men, Owen and Patrick Johnston, of Kaiata, were charged with being found in possession of imported game, to wit opossums. <i>(May 26, 1920,Grey River Argus,4</i>)

>  I cannot allow to go unchallenged David Yerex's assertion that the opossum is now possum... The scientists' preference for "possum" over "opossum" is on the rather dubious etymological grounds that "possum" is the common Australian spelling and that the first European to sight and record the animal, Captain Cook, called it a "possum". <i>(Jan 16, 1982,Listener (New Zealand Listener),6</i>)

>  <i>O-pos'.sum</i> (o.-p&obreve.s'.s&ubreve.m), <i>n. (Zoo.l.) [Add.]</i> 2. The phalanger; - called also, colloquially,<i>possum</i> . See PHALANGER, in the Dictionary. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2028</i>)

>  <i>opossum, possum ... n. marsupial, a phalanger ... of several genera [Orig. America]. </i> <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)

>  <i>opossum</i> ... 2. Austral. & N.Z. any of various similar animals, esp. a phalanger. Usually shortened to <i>possum</i> <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,792</i>) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 526; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 339]]

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> Max has heard the Dawn Chorus; thinks of fledglings starving to death, the parent birds slain by stoat, weasel, cat; the opossum plague from the rata cliffs; ... <i>(1981,Exiles of Asbestos Cottage, The,53</i>)

>  Staring straight into the lens of a camera, he can sometimes doisplay the composure of an opossum frozen in headlight beams. <i>(Jun 4, 1983,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30</i>)

>  It seems that opossums are increasing in the bush near Masterton. <i>(Apr 14, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  We agreed to hold a convivial evening in our tent, as it possessed the luxury of an opossum rug. <i>(Dec 23, 1890,Otago Witness,37</i>)

>  I could see the opossums every evening perched on dead limbs 30 yds outside the fence. <i>(Oct 30, 1890,Otago Witness,18</i>)

>  Mr G Langensiepen showed two valuable opossum rugs. <i>(Nov 26, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  A member of a party camping out on the Dun Mountain, Nelson, recently, was attacked by an opossum, which fastened on to his ankle. <i>(Feb 18, 1897,Otago Witness,8</i>)

>  WE have THIS DAY Received a Fresh Consignment of RUGS AND MATS of the finest quality, Made of the Skins of the Native Cat, Kangaroo, Black, Blue, Grey, and Silver Opossums. <i>(Jun 19, 1878,Star, The,2</i>)

>  The danger of the extermination and extinction of the opossum in New Zealand through recent removal of protection was strongly emphasised. <i>(Jul 23, 1913,Evening Post,2</i>)

 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a designated area where a possum hunter is contracted to work.

>  <b>MACINTOSH</b> He was poisoning in a 1600 acre block granted to him by the New Zealand Forest Service as an opossum block. <i>(1974,Never a Dull Moment,127</i>)



## opossum rug/possum rug
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [also wogga](../A#also-wogga))

>  Wilson, Taine and Co. - Opossum rugs, &c., in the rooms, at 2 p.m. <i>(Jun 21, 1883,Southland Times,2</i>)

>  <b>CARR</b> Sheets are not fashionable, while three sacks sewn together make what is termed a `New Zealand Possum Rug' or `Wogga'. <i>(1913,Country Work,6</i>)

>  Opossum Rugs and Mosgiel Mauds. <i>(Oct 15, 1887,Nelson Evening Mail,4</i>)

>  <b>SEWELL</b> Mine consisted of a few changes of Linen, an Opossum rug, a blanket, air bed and pillow and a few etceteras. op. cit. 2/212 bed, blanket and possum rug. <i>(1856,Journal,2/207,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <b>T.S.GRACE</b> He took out a box and placed on the floor three of the finest sleeping-mats I have ever seen,and quite new. On these he put two large, new blankets and a splendid opossum rug, and a pillow. <i>(Oct 28, 1871,Journal</i>) [NOTES: 1871 Oct 28 T.S.Grace Journal in Grace PioneerMissionary (1928) 237]

>  <b>NEAVE</b> I was to get a liberal screw and found for four months [at Otematata Station], beginning the 8th of March [1858], with... two cotton shirts ... no underclothing, no socks, but pieces of blanket rolled round your feet (we called them Prince Alberts) ... I had to make myself a cap out of a piece of an old opossum rug. <i>(1980,Land of Munros, The,65</i>) [NOTES: 1917 James Strachan Experiences (Hocken L.) in1980 Neave The Land of Munros]

>  <b>SEDDON</b> ... two of us would find ourselves wrapped in the old grey opossum rug, the rug with the red cloth lining nestling on the floor near the feet of our parents on the front seat. <i>(1968,Seddons, The,30</i>)

## OPs
 
 <i>abbr.</i> other people's; something borrowed or cadged (usually a cigarette cf. DOOFER) [Jim Cameron writes: Formerly common esp. in relation to cigarette smoking, and thus perhaps dated.
