## orache
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Atriplex </i>spp.

>  <i>orache [s] Atriplex hastata</i> L.<i> Atriplex patula L. </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,55</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> ORACHE (<i>Atriplex patula</i> and <i>A. hastata</i>). ... Like fathen both orache species are native to much of the Northern Hemisphere. They occur widely in New Zealand. . . <i>(1980,Weeds of Crops,70</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <i>orache red</i> [s] <i>Atriplex hortensis</i> L.f.<i> rubra</i> Roth <i>Ibid.</i> 63 red<i>orache</i> [s] <i>Atriplex hortensis</i> L.f. <i>rubra</i> Roth <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,55</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BROOKER et al.</b> <i>Atriplex patula</i> L. Common name: <i>Common orache</i> ... [Not in Allan's Flora: quotes Hooker's Banks Journal `herb like `lamb's-quarters' or `fat-hen'': as native NZ herb] <i>(1981,NZ Medicinal Plants,39</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Atriplex Billardieri </i> <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story,2nd ed. ed.</i>)

>  <b>MOORE & IRWIN</b> <i>ATRIPLEX NOVAE-ZELANDIAE</i>. New Zealand orache is a sprawling plant of sea-shore gravel and sand, lightly mealy on its younger parts. <i>(1978,Oxford Book of NZ Plants,52</i>)

## orange blossom
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Hoheria populnea</i> (See [lacebark](../L#lacebark))

>  <b>FEATON</b> HOHERIA POPULNEA (<i>A.Cunn.</i>) ... The `HOUI' or `WHAU-WHI'.- ... grows abundantly in both Islands. ... Owing to the chaste [snow-white] appearance of its flowers, it has not inaptly been termed by some the `New Zealand orange Blossom'. The inner bark is peculiar, and consists of layer upon layer of laced fibre, ...From this peculiarity it is called by the settlers the `Thousand Jacket', `lace-bark', or `Ribbon-wood'. <i>(1889,Art Album NZ Flora,55</i>)

## orange roughy
 
 <i>n.</i> a coastline patrol officer, as in the TV drama Orange Roughies [(transferred use)]

>  Orange Roughies (one): Originally scheduled for 2005, this follows the coppers who patrol our coastline. <i>(Jan 14, 2006,Dominion Post,E7</i>)

>  The talented Aucklander will also star in the new water-cop drama <i>Orange Roughies </i>. <i>(May 14, 2006,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),13</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a bright orange, fine-fleshed, deep-water food-fish. spg <i>roughie</i> a fish species =ROUGHY [x-reffed to R] now 1988 mainly referred to in menus, etc. as ROUGHY ; [new example and suggested amendment to definition]<i>Hoplostethus atlanticus</i>, a deep-sea fish first discovered in commercial quantities off New Zealand in 1975 and now widely fished throughout the world (Bradstock)

>  We had to get rid of the orange roughy. The old Austin needed work on its engine, in addition to the extensive panel damage. <i>(Aug 1991,Metro,111</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> ORANGE ROUGHY <i>Hoplostethus atlanticus </i> <i>(1982,Collins Guide,175,1984 ed.</i>)

>  We're [sc. the Rural Bank] supporting theOrange Roughy, squid, tuna, mussel andcrayfish fisheries the same way wesupport traditional farming. (Advt). <i>(Apr 6, 1985,Listener (New Zealand Listener),72</i>)

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> In November 1975 [the Japanese research vessel <i>Shinkai Maru</i>] was trawling on the Chatham Rise when she brought up a full bag of brightly orange-coloured fish. MAF scientist Gavin James and his Japanese counterpart Tadashi Inada had never seen the fish before, nor could they find it in their identification guides. After a careful inspection they decided it looked a bit like an inshore fish of the roughy family. It was orange, so they recorded it as 'orange roughy'. <i>(2004,Hooked,284</i>)

>  The fish, known as the `rattail' or Coelinchorus, were caught off the coast of ... Taranaki, in the orange roughy grounds at a depth of 800m ... The rattail also known as the grenadier is closely related to the codfish family, feeding on the bottom of the ocean ... Of the 40 known species of Coelinchorus 11 of them seem to be indigenous only to New Zealand says Mr Paulins. Notes check spelling Coelinchorus <i>(Jul 22, 1988,Evening Post,14</i>)

>  Few commercial fish species, other than southern bluefin tuna, southern rock lobster and the Challenger orange roughy, have been depleted to such a level. <i>(Apr 19, 2000,Dominion,12</i>)

>  Recent specimens placed in bottles on a shelf in the ethnological room include the Roughy, the John Dory, the silver dory, the rat tail, the flounder, the sole, the leatherback, and seahorse. <i>(Oct 8, 1907,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  From the dark obscurity of its deep ocean habitat orange roughy has become a common name in New Zealand and a common sight in North American supermarkets and restaurants over the past six years. <i>(May 17, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),34</i>)

>  [Hdg] Last of the orange roughy. As a species, orange roughy don't have a heck of a lot of crowd appeal. <i>(Jun 9, 2001,Dominion,5</i>)

>  The state of orange roughy fisheries has roused the ire of Mr Webber and other conservationists. <i>(Jun 9, 2001,Dominion,5</i>)

>  Trawling at 1000 metres in the rough waters off the Chatham Rise three years ago the Wesermunde began hauling in loads of a strange orange fish. ... It appeared similar to a fish the Russians called `sea perch' and to a fish caught by the Japanese off the Okinawa coast - a fish they did not like because is caused diarrhoea. ... It had beautifully white (desirable flesh, thick fillets with no small bones, it cooked well, ... and it was sizeable (cheap to process). ... Now known as orange roughy ... <i>(Nov 22, 1981,NZ Times,17</i>)

>  Many joint fishing ventures are likely to pull out because of the new Government policy on orange roughy. The policy on roughy, the prime deep-water species, meant the writing was on the wall for most joint ventures... The economics of the ventures would hinge on the orange roughy quota. <i>(Dec 16, 1981,Dominion,3</i>)

>  Orange roughy: kiwifruit of the sea, the fish which is a blueprint for the export market, with its firm-textured, white, odourless fillets. Or, to be technical, hoplostethus atlanticus: average length, 30 to 40cm; average weight 1.5kg. Orange body and fins, deep body, massive head with conspicuous bony ridges and cavities. A deep water species, irregular in distribution and abundance. Caught by trawling, main season May to August, resource size unknown butundoubtedly large. <i>(Aug 1, 1983,National Business Review,33</i>)

>  Concern is growing over the effects of poaching orange roughy fish from the Challenger Plateau west of New Zealand. <i>(Nov 7, 1983,Evening Post,6</i>)

>  [insert] We regret that the photo of the two Orange Roughie fish on page 339 has been printed in reverse <i>(Dec 1986,Consumer,No. 245</i>)

>  <i>orange roughy</i> NZ edible sea-fish. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,527</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng.Dict.]]

>  ...from the outset, there's been one fish Martin has been at great pains to avoid. He describes it as a very pretty white fillet off a really ugly fish, to which I might add it is hugely expensive and utterly without flavour; it's that erstwhile glamour fish of the 1980s, orange roughy. <i>(Feb 24, 2004,Dominion Post,E6</i>)

>  For Pete's sake, at least <i>look</i> as if you want to defend yourself against something fiercer than an orange roughy rustler. <i>(Dec 12, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),18</i>)

>  The orange roughy fish roe is the product of three years' work by a small family fishery in Cuba Street. <i>(Oct 21, 1991,Evening Post,15</i>)

>  Toothfish has quite a unique flavour. Orange roughy is more opaque and white with a fleshy feel. <i>(Aug 13, 2003,Dominion Post,A7</i>)

>  With her agent-orange hair and colourful costumes she blazes a trail on TV3's late-night show... We grew up with pleasant news presenters like Marama Martin and Karen Sims.. and then along came the Orange Roughy. <i>(Oct 23, 1992,Dominion,22</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> an orange box of instrumentation

>  [Hdg] ECONZ's John Clarkson, left, and John Warne of Telecom with an orange roughy... ECONZ builds and maintains the brightly coloured plastic boxes, known as orange roughies, that sit on the back of the boats. They transmit race data, such as speed and position, via satellite technology back to land five times every second. <i>(Nov 17, 2002,Sunday Star Times,E4</i>)

## orange-fronted parakeet
 
 <i>n.</i> the parakeet Cyanoramphus malherbi [Also known as the South Island parakeet and orange crested parakeet, the endangered orange-fronted parakeet could be forgiven for having an identity crisis, since the debate over its taxonomic status has spanned three centuries.]

>  [Caption] (45) Orange Crested Parakeet .. (67) South Island Parakeet. <i>(Dec 18, 1907,Otago Witness,28</i>)

>  The orange-fronted parakeet has a pale lemon-yellow crown, an orange forehead band and orange rump spots, while the yellow-crowned has a bright golden-yellow crown, with crimson frontal band and rump spots. <i>(Aug 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 52/14</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> PLATYCERCUS ALPINUS. (ORANGE-FRONTED PARRAKEET.) .. <i>(1888,Birds of New Zealand,1/146</i>)

>  <i>223. Cyanoramphus malherbi </i>(Souance[e-acute], 1857) <i>Orange-fronted parakeet</i> ... South Island only (very rare) ... <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,59,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <i>223. Cyanoramphus malherbi </i>(Souance[e-acute], 1857) <i>Orange-fronted parakeet</i> ... South Island only (very rare) ... <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,59,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Orange-fronted parakeet Cyanoramphus malherbi</i> (SOUANCE', 1857) ... Since European settlement the orange-fronted parakeet has never been as common as other New Zealand parakeets. ... OTHER NAME: <i>Alpine parakeet. ... Probably now in the South Island only. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,247</i>)

>  The first recorded mention of the orange-fronted parakeet was in 1857 by Charles De Souance, who named it <i>Cyanoramphus malherbi</i> from a single specimen, source unknown. <i>(May 2002,NZ Geographic,51/10</i>)



## orange-leaf
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Coprosma lucida</i> ORANGE-LEAF (TREE)

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Orange-leaf of Otago. <i>Coprosma lucida</i>, etc. <i>(1867,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766</i>)

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> [<i>Coprosma lucida</i>, Forst.] The `Broad-leaf' or `Orange-leaf' tree of the settler; terms, however, which are probably not only applied to other species of the genus <i>Coprosma</i>, (e.g. <i>C. robusta</i>, Raoul,) but to species of genera belonging to very different families - and especially to <i>Griselinia lucida, (N.O. Corneae,)</i> whose foliage closely resembles that of <i>Coprosma lucida ... C. lucida</i> is also known to the Otago natives as `Kalamou' (Hector); and to the North Island Maoris as `Karamu', [fn] `Kakaramu,' or `Karangu,' [fn] (Colenso.) [fn] Also applied to <i>C. foetidissima.</i> <i>(1868,Contributions to NZ Botany,71</i>)

## orange-wattled crow
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [also crow](../A#also-crow))

>  The orange-wattled crow is sometimes mistaken for [the huia] by trampers. <i>(Sep 4, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/167 : 10</i>)

>  The orange-wattled crow.. very much resembles the North Island species with the exception of the wattles, which are red or reddish-brown. <i>(Apr 17, 1890,Otago Witness,18</i>)

>  <i>G. cinerea</i>. - Kokako, Orange-wattled Crow. South Island. Very irregular in distribution. <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/112</i>) [NOTES: (Martin)]

>  Sir James Hector, in drawing attention.. to a very fine specimen of the New Zealand crow or jay. as it should more properly be called, made interesting references to the complete isolation of the orange wattle crow, in the South Island, and the blue wattle crow in the North Island. <i>(Dec 21, 1897,Otago Witness,10</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) (See [kokako](../K#kokako))

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 41. GLAUCOPIS CINEREA. Gml. Orange-wattled Crow. <i>Callaeas Wilsoni.</i> BULLER. <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,16</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> GLAUCOPIS CINEREA. (ORANGE-WATTLED CROW.) ... <i>Native name</i>.- Kokako. ... This species is the South-Island representative of <i>Glaucopis wilsoni</i>, to which it bears a general resemblance, except in the colour of its wattles and its rather smaller size. <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,155,1888 2nd edn I/5 ed.</i>)

>  <i>G. cinerea</i>. - Kokako, Orange-wattled Crow. South Island. Very irregular in distribution. <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/112</i>) [NOTES: (Martin)]

>  ... crows [are represented] by a genus - <i>Glaucopsis</i> - ... with one species in the north and another in the south (the orange-wattled crow). <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,195</i>)

>  <i>Callaeas cinerea</i> Gm. Orange-wattled Crow. With the spread of settlement the diminution of the numbers of this tame species was almost inevitable. <i>(1923,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/97</i>) [NOTES: Myers Endemic Birds]

>  <b>HOWARD</b> [Stewart Island birds include] the fern-bird (<i>Bowdleria fulva</i>), the bell-bird ... and in the second class: Orange-wattled crow (<i>Glaucopis cinerea</i>) ... <i>(1940,Rakiura,323</i>)

>  <b>POWELL</b> BLUE-WATTLED CROW (<i>Callaeas wilsoni</i>). A moderately large dark bluish grey bird with a stout black bill, ... and conspicuous wattles of bright blue. The South Island species, <i>C. cinerea</i>, is almost identical except for the wattles, which are orange....The Orange-wattled crow is still plentiful...The Maori name for both species is Kokako. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,87</i>)

>  <i>Kokako Callaeas cinerea</i> (GMELIN, 1788) ... <i>North Island Kokako C.c. wilsoni</i> (BONAPARTE, 1851). <i>South Island Kokako C.c. cinerea </i> (GMELIN, 1788). OTHER NAMES: <i>Blue wattled crow, blue gill, orange wattled crow, organ bird. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,303</i>)



## orange-wattled kokako
 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (Environment)

>  <i>Glaucopis wilsoni.</i> - Kokako, Blue-wattled Crow. North Island. Very irregular in distribution. <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/112</i>) [NOTES: (Martin)]

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> There are two races of kokako, <i>Callaeas cinerea wilsoni</i>, the blue-wattled kokako, which occurs in the North Island, and <i>Callaeas cinerea cinerea</i>, the orange-wattled kokako, inhabiting the South Island and Stewart Island. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/235</i>)

The thick, starchy, tuberous roots formed a part of the winter food supply of the maori, especially of Tuhoe of the Urewera district. When being sought by the Tuhoe the tubers would be called maukuku, not huperei; if the wrong name was mentioned during the search, the roots were said to hide themselves. <i>(1997,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,57</i>)

## ordinary bloke
 
 <i>n.</i> the man in the street [cf column in Wellington Contact-->] (See [also ordinary joker](../A#also-ordinary-joker))

>  "I'm a person of fairly modest abilities," he'll say. Or, "I'm just an ordinary bloke." <i>(May 31, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> ordinary bloke/joker the typical working man, generally held to be possessed of ggod sense and no nonsense, nor frills, neither; eg `Crumpy's one of your genuine ordinary jokers, whadya reckon?' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)

>  Hillary.. has continued to be the type of New Zealander we'd like to see in the mirror each morning; practical, hard-working, self-effacing, determined, modest ... an ordinary bloke. If it was possible to have an atypical national character, it would be Hillary. He was an ordinary bloke who performed an extraordinary deed and, having come down from his mountain, he remained as untouched by the heights of his fame as if he'd never attained them. <i>(May 31, 2003,Dominion Post,C23</i>)

>  Frank Sargeson aimed to write about the ordinary bloke, but he did not reach him. Barry Crump did with his good keen caricature. <i>(Apr 10, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),27</i>)

>  <b>JAMES</b> Muldoon constructed a stereotype of the 'ordinary bloke' or 'ordinary citizen' whom he held, in his speeches, in exaggerated reverence as the fount of all wisdom and the touchstone of political legitimacy. <i>(1986,Quiet Revolution, The,84</i>)

>  They recalled his lifelong touchstone - the ordinary bloke. (And bloke-ess, when Sir Robert dabbled wickedly with feminism in later life.) <i>(Aug 6, 1992,Dominion,2</i>)

>  Because he could not tax his ordinary bloke too much, he borrowed, in bucketsful... All that together gave him inflation, which ate away the ordinary bloke's living standards. <i>(Aug 9, 1992,Dominion Sunday Times,9</i>)

>  Keep up the good work on behalf of the ordinary bloke and blokess, yours sincerely, Rt Hon Don McKinnon. <i>(Aug 28, 1992,Dominion,23</i>)

>  Here we have the real "Bloke" and the very "Doreen" - local color, atmosphere, properties, imidentials [?] - all are perfect. <i>(Jan 19, 1920,Poverty Bay Herald,5</i>)

>  At the same time it serves to prove his pitiful boast that he can go one - or two - better than the average bloke. <i>(Aug 13, 1921,NZ Truth,1</i>)

>  Otherwise he's just an ordinary bloke like the rest of us. <i>(Mar 24, 1927,NZ Truth,6</i>)



## ordinary claim
 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i>

>  It will be necessary not only to grant prospecting claims, but to define what shall be the size of ordinary claims; to make provision for water-bailing, for the amalgamation of claims, and for the issue of leases, so as to encourage the erection of proper machinery for pumping and crushing. <i>(Feb 1, 1862,Otago Witness,5</i>)

>  At this Court yesterday, Warden McCarthy granted the following applications .. A. Otway for mail tail race and ordinary claims. <i>(Jul 7, 1904,Southland Times,3</i>)

>  Growcott and Hartke (Mr Wells), surrender of ordinary claim at Seddon's Terrace. - Accepted. <i>(Jun 6, 1919,Grey River Argus,4</i>)

>  If neither of them should get anything payable then the prospectors may be said to have all the gold in their own claim, which would be nothing new in this country, where the leads are so short, and in this instance where the prospecting claim is so long, being equivalent to two ordinary claims, so that it is nothing wonderful if some of the many claims marked out on either side of them should be duffers. <i>(May 5, 1876,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  At this Court yesterday, Warden McCarthy granted the following applications .. A. Otway for mail tail race and ordinary claims. <i>(Jul 7, 1904,Southland Times,3</i>)

>  An ordinary claim must not exceed an area of five acres. <i>(Jul 3, 1930,NZ Truth,24</i>)

>  <b>WEKEY</b> Claims shall be classified thus;- 1. Ordinary Claims, i.e., alluvial claims worked without the aid of sluices or machines. 2. Sluice and machine claims, i.e., alluvial claims worked with the aid of sluices or puddling machines. 3. Wet Claims, i.e., alluvial claims in flooded ground ... 4. Creek or river claims, i.e., alluvial claims in the bed of creeks or rivers. 5. Quartz claims. <i>(1862,Otago As It Is,61</i>) [NOTES: (quoting Otago Gfds Regs)]

>  Phil. T. Green, application for protection for ordinary claim at the Waiho river, for three months - Granted. <i>(Jul 30, 1895,West Coast Times,4</i>)

>  Three miners, of whom I am one, applied for an ordinary claim. We proved it to be payable, but because a man named Buchanan squatted on it as the owner of residence area, the Warden refused to grant our application. <i>(Aug 11, 1888,West Coast Times,4</i>)

>  Claims are classified under five heads, vis.: Ordinary claims, i.e., alluvial claims, worked without the aid of sluices or machines.. The size of Claims is then fixed as follows: Ordinary Claims shall not exceed 30 feet by 30 feet for each person, unless the depth of sinking exceeds 50 feet, in which case such claim may be extended to 35 feet by 35 feet. <i>(Jul 5, 1862,Otago Witness,6</i>)

## ore to ore
 
 <i>phr.</i> [(goldmining)]

>  The ore-shoots of all the approximately parallel Hauraki veins occurred in corresponding positions in the veins, and pitched in the same direction as the main shoot. This is the structure termed in the older mining phraseology `ore to ore', or shoots `back to back'. <i>(1907,NZ Geol. Survey Bulletin,No. 4 (N.S.): 106</i>) [NOTES: (Fraser and Adams Coromandel)]

## organ bird
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) the indigenous bird, kokako [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> There was however one bird that was distinguished from all the rest, as well by the compass and variety of its notes, as by their incomparable sweetness. This bird, which has been brought to Port Jackson, and highly prized there, is called by the colonists [335] the organ bird, and is, I believe, peculiar to New Zealand: ... <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/334</i>)

>  <b>SHEPHERD</b> This day returned to Albion cove saw ... a Parot of brownish colour and a large white bird as large as a turky and a small sort of brown teal duck, a large black and white Duck called a Paradise Duck a black bird called the organ bird shags a Black Robin with a white breast ... <i>(Mar 9, 1826,Journal</i>) [NOTES: 1826 Mar 9 Shepherd Journal in 1940 Howard Rakiura 359]

>  `The organ-bird', says Mr. Nicholas, `is, I believe, peculiar to New Zealand. The notes of thenightingale .... are, in my opinion, much inferior to the song of this bird.' <i>(1835,Some Particulars Concerning NZ,12</i>)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> KOKAKO <i>Callaeas cinerea Other names:</i> Wattled Crow, Organ-bird, Gill-bird, Bluegill (N.I.). <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,236</i>)

>  <i>Kokako Callaeas cinerea</i> (GMELIN, 1788) ... <i> North Island Kokako C.c. wilsoni</i> (BONAPARTE, 1851). <i>South Island Kokako C.c. cinerea</i> (GMELIN, 1788). OTHER NAMES: <i>Blue wattled crow, blue gill, orange wattled crow, organ bird. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,303</i>)

>  Kokako (<i> Callaeas cinerea wilsoni </i>) sometimes known as the blue-wattled crow, or the organ bird, is one of the country's most threatened species. At most only about 3000 survive. <i>(May 16, 2009,Your Weekend,13</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> The rare Kokako, or Organ-bird,.. is, rather than. the Korimako, the true Bellbird of the New Zealand forest. <i>(1938,Children of Tane,227</i>)

## Orion
 
 <i>n.</i> the Shacklock Orion range

>  to / taste the fine Scots shortbread fired in the coal range / heat. "SHACKLOCK ORION" the iron door stated... <i>(Sep 28, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),61</i>) [NOTES: Jeffrey Paparoa Holman]

>  <b>BURTON</b> In 1873, however, an enterprising English settler by the name of Henry Shacklock produced the prototype of a new coal range, which he later called the Orion. Here at last was an oven designed for New Zealand conditions; its fire box was wide and shallow to draw in the extra air needed for lignite coal, and it did not need to be bricked in. The No. 1 Orion soon outsold all its competitors, ..., and was built substantially the same way until the 1930s. <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,23</i>)

>  ...he was also chief fireman in the run up to Christmas, and that meant keeping up a constant supply of firewood for the black-leaded Orion range that squatted in a corner of the kitchen...[52] The Christmas cake would have been made a couple of weeks earlier, but there were trays of cakes to be baked in the oven of the old Orion stove, as Mum and GRandma and Aunt Liz hustled around and generally got in each other's way. <i>(Dec 2002,NZ Memories,No. 39/51</i>)



## oriori

>  <b>4</b> One teaching method used by Maoris traditionally was oriori (lullaby) singing. `An oriori, when sung to children, instils in them a deep purpose in life as well as educating them in genealogical traditions, and respect for their own culture and people,' Mr Reedy said. Orioris were also used to teach children life skills such as growing and gathering food. <i>(Aug 11, 1986,Dominion</i>)

## oscar
 
 <i>n.</i> cash - in full oscar asche [ca 1935 Havelock 1946 JNZME 20 [check quotation]]

>  <b>MILLER</b> [In the early thirties money] was also oscar, and the origin of that, I discovered, was a bit more obscure. P: money, espec. coin: Aust rh. slang in cash, from OscarAsche, Australian actor (1871-1936); AC 1919; WF n(1) 1949. <i>(1946,There Was Gold,31</i>)

>  n.(slang). money <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's)</i>)

>  Department of labour chief adviser for health and safety Mike Cosman said [the operators] had told Osh the company would be changing its plea to guilty. <i>(Jan 29, 2006,Sunday Star Times,A4</i>)

## Otago Central rail trail
 
 <i>n.</i> [rail trail itself not a NZism]

>  The Otago Central Rail Trail runs for 152km from Clyde to Middlemarch (or vice versa) and is doing for those small Central towns what the grapes have done for land values. People bike it or hike it. <i>(Dec 21, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),46</i>)

## Otago cockney
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>THOMSON</b> ...it is worthy of notice, that the colonists, soon after the formation of all the settlements, acquired distinguishing epithets; thus there was an Auckland cove, a Wellington swell, a Nelson snob, a Taranaki exquisite, an Otago cockney, and a Canterbury pilgrim. These epithets, almost already forgotten, are too characteristic to be buried in oblivion, as each originated in some peculiarity of the leading colonists. The Otago settlers derived theirs from their ignorance of the country in which they lived; the Auckland people from their extensive Sydney connexions; and the origin of the other names are sufficiently obvious without explanation. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,2/188</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> Otago cockney nickname for person from Otago province. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)

## Otago schist
 
 <i>n.</i> Geol. definition

>  It is perhaps especially notable in that it completes Mr Wood's reconnaissance mapping of the Fiordland Complex and his mapping of the Otago schists. <i>(Jul 1967,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,23/8</i>)

>  Furthermore, some of the colour matches and matching of screens between sheets are not good, especially in the case of the metamorphic subzones of the Otago schists, the Pareora series, certain Permian units and the Pomona granite. <i>(Jul 1967,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,23/9</i>)

>  Professor Hutton's work in New Zealand was on two main topics, the study of progressive metamorphism in the Otago schists, particularly in the Lake Wakatipu region, and work on heavy minerals in the gravels and sands of the west coast of the South Island. <i>(Mar 1972,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,32/13</i>)

>  Full Day Excursions: F.7, Middlemarch-Macraes. Otago schist, scheelite deposits, landforms. <i>(May 1969,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,27/2</i>)



## Otago skink
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) a splendid and highly endangered skink with brightly mottled appearance, relatively widespread in Otago and capable of living up to 40 years. [<i>Oligosoma otagense</i>]

>  The critically endangered Otago skink is the South Island's largest skink, and is found only amongst rocky outcrops in tussock grassland. <i>(2006,Internet - see Notes,2</i>) [NOTES: www.doc.govt.nz/upload/documents/about-doc/concessions-and-permits/conservation .../central-otago-plants-animals-lowres.pdf.]

>  The Otago skink, the largest reptile on the New Zealand mainland, grows up to 300 mm long and lives on schist outcrops, hunting for insects in the surrounding tussock. <i>(Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/34</i>)

>  The Otago skink and grand skink are unique to Central Otago and are the largest skinks in New Zealand. Grand skinks are black with yellowish flecks, while Otago skinks are black with grey, green, or yellowish blotches. <i>(2006,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.govt.nz/upload/documents/about-doc/concessions-and-permits/conservation-revealed/skinks-geckos-lowres.pdf]

>  A mammal-proof fence around an 18-ha enclosure was built by the Department of Conservation near Macraes Flat in eastern North Otago, to protect remnant populations of the critically endangered grand skink (Oligosoma grande) and Otago skink (Oligosoma otagense) (Reardon 2006). <i>(Dec 3, 2007,Internet - see Notes,169</i>) [NOTES: http://www.newzealandecology.org/nzje/]

>  <b>Jewell</b> Otago skink <i>Oligosoma otagense</i> .. One of our most spectacular and most endangered lizard species. <i>(2008,Reptiles and Amphibians of New Zealand,75</i>)

>  The largest reptile on the mainland today lives in the schist outcrops of Otago's tussock grasslands. In this classic lizard country - dry, warm and rocky - Otago skinks soak up the sun's heat on the many convenient ledges, retreating to nearby cracks and crevices if disturbed. <i>(Jul 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 47/64</i>)

>  Otago skinks and the equally rare grand skink, which also inhabits the rocky outcrops of Otago's tussock grassland, are hardly ever found in areas that have been intensively farmed. <i>(Jul 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 47/65</i>)

>  At Macraes Flat, an hour's drive [47] north-west of Dunedin, Tocher and other DoC staff are struggling to maintain the last sizable populations of grand and Otago skinks. <i>(Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/46</i>)

>  <b>Jenny Jones</b> The other large skinks in New Zealand are the Otago skink and the chevron skink. <i>(2002,Lizards,31</i>)

>  Four captive-bred Otago skinks from Wellington were officially welcomed by the Central Otago Ecological Trust(COET), in partnership with the Department of Conservation and Landcare Research. <i>(Nov 3, 2005,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.landcareresearch.co.nz/news/release.asp?Ne_ID=185]

>  <b>James Reardon</b> Two of the largest, most colourful species of skink - the grand and the Otago - can easily be identified thanks to their bold, almost mesmerising livery. Shiny black with bold blotches of gold, the Otago skink (<i>Oligosoma otagense</i>) can reach 30 cm in length and is a true giant among the country's endemic skinks. <i>(Jan 2006,NZ Geographic,77/93</i>)

>  "You need something attractive," he says. "And these Otago skinks are big, they're black with gold spots all over them, and they're spectacular." <i>(Sep 2009,NZ Geographic,99/18</i>)

>  When the Great Otago Skink Recovery Programme began at Macraes Flat in 2004, the populations of Otago and grand skinks - New Zealand's largest and rarest lizards - were so low it was thought both species would be functionally extinct within the decade. <i>(Sep 2009,NZ Geographic,99/18</i>)

## otaio
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a New Zealand-bred cultivar of lucerne (Medicago sativa). [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  Otaio - general purpose grazing cultivar, bred and selected for resistance to bacterial wilt, stem nematode and blue-green aphid. <i>(1995,Grasslands Range of Forage and Conservation Plants,9</i>)

>  The DSIR's latest lucerne, Otaio, has already been a decade in the research pipeline. After 10 years of trials, the crop research division now has 2 kg of Otaio seed. ibid. 22 Otaio has proved itself in Canterbury field trials to be resistant to the aphids and other diseases.. <i>(Nov 1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,21</i>)

## other side
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> the other side: (favouring prepositions FROM, OF, OVER) the other side of the Tasman, Australia [AND 1. part of Australia remote from the speaker 1827 2a. a place in the northern hemisphere, esp. England 1892 b. in W.A., eastern states]

>  <b>SKEY</b> On 'the other side',* Sam, you'd have swung on a tree, / To warn other liars about; / But, respecting your age, we'll let you go free. / With just a mild taste of the knout. [* New Zealanders signify by this expression, Australia.] <i>(1899,Pirate Chief and the Mummy's Complaint..., The,Page 129</i>)

>  Fred Jones is a very promising local conjurer, and, if I remember rightly, was a pupil of Tommy Driver, late of this city, now an othersider. <i>(May 24, 1894,Otago Witness,37</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> You think it a queer way of killing a pig with a tomahawk, do you? ... But once I put a tomahawk to a different use, for I tried it on a man on the other side. <i>(1881,Waitaruna,277</i>)

>  <b>COX</b> Christchurch Magistrates' [Court] I ax your pardon, zur, but were you ever at the Yan Yean works over the other side. <i>(1884,Recollections (various authors/titles),125</i>)

>  <b>SKEY</b> On 'the other side',* Sam, you'd have swung on a tree, / To warn other liars about; / But, respecting your age, we'll let you go free. / With just a mild taste of the knout. [* New Zealanders signify by this expression, Australia.] <i>(1899,Pirate Chief and the Mummy's Complaint..., The,129</i>)

>  <b>A TRAMP</b> Mrs White .... tells me [patent saddles] are quite common on `the other side' <i>(1891,Casual Ramblings,36</i>)

>  <b>COTTLE</b> An old friend of mine came across from the other side (meaning Australia) <i>(1891,Frank Melton's Luck,124</i>)

>  And every boat that crosses from sunny `other side' / Is bringing waves of shearers... <i>(Oct 15, 1896,Otago Witness</i>) [NOTES: in 1967 Bailey and Roth Shanties 131]

>  <b>McKEE WRIGHT</b> There was chaps from the other side that I shore with that I'd like to have taken along for mates,/ <i>Ibid.</i> 53 Every boat that crosses from the sunny `other side' / Is bringing waves of shearers. <i>(1890,Station Ballads,38</i>)

>  <b>'H'</b> The last news of Ben was also from `the other side'. <i>(1898,Grain of Gold, The,50</i>)

>  He made the mistake [of] ... describing himself as an Australian ! Some ... have peculiar ideas about our neighbours `on the other side'. <i>(Apr 7, 1906,NZ Truth,42/5/8</i>)

>  <b>BOOTH</b> [In the accommodation house were] the villainous-looking 'lag' from 't'otherside', the bullock puncher, whose every alternate word was a profane oath, the stockrider, in his guernsey shirt and knee boots ... <i>(1912,Five Years in NZ,39</i>)

>  <b>15</b> Other Side. - Australia. I suppose it is a contraction of `the o.s. of the water'. The expression is still in common use. The word o.sider has never been in use here. <i>(1933,Press, The</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>BAKER</b> The other side has for long been current in this country for the Commonwealth, i.e. the other side of the Tasman. <i>(1941,NZ Slang,43</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> OVER THE OTHER SIDE: A shearer's name for Australia. <i>(1949,High Country Days,196,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Other side, the. 2. Australia: New Zealanders': since ca. 1880. B., 1941, `The other side of the Tasman [Sea]'. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang Addenda,1127</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> ...lean Australian blademen from `the other side' across the Tasman [and] tough old Merino shearers.... <i>(1951,High Country,16</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> A professional New Zealand shearer is likely to spend some time working `over the other side' as shearers call Australia. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,147</i>)

>  other side, (the) n. Australia (i.e. other side of Tasman Sea). <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> Other Side, The Australia, the other side of the water; c.1880. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,80</i>)

>  <b>PICARD</b> I've crossed you away ... that big drought sun-blazed continent the other side of the ditch. <i>(1906,Some Ups and Downs in NZ&A,7</i>)

>  ON THE OTHER SIDE. .. The first impression that a New Zealander gets in travelling through Australia is that of difference. <i>(Apr 6, 1893,Otago Witness,49</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> the other side: (from a northern hemisphere point of view) the Antipodes, the other side of the world

>  New Zealand is thus emphatically "on the other side" the Ball." [sic] It is the nearest land to England's antipodes, and in our voyage thither it is almost a matter of indifference whether we turn to the right or the left, - the east or the west, for it is within a few miles of where the longitudes, east and west, coincide. <i>(Sep 6, 1839,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,7</i>)

>  Such remissness as this is highly blameable, especially when it is an English mail, as persons are always anxiously waiting for news from their friends at the other side of the globe. <i>(Feb 11, 1843,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2</i>)

>  THE OTHER SIDE. LONDON .. The Marquis of Northhampton had hundreds of forms under the Budget to comply with. He filled them up without difficulty. <i>(Nov 5, 1910,Grey River Argus,6</i>)

>  <b>KENNAWAY</b> [Hdg] Landing on the Other Side. <i>(1874,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,8</i>)

>  <b>BAINES</b> The natives seemed intuitively to know that I was a new `pakeha' by that ship from `the other side', for, pointing from me to her inquiringly, I nodded. <i>(1874,Edward Crewe,21</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> the other side: (Stewart Islanders') =BLUFF (cf OVER THERE

>  Stewart Islanders refer to the rest of New Zealand as "the other side" and, perhaps for that reason, they have managed to retain a community and a set of manners which enable people to live in peace. <i>(Feb 3, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7</i>)

>  <b>DEMPSEY</b> And we are apt to say, when speaking of a person who is away, that he is `off the Island'. If we are told that someone has gone over to the Other Side ... we understand he has gone to Invercargill or to Bluff. Where the North Islander talks of his beach cottage, or bach, we call these little places cribs, a much more euphonious name. <i>Ibid.</i> 92 It was sad to have to disillusion these nice people and confess that we rarely saw an oyster, unless we crossed over to the Other Side and saw them in the fish shops of Invercargill. The far-famed Stewart Island oysters are dredged from Foveaux Strait ... <i>(1964,Little World of Stewart island,6</i>)

 <i>n.</i> an Australian

>  The word other sider has never been in use here. <i>(1933,Press, The</i>) [NOTES: Acland]

>  While admitting that the othersiders can do just as they please with N.Z. bowling, the palpable leg-pulling that has gone on so far is irritating. <i>(Mar 5, 1910,NZ Truth,3</i>)

## our muttons

## ourisia
 
 <i>n.</i> [<i>Ourisia macrocarpa</i>]

>  These included specimens of the Ranunculus, believed to be Ranunculas Traversii, and of Celmisia Verbascifolia, Senesio Bifestulosus, Ourisia Sessilifolia, Ourisia Macrocarpa, and one or two other plants which it was impossible to recognise by Hooker's Handbook, or even with Mr Beverly's assistance. <i>(Dec 27, 1867,Otago Witness,9</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  In among bits of rocks kept swampy with water are growing .. the pure white ourisia, delicately lovely in shape and colour, two or three blossoms on a slender stem rising from a bed of dark green leaves. <i>(Oct 17, 1900,Otago Witness,68</i>)

>  The great white buttercup of the Southern Alps, handsomest of its genus, grows in profusion, only rivalled in beauty by the marguerite-like flowers of the numerous different kinds of `cotton plant' or mountain daisy [fn Species of <i>Celmisia</i>.]. To this assemblage of plants also belong the New Zealand `Edelweiss' [fn <i>Helichrysum grandiceps </i>] (no mean representative of the Swiss plant [190] from which it derives its name), and the exquisite white-flowered ourisias [fn <i>Ourisia macrocarpa</i> and <i>macrophylla</i>.], ... <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,188</i>)

>  <b>DENNIS</b> In the spring a number of ourisias flower on the Paparoa tops, including the mountain foxgloves <i>O. macrocarpa</i> and <i>O. macrophylla</i> (the latter of which also grows in the lowlands). <i>(1981,Paparoas Guide,165</i>)

>  New Zealand Alpine Flowering Plants, packed and delivered free on rail .. Ourisia (3 varieties) ... <i>(May 7, 1896,Otago Witness,18</i>)

>  Your specimens arrived in fine order, are very highly prized, and for which accept best thanks. No.1 is ligusticum aromaticum; 2, ourisia glandulosa ... <i>(Feb 20, 1886,Otago Witness,8</i>)

>  There were keas everywhere.. with threevery wicked ones having a contest to seewhich could bite off the most ourisiastems. <i>(1933,Canterbury Mountaineer, The,II/40</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Ourisia Colensoi </i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Ourisia Colensoi </i> <i>(1908,AJHR</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Survey Tongariro National Park]

## out
 
 <i>adv.</i> (Rural) [of dairy cows] not being milked

>  <b>NELSON</b> The cows are not out for the worst of the winter. <i>(1999,Eugene Nelson Glossary,3</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> an out-settler (See [out-settler](../O#out-settler))

>  Red tape, we `outs' call it. <i>(1860,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/140</i>)



 
 <i>adv.</i> (out [place name] way) a vague directional reference to a near distant district [Jim writes : Additional illustrations of the NZ use of "out" as a preposition are from the <i>Wairarapa Times-Age</i> 5/5/99 - "out the back of the race" - and "...when the train comes out the [Rimutaka] tunnel..."; <i>Pacific Waves 3/00. And I have recently heard someone in the office of a club say "the card is out the bar."]

>  <b>FULLARTON</b> ... a milk-run out Henderson way. <i>(1944,Troop Target,24</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> Alan didn't have a bad life in Dunedin, probably because a good deal of it was out the point, at Puratanu. `I've got a wee crib there. Get over as often as I can. ... I've always liked the old shellfish, the gulls and herons. <i>(1985,G'day Country,171</i>)



 
 <i>adv.</i> obs out-district 1835, - farm 1805, - settlement1805, - settler 1802, - squatter 1847 2. comb at a distance from principal establishment of a rural property out-camp 1905. - camper 1900, - hut 1873, - padock 1886 -->[OUT elliptic. for `out in (to) the back country] A. av remote from major centre of population 1875 (e.g. `which led `out back'') B. aj pertaining to ... remote parts of the country 1896 C. n. a. sparsely inhabited country remote from a major centre of population 1893 b. great Australian outback 1936]



 
 <i>adv.</i>

>  They entered the bonds a few years back, and have a child now two and a-half years of age, which is `out,' or, in other words, is being reared in some institution <i>(Dec 18, 1909,NZ Truth,234/6/3</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> (See [cf. outer n](../O#outer-n))

>  `Out' bettors tore their hair and swore -/ The `ins' were in the sun! <i>(May 6, 1917,NZEF Chronicles,137</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> 1. in local use: in combinations, away from, outside the bounds of settlement; the main homestead (station) etc. -->see OUT-HUT, -LIFE, SETTLEMENT, -SETTLER, -SHEPHERD, -STATION. cf OED outside adj 2b Austr. 1881, 1885 `situated without the line of settlement;in the bush.

## out the monk
 
 <i>phr.</i> in the unknown desert, up the blue [ca 1940 North Africa (Arthur Helm)]



 
 <i>phr.</i> incapacitated

>  <i>Thirteen List:</i> Army medical list showing personnel stricken by palsy, out the monk, or otherwise sick and ailing. <i>(1946,Johnny Enzed in Italy</i>)

>  <b>DAVIN</b> Let Jerry plug away till he's sick of it and out the monk for petrol <i>(1947,For the Rest of Our Lives,331</i>)



 
 <i>phr.</i> disabled in various ways, unconscious, blind drunk, had it

>  Our water-bottles had it (what's known as "out the monk"). <i>(Aug 21, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/165 : 5</i>)

>  I'm dashed near out the monk. <i>(Aug 28, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/166 : 4</i>)

>  Then how about the Bulgarians?... They tell me they are out the monk, too. <i>(Sep 4, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/167 : 4</i>)

>  Everything's not roses When trucks are "out the monk." <i>(Oct 23, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/174 : 5</i>)

>  I see you missed it this week and you're out the monk. <i>(Dec 18, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/182 : 4</i>)

>  When jokers go out the monk on vodka.. hey have to build an inacke round them before they wake up. <i>(Feb 5, 1945,NZEF Times,IV/189 : 4</i>)

>  Without a [flit-gun] you're out the monk. <i>(Oct 30, 1944,NZEF Times,5</i>)

>  `I got the NZEF Times today,he said' said the bloke, `and I see you two spruikers have had it ... I see you missed it this week and you're out the monk, says this rooter `said the bloke.' I've been waiting for them to give you the bum's rush ... he says' <i>(1946,Johnny Enzed in Italy,41</i>)

>  <b>WATSON</b> Tony saw Dick and Fred out the monk under them pine trees by the lake yesterday. <i>(1965,Stand in the Rain,135</i>)

>  When jokers go out the monk on vodka they have to build an inacke around them before they wake up. <i>(Feb 5, 1945,NZEF Times,45/4</i>)

>  Take Joe's [sc Stalin's] boys, for instance. ... All out the monk on vodka sta sera, I'll bet. <i>(1945,Sixes & Sevens,7</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> And those drunken sprees helped the others to forget. But no-one ever saw him out the monk. No-one ever saw Mick paralytic. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,135</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Play is stopped when one of the forwards is seen to be out the monk but they soon bring him round and down goes the scrum again. <i>(1970,On the Ball,136</i>)

>  We trust you will promptly deny anyallegations that our disappearance fromyour esteemed journal is due to the factthat we are (a) out the monk (b) absentwithout leave (c) in the boob, and (d) onthe snorer. <i>(May 21, 1945,NZEF Times,IV/204 : 5</i>)

>  Every time it was necessary to put him[sc. a pig] in the box for a move he wasput "out the monk" by a hyperdermic [sic]injection of morphia. <i>(Dec 24, 1945,NZEF Times,IV/235 : 8</i>)

>  <b>MORRIESON</b> It's Jack Voot, real shickered. Looks like he's out the monk. <i>(1976,Pallet on the Floor,69</i>)

## out-district
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an isolated farming settlement far from a town or city.

>  Perch have been distributed in the out districts. <i>(May 8, 1908,Wanganui Herald,7</i>)

>  We have not an easy means of communication with our out-districts. <i>(Aug 27, 1864,Weekly News,10</i>)

>  ... the sale of spirits on the two gallon system. This I consider to be the chief curse of the out districts. <i>(Jun 9, 1858,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/408,1960 ed.</i>) [NOTES: (J. Morgan)]

>  The sale of spirits on the two-gallon system. This I consider to be the curse of the out-districts. <i>(1858,BLANK ENTRY,408</i>) [NOTES: in Scholefield (1960) Richmond-Atkinson Papers I]

>  Returns from the out districts of the electorate are not to hand but the pool is certain to be less than half the roll number and will therefore be inoperative. <i>(Mar 27, 1894,West Coast Times,4</i>)

>  It is situated on St. John's Hill and commands a view of the whole of the town and out districts. <i>(May 8, 1911,Grey River Argus,5</i>)

>  Our out-district subscribers are requested to communicate with the respective agents of the districts in the event of the delivery of thier papers being attended by any irregularity. <i>(Apr 17, 1873,Daily Southern Cross,2</i>)

## out-hut
 
 <i>n.</i> [(station obs)]

>  <b>JONES</b> Nowadays, I believe most of the runs have out-huts built to save the trouble of shifting the tents etc. <i>(1933,Autobiography,60</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) out-station or whare far from the station homestead.

>  John McKenzie .. gave evidence of having left a hut near to the home station, about the end of May last, to reside temporarily at another out-hut on the station. <i>(Jul 28, 1883,Tuapeka Times,2</i>)

>  Mourie paid tribute to the huge effort put in by his tight five yesterday, who, to use a popular phrase, "outpassioned" their Waikato opposites... The tight I've and loosies really stood up and put in a huge effort which did us proud. <i>(Oct 17, 1999,Sunday Star Times,B1</i>)

## out-station
 
 <i>n.</i> formerly, an outlying bush farm. spg occas. outstation; a homestead (or living quarters) on a partof a sheep-station distant from the main homestead; asubsidiary station

>  <b>BIDWILL</b> I had not been there long before I was joined by a native called Peter, who had been left by MrChapman to superintend the building of a house, which the natives are putting up for him, and which he will use as an out-station to visit every two or three months, just as the clergymen do in England with their distant flocks. <i>(1841,Rambles in NZ,62,1952 ed.</i>)

>  Captain Thomas ... & I breakfasted at Rhodes's; afterwards pulled across the harbour ... to Mt. Pleasant, an out-station of Rhodes; ... <i>(Dec 15, 1848,Torlesse Papers,40,1958 ed.</i>)

>  We have two brothers, shepherds, at our out-station <i>(1850,Notes on NZ,9</i>) [NOTES: `Sheep farmer NearWellington]

>  Shelter will be found at the `out-station' of Teburnburn, belonging to the owner of the Tuitrata domain <i>(1853,Australian & NZ Gazette,71/223</i>)

>  <b>FITTON</b> ... the pastoral stations north of Otago extend ... until they meet the stock-owners' out-stations South of Canterbury. <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),181</i>)

>  Went to out-station <i>(Jan 3, 1856,St Leonard's Journal</i>)

>  <b>FULLER</b> Out-stations are started for the shepherds, who watch separate flocks, to live in. <i>(1859,Five Years' Residence in New Zealand,157</i>)

>  <b>MALING</b> [The land] is not [necessary to me] now though in a few years time the spot would suit me well for an out station. <i>(1960,Samuel Butler,56</i>) [NOTES: 1861 Oct 13 Butler to Tripp in 1960 Maling Samuel Butler 56]

>  I went over to the outstation <i>(1861,McLean Papers,20/8</i>) [NOTES: Condie]

>  <b>BUTLER</b> As your mob increases, you can put an out-station on the other side of the run. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,X/150</i>)

>  [We] went for a ride to take sugar and tea to an out-station. <i>(Jul 28, 1865,Chudleigh Diary,195,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BARKER</b> ... we stopped at one of our shepherd's huts, technically called an out station, and accepted his offer of luncheon. <i>(1866,Station Life in NZ,79,1870 ed.</i>)

>  <b>KENNAWAY</b> Turned out to be shepherd [sic] from the nearest out-station to us, (about thirty miles distant), who had pushed back in search of lost sheep. <i>(1874,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,68</i>)

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> There are two woolsheds on the station: one at the homestead and the other at the outstation, where shearing commenced this season with the wether flock. <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,42,1935 ed.</i>)

>  <b>JOLLIE</b> Keeping the out-stations supplied with stores. <i>(1879,Reminiscences,49</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> ... Gilbert decided to send Mike ... , as he had the bullock dray already loaded ... to take over to the out-station, and both ... thought that Mike could be trusted to keep the boundary in the mean time; ... <i>(1881,Waitaruna,144</i>)

>  <b>POTTS</b> [The kea] has discovered the out-stations of some of the back-country settlers. Of course, every station has a meat-gallows. <i>(1882,Out in the Open</i>) [NOTES: quoted in 1895 NZ Reader 84]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Out-station, n. a sheep or cattle station away from the Head Station (q.v.). [1844] <i>(1898,Austral English,332</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The runholder was also allowed a pre-emptive right of purchase on his run of eighty acres for his principal station and ten acres for each out-station. This concession ... proved later to interfere with the fuller settlement of the country, as the runholder, in his phrase, `picked the eyes out.' <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,168</i>)

>  To facilitate the working of the station, there were three places where the hands resided. The Homestead, ... the chief one, was at the end of the station nearest civilisation. About eight miles further back was a cottage, known as the `Out-Station' ... [Further back] the Ruahine whare ... This whare was therefore the furthest back habitation ... It was erected for the accommodation of shepherds when mustering the back-blocks. <i>(Apr 1904,NZ Illustrated Magazine,48</i>)

>  <b>GASCOYNE</b> ... but I usually reached an out-station or a shepherd's whare before dark, and was always welcome to a meal and the loan of an empty wool-pack to spread on the earthern floor byway of a bed [Hawkes Bay, c1855] <i>(1916,Soldiering in NZ,8</i>)

>  Out-station . - A separate establishment where men live and there is usually an overseer, and where part of the station work is carried out. There were never many o.ss. in Canterbury, though The Levels had two and several other stations one. In Australia (whence, of course, the word came) they seem to call any hut with yards an os. ([1898 Morris Austral-English], Butler First Year ], p. 132) <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>NEWTON</b> (gloss) Some high country stations are so extensive that it is necessary to have a second homestead situated at some distant part of the property. Such a place is termed an `out-station'. On Molesworth station they even had a sub-manager at the out-station (Tarndale) in the early days. <i>(1947,Wayleggo</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The custom in the old days was to call the buildings on a run `the station,' and the land that went with it was `the run.' On a big property you could find several `stations,' and the principal one was called `the home station,' while the others were `out-stations.' This was the old Australianusage which was carried over to New Zealand at first, but gradually the signification was altered by loosely calling the whole run a station, and then the real station became a homestead and the out-stations became shepherds' huts. In this present book it will be noticed that generally thewriter has preserved the olden custom of calling the main buildings the station and the land the run. The runs over in Australia being, as a rule, much larger than those in New Zealand, the `station' and `out-station' phraseology was preserved much longer than over here, where even inadvertisements in the late 'fifties we see the English word `homestead' ousting the colonial word `station.' <i>(1947,Early Runholding in Otago,11</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> In that case, there will be a main station and one or two out- stations with huts for musterers and perhaps even a permanent cook or married man. <i>(1951,High Country,11</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> Having no horse, I decided to go by car to Jollie's Pass and walk with my swag to Tarndale, Molesworth's out-station, about forty miles from Hanmer. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,2</i>)

>  outstation. A subsidiary sheep or cattle station away from the `head station'. <i>(1958,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),286,8th ed.</i>)

>  out-station n. station distant from head-station. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621</i>)

>  out-station n. station distant from head-station. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> From the homestead, out at the coast, it was a full sixty miles to the far boundary. ... Access to the out-station ... was by pack-track only ... . <i>(1973,Big Country,85,1977 ed.</i>)

>  <b>FERNANDEZ</b> Each week ... Bessie packed up stores for the cooks at the two out-stations, for the rabbiters' camp and for the station cook-house. <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,15</i>)

>  outstation n. NZ & Austral. sheep or cattle station distant from head station; remote position. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,533</i>) [NOTES: cf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 803]

>  <b>NESFIELD</b> [We were] sent to learn the art of sheep-farming at different out-stations. At these out-stations are shepherds and boundary-riders stationed. <i>(1881,Chequered Career,32</i>)

>  <b>ELWORTHY</b> .. the cottage which was built as an outstation residence for the original Holme Station taken up by Edward Elworthy in 1856. <i>(1988,Through the Looking Glass,114</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Return relative to the out Stations of Resident Magistracy. <i>(Nov 25, 1853,Daily Southern Cross,3</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a dwelling set at a considerable distance from the main station homestead and buildings

The result of the meeting was so unsatisfactory that he considered the charge should be called the Waiareka, regarding Maerewhenua as an out-station. <i>(Dec 9, 1875,North Otago Times,2</i>)

## outback
 
 <i>adv.</i> ?separable constr.: also WAY OUT BACK

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> Now I've been around some stations way out back upon the hills,/ <i>(1909,Ballads about Business,43</i>)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> Mark felt a real sympathy for some of the men and women out back in the high country - where runs ran right up to the glaciers ... <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,236</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Gray and I had been out back and, as usual, had lost track of time. We had sat down for a smoke and a chat under a good-sized specimen of kanaka. <i>(1971,Plekhov Place, The,26</i>)



 
 <i>adv.</i>

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> Seven ninths of the Mindorie men had been born out-back, and bred in the hill-country <i>(1904,Sons o' Men,2</i>)

>  Curious things take place outback. <i>(1915,Countess of Liverpool's Gift Book,40</i>) [NOTES: 1915 Ethel Jones (in) C. of Liverpool's Gift Book]

>  [title] `It'll be jest an ordinary sod whare.' `But you made it, dearest.' `And you won't haveno folk anywheres near yer.' ... `Yer don't know the life of a woman out-back. ...' He ... went away ... to the home that he had built her. It stood on a new-leased Government block, ... <i>(1930,NZ Short Stories,164</i>) [NOTES: G.B.Lancaster `In the Down-Country']

>  <b>MASON</b> (Youth at the Dance) The lone hand digging gum/ [and chck] the starving bushie outback/ <i>(1936,Collected Poems,85,1975 ed.</i>)

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> There is a certain amount of danger in our work, but what looms more important in our lives outback is boredom, or a shortage of tobacco. <i>(1952,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,23</i>)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> In the hotel at Martinborough, Ellis was heard [in 1903] to openly say: `Hold hard; I'll meet that so and so Collinson outback sometime, then I'll do for him.' <i>Ibid.</i> 200 However, an old boar will still occasionally drift in from the virgin bush country outback, and make its headquarters, while he lasts, in a place called Boar Gully <i>(1960,Back-Country Tales,142</i>)

>  <b>FERNANDEZ</b> Weeks out mustering without a packman-cook, crowded ... in the little smoky huts out-back. <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,2</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i> remote, isolated

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> In my recent outback explorations I have met with scenes which horrified ... and brought home to me ... the imperative necessity ... that it be a criminal offence, punishable with hard labour imprisonment, to practise tohungaism, boast of tohungaistic reputation, apply to a tohunga for illegal operations, and learning the incantations, ... <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,262</i>)

>  <b>BARR</b> From the North Cape to the Bluff there is scarcely a spot ... in which the game is not played. Many of these outback townships are not connected by railway ... <i>(1908,British Rugby Team in Maoriland,163</i>)

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> Availing ourselves of one of Cook's excursions to the outback country behind Nice, we motored forty-six miles to Vesubie ... <i>(1912,Impressions of a NZ Pastoralist on Tour,146</i>)

>  Their carts full of groceries scoured the country ... supplying the wants of the outback settlers. <i>(1914,History of North Otago,111,1978 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ANTHONY</b> If you searched the outback district, from the Three Kings to the Snares, I doubt if a worse built whare than that could have been found. <i>(1975,Follow the Call,112</i>) [NOTES: a1927 Anthony Dave Baird in Follow the Call (1975)]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> One I remember, carved with a pocket knife during spare time at outback camps, depicted a deer's head in the centre of a shield and below it a chamois standing on a rock. <i>(1952,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,26</i>)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> ... but road or bridge could be life or death to an outback settler. <i>(1958,Making of a New Zealander, The,17</i>)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> Neither closing hours or age limits counted for much in outback hotels of those days... ...,[there] were the packmen, waggoners, navvies of the road construction gangs, shearers, wool-scourers, shepherds, wild dog and pig hunters, and other out-back workers of those wild colonial times. <i>(1959,Tales of the Mails,50</i>)

>  <b>TUWHARE</b> ... glad that the stuff of poetry ... can be given form by the pen of a Maori boilermaker on an outback construction job. <i>(1977,No Ordinary Sun,7,3rd ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1963 Feb 8 R.A.K.Mason in1977 Tuwhare No Ordinary Sun]

>  <b>McLAGLAN</b> Air Travel Limited had inaugurated a modest but adventurous service for those outback settlers south of Franz Josef. <i>(1965,Stethoscope & Saddlebags,172</i>)

>  <b>WATSON</b> All sorts of characters used to gather there, hunters and miners, ringers from outback stations. <i>(1986,Address to a King,59</i>)

>  Yet good work is done in these out-back schools, and even the very furthest out have libraries, museums, concerts, and gardens. <i>(Feb 16, 1906,Wanganui Herald,4</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Last week, a witness who came from outback, in answer to this query, said, "Then we went and had a pot" - . <i>(Dec 27, 1924,NZ Truth,6</i>)

>  A bushman ... `look here', said the bushie ... said the man from the outback. <i>(Nov 11, 1905,NZ Truth,21/7/1</i>)

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> <i>(1928,Where the White Man Treads,300</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> That was the notice the other day in a King Country paper. `Ladies, a basket' is a customary dance-night formula in the outback, and a convenient arrangement it is. Those liberal baskets ofhome-made dainties outdo anything you can buy for the money in your town shops. <i>(1935,Hero Stories of NZ,158</i>)

>  Two new-fangled literary expressions, out back and backblocks, are driving back-country outof newspaper use. `The out-back' was, I think, coined by the `Bulletin' writers, and `back- blocks' is from the North Island. <i>(Sep 9, 1933,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> That wouldn't have mattered, for the town, to which many bushfellers and men from the outback came when they drew their pay cheques, was accustomed to small troubles. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,145</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> No one can really give you the pleasure to be found in the `outback' by describing it. <i>(1952,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,194</i>)

>  <i>(1963,Moderna Sprak,LVII 8</i>)

>  <b>McNAUGHTON</b> Bluey was a man who loved the outback. He could name every tree and shrub, and get the wild birds to eat out of his hand. <i>(1970,Tat,86</i>)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> Today the remoteness of the outback is fading. <i>(1973,Historic Sheep Stations NI,99</i>)

>  She loved the outback, the huts, ... the hills, ... bush, its deer. <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,24</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [(cf also uses in av)]

>  <b>ROGERS</b> `Hal. When did you blow in? ... I told you to stay in the outback till you got all those crossbreeds.' <i>(1960,Long White Cloud, The,13</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) [of large stations] furthest from the main station buildings.

>  <b>MILLS ET AL</b> Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock .. in late October 8000 wethers were mustered into the outback yards for fat-drafting. [out-back] <i>(1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,119</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [of property] isolated.

>  .. it was a decided advantage to have an 'out-back' run, as it was less likely to be exposed to infection. <i>(Mar 1947,NZ Journal of Agriculture,251</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Te Hoe, which bounds on to Ngatapa at Te Kooti's Lookout, is another of the real outback stations. <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,195</i>)

>  It may be true that all circuses are the same circus, but the buck-jumpers are a distinctive variation. The performance was pre-eminently practical; it was the sort of thing that the budding squatter might hope to go through out-back some day. <i>(Jun 26, 1907,Hawera & Normanby Star,5</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> ... ,or the Bohemians who founded Puhoi (the name of which, corrupted, is apparently the origin of the slang term `the Boo-ay', a synonym for `the out-backs'). <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,97</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> a person from the OUTBACK

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> I asked a farthest outback his tenure : `Well,' and he lingered on the `well,' `mine is a perpetual lease ... ' <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,295</i>)



## outclip

## outcrop
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McARA</b> Outcrop: The exposed portion of a reef, where it projects above the ground. The Martha Reef was the only one in the Martha Hill area which outcropped, the Royal coming in at No 4 level and the Edward at about No 5 level. OED In Mining and Geol. The cropping out or emergence of a stratum or vein at the surface; or the edge of the stratum or vein that thus crops out [cf the second use, in the quot. above] <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328</i>)

>  The geological map was made by using as control points the creeks and streams, ... the position and extent of shallow underground mine workings, and their connections with the surface (locally known as `outcrops'), and surveyed outcrops of coal. <i>(1930,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,12:130</i>) [NOTES: (Penseler-Pukemiro dist.)]

## outlaw
 
 <i>n.</i> a recalcitrant sheep [(farmers')]

>  <b>MASTERS</b> On the way over with the wethers Fred and Hermit saw and ignored each other, as had been their custom since Fred's one and only attempt to muster the outlaw in with other sheep some time previously. <i>(1960,Back-Country Tales,125</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a difficult horse to break in or catch.

>  <b>KEENE</b> Not all horses were broken in as easily as Ruby was. Sometimes Father would have what he called an outlaw, and then sterner measures, much more time and unending patience were necessary. <i>(1978,Mimiwhangata Station,49</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) an animal that does not conform.

>  A cow that is difficult to handle: an outlaw. <i>(2001,Questionnaire Respondents (DB)</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a horse with a tendency to buck or pigjump.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> This pony Glen had gone dirty and was a real little outlaw. <i>(1949,Wayleggo,69</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. I might explain that at a rodeo "good" horses are bad horses - outlaws that are chock-full of fight and dirt and buck. <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,50</i>)

>  <b>WATT</b> On those free Sundays we would also run the horses in from the range where the young ones were, and the old mothers, and the three-year-olds that hadn't been ridden, and horses that had bucked riders and been turned out again to become outlaws. <i>(1990,Wild Horses & Me,87</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> motorcycle gang member [[Is this form a specific gang name.]]

>  <b>NEWBOLD</b> OUTLAW (n) Motorcycle gang member. <i>(1982,Big Huey, The,252</i>)

## outrigger
 
 <i>n.</i> an electrified wire attached to a conventional fence; an electrified wire placed away from the main fence but attached to it [(farming)]

>  Outrigger: An electrified wire attached to a conventional fence. Supported so it is away from the fence. <i>Ibid.</i> 29 Outrigger: An electrified wire placed away from the main fence but attached to it. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,26</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BARRY</b> We had two horses in the cart, one running in what is called an `out-rigger', and a large board or sort of tray nearly covered the top of the cart. <i>(1879,Some Ups and Downs in NZ&A,219</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a wire construction beside or on top of a fence to prevent bulls, horses and other stock from reaching over or trampling the fence.

>  <b>MAF</b> outrigger: an electrified piece of wire placed away from the main fence but attached to it. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,29</i>)

>  Part of the cost of running bull beef has been an elaborate electric fencing system with outriggers on all fences and gates. <i>(1987,NZ Journal of Agriculture,Jan/Feb 7</i>)



## outrun
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a part of a dog-trial event.

>  The competitor may move about within the 'quad' [A] to work his dog during the outrun, head and pull, to allow the sheep to enter between points B-B. [outrun] <i>(1991,Constitution of NZ Dog Trial Assoc,39</i>)

>  <b>MCGREGOR</b> Class I is the outrun and head, pull and hold in the ring. <i>(1980,Dog's Life, A,58</i>)

>  The Long Head or Long Pull, consisting of Outrun to head, Pull, and Hold in the ring. <i>(1991,Constitution of NZ Dog Trial Assoc,33</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a movement of a sheepdog when directed to move around a mob of sheep.

>  <b>MAF</b> It [the dog] fails to stop after its out-run, halfway around the sheep, and directly to its handler. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,25</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Run-out: The act of running out to head sheep. Outrun - the manner in which the dog runs out. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,169</i>)

>  <b>MILLS ET AL</b> 'Crossing his line' is another bad fault. It occurs when a dog at some point in his outrun crosses over an imaginary straight line between you and the sheep ... <i>(1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,34</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. I made it my business to see this dog working at the Waikoau trials the following day. But for the fact that his outrun was excessively wide he put up a good run ... <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,195</i>)



## outside
 
 <i>adv.</i> esp. GO OUTSIDE, to go outside the Sounds into the Strait; beyond the Entrance [ca1941 on the Tamahine `It's pretty rough outside. Go easy on the fizz.]



## outside beat
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the longest and most onerous mustering area, circumnavigating a block boundary.

>  <b>STACEY</b> I had been given the outside beat which meant I had to circumnavigate the entire block of several thousand acres while the others were spaced out to go through the middle of the block. <i>(1996,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,104</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> All the sheep on this side of the range had to be mustered right over the top and the outside man had a beat looming with trouble. <i>(1949,High Country Days,27</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> The beat on the top of the hill is known as the 'top beat' and the one along the bottom as the 'bottom beat.' This is rather obvious - but the longest and most strenuous is known as the 'outside beat.' <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,24</i>)

>  <b>MCMILLAN</b> The man who takes the outside beats is weather burnt and trim. <i>(2000,Country Bloke and Other Verse, The,249</i>)



## outside hole
 
 <i>n.</i> [(goldminers')]

>  <b>GORDON</b> If gold was found in flat valleys the lead was always supposed to follow their course; whereas in many instances it went right across their course: and while experienced diggers were engaged in sinking prospecting holes and shafts up and down the valley, a new chum, who had never see agoldfield before, would often come and put down what was termed an `outside hole', and strike the lead as rich as ever. <i>(1889,Miner's Guide, A,72</i>)



## outside man
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BILLING & MANNERING</b> The puramid-shaped polar tent is pitched and the "inside man" begins to cook the evening meal while the "outside man" feeds the dogs their frozen blocks of pemmican, carefully keeping the old magazines put inside the pemmican tins as packing, back in New Zealand. <i>(1964,South : Man and Nature in Antarctica,71</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a musterer with a long and onerous beat around the boundary of a block.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> All the sheep on this side of the range had to be mustered right over the top and the outside man had a beat looming with trouble. <i>(1949,High Country Days,27</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. when mustering that country the outside man actually tramped the ridge of the Divide itself. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,204</i>)

>  <b>STACEY</b> The outside man went down the side of Lake Tennyson to the Clarence. <i>(1996,Tales of a Tussock Jumper,70</i>)



## outski
 
 <i>adv.</i> (informal) an extension of out

>  From the former SIS Inspector General with his "outski" comments to the Immigration Service officers who obstructed the Ombudsman's inquiry, we have seen a whole parade of officials displaying a damning lack of respect for their democratic duties. <i>(Dec 18, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7</i>)

>  If that wasn't the case, then she wouldn't have a decision. I'd be making my decision and it would be 'outski' on the next plane. <i>(Nov 29, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),23</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> My Olaf is too independent, he won't crawl to him so he's outski. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,155</i>) [NOTES: not in OED]

>  If Winston won the auction, it would be outski on the next plane for Ahmed Zaoui and who knows how many other refugees, immigrants and any other Tom, Dick or Ali who had in some way displeased Himself. <i>(May 15, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),15</i>)

>  ...while Inspector-General of the SIS, he observed that Mr Zaoui should be "outski". a remark the High Court decided ruled him out of reviewing the Security Intelligence Service's information on Mr Zaoui. <i>(Nov 27, 2004,Dominion Post,B4</i>)

## over
 
 <i>adv.</i> [also Chathams and Stewart Isds usage]

>  He [a convict] came over to New Zealand, and at once commenced sticking-up in Otago. <i>(Apr 18, 1868,Marlborough Express,4</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> = winter over [As in "he overed with the winter party" - heard in a radio documentary on Antarctica in Spectrum;]



## over the (shearing) board
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) across the shearing board [used with reference to the shearing of sheep, or the use of the shearing board to crutch or to examine sheep].

>  Last year this one contractor shore 40,000 sheep in the spring, and this season he has more than that to 'put over the boards.' <i>(Sep 1, 1949,NZ Farmer,55</i>)

>  <b>STEVENS</b> Muster the whole flock and put each sheep over the shearing board. <i>(1958,Sheep.  Part 1, Sheep husbandry,118</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. everything is double-shorn and including lambs over 30,000 sheep go over the board. <i>(1972,In the Wake of the Axe,186</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. about 22,000 are shorn, and of these anything up to 18,000 are first tail-trimmed over the board. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,136</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> We put seven thousand over the board. <i>(1973,Boot in the Stirrup,74</i>)

>  WoolPro strongly recommends to growers and shearers that they raddle struck sheep (on the head) as they go over the board, then treat them afterwards in the pens outside the shed. <i>(2000,Wool Grower,Spring 5</i>)



## over the fence
 
 <i>phr.</i> over the top, unreasonable. [cf. 1984 14 F E131 Wgton Girls C. 7 OFF THE FENCE [something not good]]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Fence, over the. (Of a person) unashamed, scandalous; greedy; very unreasonable: New Zealanders' coll. variation (late C. 19-20) of S.E. beyond the pale. Perhaps ex local rules for cricket. <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,271</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> over the fence unacceptable behaviour, which Partridge thinks from local rules for cricket; eg `Attacking the Finance Minister in Parliament was one thing, but burning his book in front ofthe television cameras was, for most of his colleagues, a bit over the fence.' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,80</i>)

>  The handicapping of Holly Boy at Marlborough was a bit over the fence. <i>(Mar 17, 1923,NZ Truth,12</i>)



## over the hill
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> to or in Canterbury [West Coast use]

>  <b>PEARSON</b> over the hill: West Coast expression for `across the Southern Alps', that is, in Canterbury province. <i>(1963,Coal Flat,421</i>)

>  King Bait. One thing everybody does know about the Coast is bait. White bait. ... Tons ... are railed and flown over the hill, weekly. <i>(1977,Listener Short Stories,118</i>) [NOTES: 1974 Hulme]

>  <b>HULME</b> I was newly-arrived from Christchurch and ... was well-content to get [whitebait] 40 cents cheaper the pound than the family over the Hill. <i>(1986,Te Kaihau,38</i>)

>  This gets him into trouble with snotty conservationists from "over the hill" (Coast-speak for the rest of the South Island), who want to have him prosecuted for plundering a protected species of tree. <i>(Oct 8, 2000,Sunday Star Times,F2</i>)

>  <b>McGill</b> over the hill Canterbury, to West Coasters. <i>(1989,Dinkum Kiwi Dictionary, The,76</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>phr.</i> to Christchurch over the Port Hills from Lyttelton, and vice versa.

>  Many houses to let in consequence of some persons having gone to the diggings - others over the hill (as they call it) into the Plains. <i>(Feb 3, 1853,Journal of Henry Sewell,1/123,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MITCHELL</b> I got over the hill to Port Lyttelton, and got a job keeping a tip-head on some reclamation work that was going on there. <i>(1889,Rhymes and Rambles,19</i>)

>  Over the hill.-In the earliest days to go o.t.h. simply meant to go to Lyttelton; but in later years it meant `to be sent to gaol' at Lyttelton. <i>(Jan 27, 1934,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Gloss. additions]

>  <b>MAY</b> Vessels arriving at Lyttelton from Dunedin added their quota, the diggers `proceeding immediately over the Hill upon landing at the jetty'. <i>(1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,114,1967 ed.</i>)

>  <b>LANGDALE-HUNT</b> I eventually rode over the hill to Lyttelton and the ship after dinner on Monday but I was not allowed to put the horse and dogs aboard ... <i>(1985,Last Entail Male,72</i>)



## over the hooks
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  Baconer pigs (those weighing over 32 kg) are auctioned at saleyards to representatives of the meatworks - they are also sold directly to the works, on a per-kg basis `over the hooks'. <i>(1974,NZ Agriculture,188</i>)



## over the platform
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) to dip sheep using a plunge or pot dip.

## over the plimsoll
 
 <i>phr.</i> drunk
