>  James Fairlie was arraigned before the Bench on a charge of loading himself with beer over the Plimsoll mark. <i>(Nov 20, 1909,NZ Free Lance,4</i>)



## over the range(s)
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  Of the other works, we have noticed from time to time the progress which is being made with the road over the ranges to connect this district with the East Coast, and it is now our good fortune to announce that the traffic road from Omahu to Ohinemuri will be pushed on with all speed. <i>(Sep 14, 1880,Waikato Times,2</i>)

>  He was carried over the ranges to Coromandel hospital. <i>(Aug 9, 1899,Marlborough Express,2</i>)

>  The recommendations of the engineer will be supplemented by a special report of the Mayor (Mr. J. P. Luke) and Councillor A. H. Hindmarsh, who accompanied the City Engineer on a recent expedition over the range to the Orongorongo. <i>(Apr 9, 1915,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  The height of the pass is about 2,000 feet; but the ascents are easy, and with proper care and judgement a bridle path might be carried over the range at no very great expense. <i>(Jan 13, 1844,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,385</i>)

>  [Photo caption] SCENE AT MR M'MINAMIN'S SHEEP STATION. The bodies of four of the unfortunate victims of the wreck about to be placed on pack horses and carried over the ranges. <i>(Feb 24, 1909,Otago Witness,44</i>)

>  Accompanied by Rhodes ... walked over the ranges to the bay ... called Terape [near the future Christchurch]. Arrived at Mr Gibie's cattle station at 5 p.m. - cows in abundance. <i>(Dec 16, 1848,Torlesse Papers,40,1958 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> Erewhon or Over the range [title NZNB no.844 1/150] <i>(1872,Erewhon</i>)

>  <b>PINFOLD</b> Many times has the pioneer worker gone on foot from Coromandel over the `range' to Mercury Bay, ... <i>(1930,Fifty Years In Maoriland,65</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> During the early days ..., the only access to Waihi was by bridle track over the range. <i>(1886,Off the Sheep's Back,6</i>)

>  The road over the range is now cut nearly the whole distance, but the report we hear of it is not very favourable, as a bad line for it we are told has been chosen. <i>(Apr 1, 1857,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2</i>)

>  When we say there is a road over these ranges, we wish it to be perfectly understood that we do not mean a made road, or even a beaten track, but only that the country is available from that point. <i>(Jul 6, 1861,Otago Witness,4</i>)

## over there
 
 <i>phr.</i> [cf. Mainland, other side]

We say `mainland', and we do know what's going on over there. We say `over there at New Zealand' as a joke nowadays. <i>(1985,I'm Ninety-five,270</i>) [NOTES: Wattie, Stewart I.]



## over-driving
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) droving sheep for too long at one time.

>  <b>WHYTE</b> One day's over-driving can take more out of sheep than can be recovered by a month of good feeding. <i>(1927,Sheep-Dog, The,166</i>)



## over-eyed
 
 <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of a sheepdog] too strongly eyed [so that many sheep become transfixed by his/her gaze.]

>  <b>WHYTE</b> Over-eyed - this is a weakness, and a dog possessing it has a tendency to draw the sheep on to him (ie they will turn with their heads erect and gaze fixedly at him.) <i>(1927,Sheep-Dog, The,53</i>)

>  <b>WHYTE</b> I emphatically condemn the over-eyed, glowering emotional dog. <i>(1927,Sheep-Dog, The,37</i>)



## over-prime
 
<b>1</b> <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of farm stock] carrying too much fat.

>  Over-prime heifers were being discounted down to $1.45, as local trade buyers were very selective. <i>(Apr 12, 2001,NZ Farmer,41</i>)



## over-run
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [used elsewhere, but recorded first in NZ] the increase in bulk when butterfat is made into butter.

>  Dairy farmers and factory managers are very familiar with the term 'over-run.' <i>(Mar 4, 1888,Hawera Star,2</i>)

 <i>adj.</i>

## overdrill

## overdrive

## overfat
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) an overweight animal, usually for slaughter.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> This, of course, was well before the days of overfats. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,240</i>)

>  Watch those overfats! <i>(Nov 6, 1963,Straight Furrow,13</i>)

>  Overfats are no problem and 28-30 lb lambs have been repeatedly drafted at 12 weeks. 2100 lambs drafted with one overfat. <i>(Nov 7, 1963,NZ Farmer,45</i>)

>  There is far less dagging than you possibly imagine. Overfats are no problem, even at very heavy weights. <i>(Aug 10, 1972,NZ Farmer,95</i>)

>  A mixed yarding ranging from very light to overfats saw fewer good cutting sheep than usual in the yarding of 4700. <i>(Dec 7, 1988,NZ Farmer,36</i>)

>  Recently shorn: Overfats $28-$30 ... <i>(Aug 1, 1996,NZ Farmer,42</i>)

>  Overfats felt the heat of the new season spring lamb competition. <i>(Oct 1, 1998,NZ Farmer,35</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of animals for slaughter] overweight or overprimed.

>  .. It is obvious that extreme emphasis must be laid on the avoidance of overfat lamb production. How much fat can a lamb carcase contain before it is graded overfat? <i>(Feb 1966,NZ Journal of Agriculture,57</i>)

>  If .. the picker has come in a little late, then a high proportion of the lambs will be overfat. Overfat lambs are produced mainly as a result of inefficient farming practices. <i>(Feb 1966,NZ Journal of Agriculture,58</i>)

>  The percentage of over-fat lambs is increasing ... <i>(May 12, 1993,NZ Farmer,43</i>)

>  Heavy overfat ewe lambs $48-$51 ... [over-fat] <i>(Aug 1, 1996,NZ Farmer,42</i>)



## overgraze
 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to allow stock to remain too long on a crop or pasture or to place too many stock in one area.

>  .. extreme care is necessary to see that a newly oversown paddock is not so overgrazed that stock pull out many seedlings and is not so undergrazed that the seedlings are smothered by the growth of established species. <i>(Apr 16, 1956,NZ Journal of Agriculture,362</i>)



## overgrown
 
 <i>adj.</i> fleece wool which is more than 12 months wool growth; If very long = DOUBLE FLEECE [(woolgrowers')]

>  Overgrown: Fleece wool which is more than 12 months wool growth. If very long, may be called double fleece. Sheep with an overgrown fleece are commonly called stragglers because they have missed a muster. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,57</i>)



## overhand stoping

>  <b>McARA</b> Overhand stoping: Working a stope from the bottom upwards, generally in a series of horizontalslices, and frequently using upper holes. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328</i>)



## overhang
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  ... the risk with an overhang party is ... <i>(Jul 5, 2005,Radio NZ / National Radio,Nine to Noon</i>) [NOTES: Linda Clarke interviewing Jane Clifton]



 
 <i>n.</i> of a building, a room built above a garage

>  No sterilising equipment was available for medicine preparation and the rongao were made in a garage overhang using aluminium pots and water from a garden tap. <i>(Nov 4, 2003,Dominion Post,A1</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> electoral situation

>  A victory by Waiariki's Tuariki Delamere.. could result in him becoming the country's first "overhang" MP - upping total seat numbers to 121, Professor Roberts said. An overhang is when a party wins more electorate seats than the total number of seats its share of the party vote entitles it to. <i>(Oct 28, 1999,Evening Post,14</i>)

>  National loses seat after party vote share falls while 'overhang' cut to one by Maori Party <i>(Oct 3, 2005,NZ Herald,A6</i>) [NOTES: [hdg]]

>  There may be insufficient seats to ensure that qualifying parties are represented in Parliament in proportion to their overall party vote support. It may also increase the possibility of an "overhang" and thus reduce the prospect of a truly proportional Parliament. <i>(Nov 27, 1999,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26</i>)

>  ..parliament is almost certainly going to move into overhang (more than 120 seats) for the first time. <i>(Jun 18, 2005,Dominion Post,A7</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [Internet search Feb 2006: 1 citation found in Austral.]

>  "The market is very seriously overstocked with Argentinian, South African and Chilean apples, plus a large overhang of last season's European apples," it said. <i>(Aug 14, 2005,Hawke's Bay Today,D5</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a shelter rock in a mountain area under which musterers, hunters and stock can camp. (See [rock camp](../R#rock-camp))

>  <b>CURTIS</b> Underneath the overhang there was enough room to bed down a dozen people. <i>(1996,Beneath the Mountain Mist,59</i>)

>  <b>PICKERING</b> By including the old tin musterers' huts, the 'dog-kennel' bivouacs, recognised rock bivs, rock overhangs and the odd sea-cave, accommodation of some sort has now run up to almost a thousand sites in the hills. <i>(1988,Hills, The,7</i>)

>  <b>AXBEY</b> The overhang was such that it afforded them protection ... <i>(1991,Along the Track: a collection of NZ hunting yarns,142</i>)

>  <b>KERR</b> Rock paintings under overhangs of limestone and clay still exist. <i>(2000,High Times in the High Country,105</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i>

>  ASB Securities analyst Steven Wright estimated Fonterra would probably be left holding about 8 per cent of Wrightson. That might be considered an overhang and depress the share price. <i>(Jun 24, 2004,Dominion Post,C1</i>)



## overhead
 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) [dog trial heading dog] to run too far around the back of a mob of sheep.

>  <b>GORDON</b> It wasn't a 48 [dog trial score] as she overheaded, which means she went past noon on the clock and landed at 11. <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,111</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a long shaft in a shearing shed from which shearing machines are suspended and driven.

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> Shaddow Waru, the expert, was busy in the engine room getting the Lister running smoothly. He had already oiled the overhead. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,25</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> The overhead was a very long shafting some two inches in diameter which was suspended by some dozen or so bearings. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,17</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> I got it [the engine] lined up for the pulley on the shearing overhead and put on the belt ... <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,147</i>)



## overheads
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WILSON</b> The flour was what was called `overheads' - i.e. only the bran was taken out. <i>(1912,Reminiscences of Early Settlement Dunedin,59</i>)

>  Duncan's flourmills followed M'Glashan's, of Woodhaugh, in busily turning out rough flour called "overheads," from which only the bran was taken out. <i>(Nov 23, 1920,Otago Witness,52</i>)



## overhunting
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [dog trial huntaway dog] placing too much pressure on sheep too soon in a run.

>  Over hunting - Shepherd error in letting a dog hool into sheep as soon as a run's begun and applying too much pressure. <i>(Nov 15, 1983,NZ Journal of Agriculture,21</i>)



## overing
 
 <i>n.</i> = wintering over [Heard on Spectrum 1/3/1999 re Antarctica]



## overland
 
 <i>adv.</i> spec meaning distinguishing land or cross-country travel from sea transport and travel in early NZ

>  Captain Mitchell and Mr Dashwood have lately arrived here overland from Nelson, after a six weeks' trip: they ... appear to have found a very practicable road for driving stock. <i>(Jun 24, 1850,Torlesse Papers,150,1958 ed.</i>)

>  Lance came by steamer ... bringing 125 fat cattle. That is, the cattle are being brought overland and he has come to see about selling. <i>(May 4, 1865,Chudleigh Diary,180,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HAAST</b> Only those diggers went back overland who had not the means to take a passage in a steam or sailing vessel from the West to the East Coast. <i>(1879,Geology of the Provinces of Canterbury & Westland,72</i>)

>  <b>MITCHELL</b> ... I travelled overland to Christchurch on two pounds of bread and two Maori hens. <i>(1889,Rhymes and Rambles,19</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> In May, 1852, Mr. ... Valpy ... made the journey overland from Christchurch in twelve days' hard travelling, with a view to see the carrying capacity of the country and the feasibility of driving stock across it, and the rivers especially. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,150</i>)

>  <b>PFAFF</b> From there [Hyde, Otago] I walked overland to the West Coast, landed in Hokitika in the latter end of 1865, ... <i>Ibid.</i> 146 Hearing at the latter port that the first boat had arrived at Hokitika [1864], but was bar-bound, we decided to travel overland, going via ... Otira. <i>(1914,Diggers' Story, The,111</i>)

>  Arrangements might be made for sending a mail periodically overland to the Bay of Islands. <i>(May 2, 1840,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2</i>)

>  Christchurch. 1st April. The Hon. the Premier arrived overland from Hokitika on Saturday. <i>(Apr 1, 1872,Evening Post,2</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i> (See [also overland mail](../A#also-overland-mail))

>  <b>MUELLER</b> The Overland Route [from Christchurch to Hokitika] was perfectly frightful. <i>(Sep 26, 1865,My Dear Bannie,41,1958 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MAQLING</b> fn This was the first overland trip from Nelson to Banks Peninsula. Though hailed as a feasible stock route it was in fact not so. <i>(1958,Torlesse Papers,148</i>)

>  [Photo caption] Rapids on the Arthur River, on the overland track to Milford Sound. <i>(Dec 19, 1906,Otago Witness,33</i>)

>  Notice of Mails. For Havelock, Picton, and Blenheim, per Overland Carrier, today, at 4.30 p.m. <i>(Oct 9, 1872,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2</i>)

>  Notice of Mails. For Havelock, Picton, and Blenheim, per Overland Carrier, today, at 4.30 p.m. <i>(Oct 9, 1872,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> [mainly or only Australian]

>  overland v.t. take stock across country. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) [sp. use] to drive stock long distances over land.

>  <b>CRESSWELL</b> Mannering decided to try his hand at overlanding. <i>(1951,Canterbury Tales,125</i>)

>  <b>VANCE</b> Fraser found difficulty in securing sheep locally, so he overlanded them from Nelson. <i>(1965,High Endeavour,33</i>)



## overland mail
 
 <i>n.</i> as distinct from sea-mail (?explained in Nelson Examiner ? in McLean Papers: frequent in early journals etc, e.g. McLean Papers) abbrev. o.m.

>  For Wellington and Auckland per the overland mail, this day, at 2 p m[.] <i>(Sep 21, 1882,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  The question of overland mails has been exciting considerable discussion on the East Coast, especially that portion lying north of Ruatorea [sic], and recently a meeting of the Te Araroa branch of the Farmers' Union discussed a letter from the Chief Postmaster at Gisborne, suggesting an alteration on present timetables which would provide a regular three-day service between Gisborne and Te Araroa. <i>(Jul 21, 1920,Poverty Bay Herald,6</i>)

>  OVERLAND MAIL ROUTE. <i>(Oct 12, 1906,Star, The,3</i>)

>  By the overland mail, the answer from Waikato to the Governor's proclamation came in. <i>(1960,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,I/709</i>) [NOTES: 1861 quote]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> ... the overland mail, between the two first of these places [Wellington and Auckland], passes through New Plymouth each way once a fortnight. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,291</i>)

>  Chas and I put up post and rail fencing ... The first Overland Mail to the West Coast went through today. They had to borrow a horse from Enys to go on one stage. <i>(Jul 4, 1865,Chudleigh Diary,192,1950 ed.</i>)



## Overlander
 
 <i>n.</i> the daytime passenger train between Wellington and Auckland

>  That'll be the Overlander, we nodded, like seasoned spotters. <i>(May 23, 2006,Dominion Post,A13</i>)

>  However, the Overlander - the daytime train between Wellington and Auckland - will still run. <i>(Oct 30, 2004,Dominion Post,A1</i>)



## overlander
 
 <i>n.</i> [type of bag]

>  STEEL AND WOOD TRUNKS, Hampers, Overlanders, Gladstone Bag[.] <i>(Nov 22, 1906,Colonist (Nelson),4</i>)

>  The common travelling bag with its rough bulging ends, for instance, is given neat square ends, and a new shape[.] "The Overlander," neat and very accomodating in capacity, is bound to become popular. <i>(Dec 3, 1895,Timaru Herald,3</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> one who drives stock a long distance [(occas. early Canterbury)]

>  He has just formed a sheep station at the Hurunui River as his flocks are becoming too numerous for his runs at Cape Campbell and the Wairarapa. His brother too is now onhis way - the first over-lander with fat sheep for the Canterbury market. <i>(Apr 6, 1851,Torlesse Papers,211,1958 ed.</i>)

>  Mr Clifford has succeeded in driving about 1500 ewes from the Wairau district ... Two other parties of `overlanders' are reported to be close at his heels. <i>(Mar 27, 1852,Lyttelton Times</i>)

>  During the summer of 1852, 3, several more overlanders from Nelson imported sheep and cattle into the Canterbury district; ... <i>(1854,Canterbury Almanac, The,59</i>)

>  One man exclaimed So help me there lies the finest horse that ever crossed the Terahmacow and all echoed him. Another said Matey never own a good horse, another said, Justly you might as well tell an overlander to drown himself at once as ride a bad horse ... Half the township would have shouted for me if I would have drank ... and I was asked all down the river if I was the man that lost a fine horse saying Well mate, I am sorry for you. Fate. <i>(Apr 30, 1865,Chudleigh Diary,179,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> With the exception of Valpy, they were the first white overlanders between Christchurch and Dunedin, and they were the first to cross cattle over the Waitaki river. <i>(1985,Coldstream,4</i>) [NOTES: 1930 Christchurch Press in Studholme Coldstream (1985)]

>  Overlander. - An Australian word for a man driving sheep or cattle a long distance. It was sometimes used in Canterbury in the early days. (Lyttelton Times, March 27 1852, [1898 MorrisAustral-English]) <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  overlander n. (Hist.) traveller who made long expeditions with stock from one colony to another; now anyone driving stock long distances; ... <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),14</i>)

>  Four miles away from the overlander's corpse lay the carcase [sic] of his horse, still saddled and bridled. <i>(Feb 2, 1897,North Otago Times,1</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  15 head Prime Fat Cattle, overlanders, heavy weights, and perfectly quiet. <i>(Jan 4, 1875,Grey River Argus,3</i>)

>  The Wanganui lot were heaviest, but they were rather uneven, while the overlanders, though smaller, were really first-class mutton. <i>(Mar 23, 1887,West Coast Times,2</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) sheep with a matted fleece.

>  <b>MILLS</b> Many farmers, through overstocking and wrong grazing methods, produce flocks containing a fair proportion of 'overlanders', sheep with matted fleeces. It is almost impossible to shear these sheep close to the skin the first time so a shearer goes 'overland' and then goes back for what wool is left. <i>(1960,Sheep-O,99</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a drover or stockowner who drives sheep long distances over land rather than by road.

>  <b>MALING</b> His brother too is now on his way - the first overlander with fat sheep for the Canterbury market. <i>(1851,Torlesse Papers,211</i>) [NOTES: in MALING Torlesse Papers (1958)]

>  Mr A Clifford is driving over about 1500 ewes from the Wairau district, losing one on the road. Two other parties of 'overlanders' are reported to be close at his heels. <i>(Mar 27, 1852,Lyttelton Times,2</i>)



## overnoised
 
 <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of huntaway sheepdogs] noisy to the extreme.

>  A dog you have to wring every single bark out of, with separate commands, isn't a huntaway. An 'overnoised' dog is just as frustrating. <i>(Nov 15, 1983,NZ Journal of Agriculture,20</i>)



## overpubbed
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  [Normanby] is muchly overpubbed. <i>(Dec 28, 1900,Auckland Weekly News,4</i>)



## overrun a head
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [sheepdogs] to make too long an arc when heading sheep.

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> Over-running the head. This is not as serious as short heading providing the dog is quickly manoeuvred into the correct position. <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,71</i>)

>  Matheson's Snow showed great speed but was slightly wide at the top of his cast, and then he slightly overran his head. <i>(Jul 5, 1951,NZ Farmer,32</i>)

>  <b>MILLS ET AL</b> If Boy stops to the right of this line he is said to have 'short-headed.' On the other hand if he goes past the line he has 'overrun his head.' If the sheep is facing right and he continues on to the straight-line position he has also overrun his head. From a distance it is often difficult to be sure if a dog has stopped on the point of balance, or overrun his head or has short-headed. <i>(1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,36</i>)



## overs
 
 <i>n.</i> -

>  The Turks replied by a rattle of musketry fire, and as we drew out to the transports we heard for the last time the plup-plup of the `overs' burying themselves in the water. <i>(1918,New Zealand At the Front,15</i>)



## oversale
 
 <i>n.</i> [(building and architects')]

>  <b>G.T.</b> `Oversales' - a technical term from carpenters' building our new house after the earthquake. In the South Island the same things seem to be called `soffits', pronounced. [s fits] <i>(1931,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1931 G.T. Diary (Dannevirke)]



## oversea
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> Visitors from our oversea dominions, when they come Home, invariably swear that they have in their own little State something better than anything in the same line to be seen in the Old Country. <i>(1912,Impressions of a NZ Pastoralist on Tour,123</i>)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> [not overseas: ?M's idiolect] Indeed, she had grown tired of correcting their ideas about oversea conditions, and accepted them with humorous resignation. <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,294</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>adv.</i>

>  <b>MULGAN</b> They climbed into a four-wheeled buggy drawn by two horses, which a bright-faced cadet (who had failed his army examinations and had been shipped oversea) had driven down the day before. <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,310</i>)



## overseas
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  Call the `Over-seas soldiers' or `Down-under' men ... Call them `Cornstalks' or `Fernleaves' - all out for a fight - But don't call them Anzacs, for that isn't right. <i>(Nov 15, 1916,Chronicles NZEF,127</i>)

>  Before leaving Auckland .. Mr. Evelyn Wrench, honorary organiser of the Overseas Club, expressed satisfaction at the result of his visit to the Dominion. <i>(Feb 27, 1913,Evening Post,3</i>)

>  Overseas Distress Relief Fund. The Wellington Committee of the Poor of Great Britain and Ireland and Belgium Relief Fund acknowledges the following donations to the fund... <i>(Sep 29, 1914,Evening Post,2</i>)



## overseas experience
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [also OE](../A#also-oe))

>  <b>REILLY</b> They were an attractive couple at training college, and their `overseas experience' had made them even more so. They had come back from the years in Britain to learn the maori language ... <i>(1986,Deputy Head,17</i>)

>  <b>OVENDEN</b> O.E., as every New Zealander knows, means Overseas Experience. The term refers, as in such colloquial phrases as: `He's gone to get his O.E., eh,'; or `Got your O.E. yet then?' to that most rare of concepts, an abstract consumer durable. It has little in common with the Australian vernacular `O.S.', which is a mere descriptive term, as in `He's gone O.S. and won't come back.' <i>(1986,O.E.,6</i>)



## overseas man
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a long-distance drover.

>  <b>GORDON</b> There were two main categories of drover. 'Locals' who did the shorter droves within the district and overseas drovers who undertook the long-distance jobs. <i>(1990,Matawhero, Elsewhere and Other Things,31</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> I always liked to have an 'overseas' man on tap, because if a potential purchaser asked for a drover and then bought a pen of stock, he was virtually committed to fill that drive. [overseas drover] <i>(1990,Matawhero, Elsewhere and Other Things,34</i>)



## Overseasia
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>RHODES</b> Long before an Australian critic was talking about `the cultural cringe', or Allen Curnow was referring to the malady of `overseasia' in New Zealand, Sinclaire had declined [2] to regard himself as a spiritual exile from anywhere. <i>(1984,Frederick Sinclaire,1</i>)



## overseer
 
 <i>n.</i> a station-manager [(hist, station)]

>  A young unmarried man is in want of a situation as Farm Overseer or Bailiff. <i>(Jul 3, 1841,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,1</i>)

>  (The Overseer's Lament) With breeches thread-bare and worn, With jumper running to seed;/ <i>(Jan 1853,Canterbury Rhymes,17</i>)

>  <b>ROCHFORT</b> We stopped here [at Rangitikei] for the night, and then went on up the river to a station belonging to Mr. H.....n, managed by his overseer, Mr. McDonnel. <i>(1853,Adventures of a Surveyor,23</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> If they sustain this character, they will ... find no great difficulty ... getting an overseer's place, with from 100l. to 200l. a year, and their board and lodging. <i>(Jun 1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,vi/76</i>)

>  <b>HARPER</b> There is a shepherd ... away up in the hills ... he scarcely ever sees anyone but the overseer of the station, <i>Ibid.</i> 59 [I] began to wonder how long the `Overseer' in the jumper and moleskin trousers would be content to lose himself in the pasture of this wild mountain solitude. <i>(Jul 20, 1861,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,52,1914 ed.</i>)

>  Clearing the bush ... till 3 p.m. when the shearers came to the other side of the river. The overseer and a sailor went to bring them over in a boat. <i>(Nov 21, 1862,Chudleigh Diary,67,1950 ed.</i>)

>  Your overseer has gone to Tarndale [station] and shot Charlie the German, and shot himself. <i>(Feb 22, 1868,Marlborough Express,6</i>)

>  <b>TINNE</b> On Titiowharu [=Titiokura], where I slept the next night, I found the overseer ... `mustering' sheep, which is the next important process to `shearing' in the squatter's annual programme. <i>(1873,Wonderland of the Antipodes,37</i>)

>  <b>FERGUSON</b> But by-and-bye the overseer/ [168] Rode out to learn the shepherds'cheer./ <i>(1883,Castle Gay,167</i>)

>  <b>KENNAWAY</b> Here ... he was overtaken and captured by an overseer who had followed on his track for two days. <i>(1874,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,137</i>)

>  <b>BOOTH</b> The station [c1859], or homestead, stood on a plateau some fifty feet above the plain; it consisted of two huts, mud-walled and thatched with snow grass. One of these contained the general kitchen ..., the other was the residence of the squatter and his overseer. <i>(1912,Five Years in NZ,18</i>)

>  Overseer. - Under manager. Os. were kept on most of the large stations in former days, and where the owner lived on the station he often managed it himself but kept an o. The word is rarely used here now. but Os. all call themselves managers; but the real difference between them is that a m. can sign cheques and an o. can't. The word was brought here from Australia by theShagroons. (C.R., p. 10;Butler A First Year in Canterbury Settlement] p. 70) <i>(Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]



## overseership
 
 <i>n.</i> [non-rural] practice of the position of workplace overseer (See [overseership; farm or station](../O#overseership;-farm-or-station))

>  Hard Labour Men. - These persons,consisting of six in all, are, we understand, kept at work by a guard of two jailers and one constable. This is rather an extravagant overseership, and we suspect it would be cheaper to confine them altogether, than to exercise them at such a cost as that. <i>(Jul 6, 1844,Daily Southern Cross,2</i>) [NOTES: Not rural]

>  <b>BOOTH</b> ... the opportunity [ c1859, to wash on the run] was an excellent one, and would in all probability lead to his ... being offered the overseership... <i>(1912,Five Years in NZ,22</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> Farm or station; practice of the position of overseer (See [overseership [non-rural]](../O#overseership-[non-rural]))

>  On reaching the up-country 'station' of his relative, he had successively beeen herder, stockman, and shearer, taking the rough with the smooth - and precious little smooth there was too - till, some 18 months after his arrival in the colony, he had been promoted to the overseership. <i>(Mar 16, 1894,Clutha Leader,7</i>)

>  A PERSON lately arrived, of long experience, who with his wife is desirous to obtain the superintendency or working overseership of a Farm or Stock Station. <i>(Jun 30, 1869,Taranaki Herald,3</i>)

>  Applications, with testimonials (copies only), addressed chairman, Waimate County Council, Waimate, and endorsed "Application for Overseership", are invited up till 11 o'clock a.m. on 28th March, 1900, for the position of Overseer to the Waimate county. <i>(Mar 15, 1900,Waimate Daily Advertiser,3</i>)



## overskirting
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the excess removing of britch and peripheral wool from a fleece.

>  Overskirting is a common and costly fault. <i>(Nov 13, 1975,NZ Farmer,48</i>)



## oversowing
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the sowing of seed on land that already carries some growth, eg scrub, in order to protect seed.

>  <b>TAIT</b> .. oversowing of grass and clover seed on the hills 1969 NEWTON Big Country of the North Island 180 This will include the breaking-in of 2,000 acres of new country annually as well as an extensive aerial topdressing and over sowing programme on the hill country. <i>(1958,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol II,496</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> Burning, oversowing and topdressing, followed by severe grazing, has worked on a few blocks. <i>(1987,People, Places & Paddocks,179</i>)



## oversown country
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) hill country native tussock land surface sown with exotic grasses.

>  <b>M H-B P</b> blocks on the oversown country, ranging from 40 ha to 190 ha, three Maku blocks and five native blocks. On the oversown country, sheep and cattle are rotationally grazed. <i>(1982,Fine Wool,33</i>)



## overstay
 
 <i>n.</i> [WWI]

>  <b>LEE</b> `I'm broke. Can't afford necessities, let alone luxuries. Unless you want to stand treat for a pauper? Buckshee?' She hurried away. ...Another New Zealander overstayed of [sic ?on] leavecame to cadge a few bob as I walked down the street. <i>Ibid.</i> 12 And a swine of a military MP came in and demanded my leave pass. ... `Just where you might hang around. Cocoa and biscuits for pennies. Cheap for the overstays.' ... `Well don't let me catch you overstaying your leave.' <i>(1976,Soldier,6</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i>

>  Wong Sing, the Chinaman the police stated they were searching for, was fined an additional GBP25 for overstaying the period allowed by his permit. <i>(Mar 21, 1929,NZ Truth,7</i>)

>  In a more recent case Magistrate Hunt announced that in future any Chinese who have overstayed their permits and voluntarily surrender themselves to the Customs Department, will be met with a fine. <i>(Oct 13, 1927,NZ Truth,5</i>)

>  <b>KENNEDY</b> Besides there was the advantage that these [Pacific island] people were not admitted permanently, and if things got tough, we could ... round up, with dawn raids in the best totalitarian tradition, those who had overstayed and forcibly depart them. <i>(1981,Straight from the Shoulder,9</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> -

>  They got GBP1 for every straggler from the fleet, and GBP8 for men-o-wars men, returned as deserters, through overstaying their liberty; viz., GBP3 from the Captain, and GBP5 from the Colonial Government. <i>(Dec 28, 1888,Bay of Plenty Times,2</i>)

>  Some of them overstayed their leave a day or two since, when one of them in resisting the police was brought almost to a state of nudity, another jumped from the boat that was taking him off to the ship, and for nearly an hour gave the boat's crew a duck-hunt in sight of the wharf. <i>(Oct 20, 1875,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  On Saturday he was arrested for overstaying his leave, and as a result of enquiries he was suspected of the offence mentioned above. <i>(May 24, 1915,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  <b>LEE</b> `I'm broke. Can't afford necessities, let alone luxuries. Unless you want to stand treat for a pauper? Buckshee?' She hurried away. ...Another New Zealander overstayed of [sic ?on] leavecame to cadge a few bob as I walked down the street. <i>(1976,Soldier,6</i>)

>  Even when .. you have obtained permission to leave the country, you may only remain absent for a certain period, fixed by law, according to your station. If you overstay your leave, your property is confiscated. <i>(Jul 6, 1904,Otago Witness,80</i>)

>  Peter Ford, warder .. overstaying leave one hour, and being slightly under the influence of drink on his return, cautioned and reprimanded. <i>(Nov 21, 1866,Daily Southern Cross,5</i>)



## overstayer
 
 <i>n.</i> a visitor who has not left New Zealand by the date required by a visa etc. [1981 Feb Avondale College (Auckland) Slang Words in Use (Goldie Brown Collection) overstayer. Bruce Rampton (Rampton Crighton & Co, Box 17-364, Wellington) wrote on 21/9/1999 that the word was still in use and sent a copy of part of a statement of claim filed in the court "...The family was informed by the New Zealand Immigration Service on or about 30 July 1997 that they must leave New Zealand immediately on the grounds that they were illegal overstayers in New Zealand."]

>  During the dawn raids, Pacific Islanders were targeted as overstayers and periodically persecuted and deported from 1974 right up to quite recently with the worst of it happening around the mid to late seventies. <i>(Nov 1999,North & South,71</i>)

>  <b>BOOTH</b> Many [Pacific Islanders] who came in [in the 1970s] as tourists stayed on as illegal workers. A new word was born - overstayer. <i>(1997,Deadline : My Story,142</i>)

>  "Anyone who speaks with a non-New Zealand accent must arouse some query or suspicion..." Police Chief Superintendent Andrew Berriman on the random checking of overstayers, 1977. <i>(Dec 29, 1999,Dominion,26</i>)

>  During an emotional scene, Sarah Smuts-Kennedy, as the palagi partner of overstayer Joe.. had a rather erratic case of running mascara... <i>Ibid.</i> 71 Joe is turned in by one of his family, who doesn't want overstayers spoiling things for legal immigrants. <i>(Jan 8, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),70</i>)

>  He [Dr Ian Prior] addressed a meeting at Newtown in 1976 to protest against dawn raids, a mechanism sanctioned by the Muldoon government to extract overstayers from their homes and deport them. <i>(Feb 26, 2009,Dominion Post,B3</i>)

>  Overstayer was added to our vocabulary [in 1975], as was dawn raid as police sought illegal immigrants. <i>(Jan 3, 1980,Dominion,6</i>)

>  <b>MANTELL</b> `Police don't often get as far as this. ... Usually personnel business. Overstayers - misdemeanours and the like.' <i>(1980,Murder or Three, A,60</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> overstayer: You could be eligible for this obnoxious appellation, although it usually gets hung on Pacific Islanders who have come to work in N.Z. An overstayer is someone who has not left the country when his visa expires. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,74</i>)

>  A stereotype of Pacific Islanders as overstayers, established during the `dawn raids' of a decade ago, remains an influence in the Labour Department's immigration division, the Race Relations Conciliator, Mr Wally Hirsh, said today. <i>(Aug 12, 1986,Evening Post,6</i>)

>  overstayer NZ Polynesian or other immigrant who overstays. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,536</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 344]]

>  In a report on discrimination against Pacific Island visitors, Mr Hirsh said the former National Minister of Immigration, Mr Frank Gill, in 1977 linked overstayers with `communicable disease' and talked of an `overstayer epidemic.' Such an equation of overstaying with disease was both revealing and irrational, Mr Hirsh said. `We submit that the racism involved in stereotyping andtargeting Pacific Island groups during the overstayer campaigns of 1974-76 has remained an influence in the department,' he said. <i>(Aug 12, 1986,Evening Post,6</i>)

>  The only publicity about Samoa then was machete attacks and overstayers. <i>(Jan 16, 1999,Listener (New Zealand Listener),56</i>)

>  <b>GOLDSMITH</b> When Bolger accused Prebble of benefitting from the votes of overstayers the debate turned very ugly. <i>(1997,John Banks : A Biography,131</i>)

>  The Muldoon government told police to round up "overstayers" and soon, Strand says, "we were stopping people on the streets." <i>(Mar 25, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30</i>)

>  [Hdg] Parliament's overstayer takes off... Self-styled "overstayer" Rowena Sutton has pledged to devote her energy to community work. <i>(May 18, 2000,City Voice,1</i>)

>  Nearly 1800 asylum-seekers who have been refused refugee status remain in New Zealand as overstayers, using the health system at taxpayers' expense... Most overstayers would be paid under the table, working on farms and in restaurants for employers who chose not to ask questions. <i>(Mar 6, 2002,Dominion,1</i>)

>  Little Big Brother is King Kapisi, though he was once Bran Muffin, working with Wellington hip-hop bands such as Gifted and Brown and the Overstayers. <i>(Mar 24, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),33</i>)

>  Was 70s New Zealand really that bad? "God, it was grim. it was Muldoon. It was dawn raids and overstayers." <i>(Sep 3, 2005,Listener (New Zealand Listener),40</i>)

>  It stemmed from an unsigned piece [sc. inthe Christchurch Star] that claimed that"backbencher fury" over the overstayersissue had been "unleashed" in theGovernment caucus that morning. <i>(Nov 20, 1976,Listener (New Zealand Listener),13</i>)

>  Amnesty Aroha, the national group seekingan amnesty for immigrant overstayers,last night appealed to the PrimeMinister.. to make no decision on theissue until they have presented him withmaterial on the overstayer situation. <i>(Nov 1, 1976,Evening Post,16</i>)

>  <b>GRANT</b> "There's one!" said Sarge. "Not just anoverstayer, but a loony overstayer. Afterhim!" <i>(1979,I Rode with the Epigrams,23</i>)

>  The Tongan-born winger has been in NewZealand for several years... "He is notan overstayer," Mr Martin said from hisNorthland farm. <i>(May 19, 1993,Evening Post,1</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> as a clothing label

>  The Overstayer tag draws attention immediately. But Kapisi insists, "I'm using it as a word of empowerment, instead of a derogatory term. I grew up with people saying, 'There goes an ovesrstayer.' So I'm flipping it right back at them. We are all overstayers, we all immigrated here [to New Zealand] at some time." <i>(Nov 20, 2003,Vivid,9</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i>

>  At just 23, he has worked on four radio stations.. is now in his third season presenting TV3s <i>Pepsi Chart</i>... "I'm not sure how long I'll do this... I don't want to be an overstayer". <i>(Mar 15, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),34</i>)

## overstock
 
 <i>v.</i> to run more stock than land can supportalso OVERSTOCKING



## overweight
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [sp. use] a fat/prime lamb weighing more than 36 lbs.

>  The buyers have reduced the weights of first grade freezers from 42 to 36 lbs. All lambs over 36lbs are counted as overweights. <i>(May 1909,NZ Farmer,360</i>)



## overwind
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McARA</b> Overwind: Failure by the engine-driver to stop his machine before the safety detaching-hook on the winding-rope came into contact with the detaching ring mounted in the headframe, thus suspending the cage in the skyshaft and allowing the rope to go over the head-sheave pulley. Overwinds had to be reported to the Inspector of Mines. To prevent overwind there were generally two warning bells which rang in the engine-room as the cage was approaching the surface. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328</i>)



## overwinterer
 
 <i>n.</i> someone who spends the winter in the Antarctic

>  <b>KNIGHT</b> The letter went on to say that the overwinterers had met with the relevant people at Scott Base, and therefore no further visit was necessary. <i>(1988,Icebound,71</i>)



## overwintering
 
 <i>adj.</i> spending the winter in Antarctica

>  <b>YOUNG</b> Though [the southern rookeries] contain penguins of the same over-wintering group who ocould be expected to meet the same difficulties, the contact of these birds with humans is practically nil. <i>(1971,Penguin Summer,98</i>)



## ovine king
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a leading breed of sheep.

>  <b>CRAWFORD</b> Merino, the ovine king of New Zealand, would have to give way ... <i>(1949,Sheep and Sheepmen of Canterbury,81</i>)



## owl
 
 <i>n.</i> owl, morepork. =ruru [so 1957/151]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Owl, n. an English bird name In New Zealand, the species are - Laughing Jackass, or L. Owl, Sceloglaux albifacies, Kaup (Maori name, Whekau, q.v.), and the Morepork, formerly Athene novae-zelandiae, Gray, now Spiloglaux novae-zelandiae, Kaup. (See Morepork). <i>(1898,Austral English,333</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> morepork or whekauOwl, n. an English bird name. In New Zealand, the species are - Laughing Jackass, or L. Owl, Sceloglaux albifacies, Kaup (Maori name, Whekau, q.v.), and the Morepork, formerly Athene novae-zelandiae, Gray, now Spiloglaux novae-zelandiae, Kaup. (See Morepork).

>  Two owls flew into St. Mary's Church, Levin, during evensong on a recent Sunday. <i>(Apr 20, 1912,NZ Truth,9</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> Pigeons, nestors, parrots, rail, kiwi, swamp-fowl, water-fowl, owls, parson-birds, all these and more were eaten; while the native dogs and rats were held to be great dainties. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/151</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Koukou, ... owl, athene Novae Zealandiae. <i>Ibid.</i> 176 Koukou. 1. v.i., onomatopoeic. Hoot. ... <i>(1892,Dictionary of the NZ Language,76</i>)

>  Two or three human heads are hanging on the old pa'aka, and an owl has perched himself in a Kowair tree hard by, increasing the effect of the singular scene by his solitary moan. <i>(Mar 27, 1847,New Zealander, The,4</i>)

>  The Frasertown correspondent of the Wairoa Guardian states that a native owl (ruru), commonly called "morepork" some time ago seized two canaries hanging in a cage under a verandah. <i>(Sep 7, 1904,Otago Witness,31</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> ... no voice came to mar the utter stillness, until ... the Ruru shuddered past, croaking [94] his eerie cry: `Moepok! Moepok!' which sounded like a uneasy wraith ..., rather than a comfortable Maori owl. <i>(1894,In the Land of the Tui,93</i>) [NOTES: 1890 Mar 3 in 1894 Wilson Land of the Tui 93-94]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> owl + modifier

>  Lately there has been a good deal said about the little grey owl and the amount of good it does or does not do in clearing the small birds out. <i>(Jan 8, 1910,Ashburton Guardian,2</i>)

>  One, apparently, is the "laughing owl," a large, white-faced member of the owl family, found only in the South Island of New Zealand, and the other is the "laughing jackass," which evidently is one of the petrels. <i>(Feb 5, 1910,Evening Post,13</i>)

>  The laughing owl (Athene albifacies) or laughing jackass, as it is often called, is found in the South Island and Kaimanawa Range, North Island. <i>(Apr 17, 1890,Otago Witness,18</i>)

>  The little owl has been very largely imported into New Zealand to keep down the introduced small birds there... <i>(Sep 8, 1909,Otago Witness,74</i>)

>  Whichever of the two dominating rats played the part of exterminator to the <i>kiore</i>, not only robbed the Maori tribes belonging to the wooded parts of the country of their principal animal food, but have, in the opinion of Mr. Buller, to answer for the almost extinction of the <i>Sceloglans albifacies</i>, or laughing owl... <i>(Jul 21, 1883,Otago Witness,28</i>)

>  From a recent article in the Field it appears that the little owl is now thoroughly acclimatised in the Midland counties of England, as well as in Kent and Hampshire. As the little owl (<i>Athene noctua</i>) will soon be fairly numerous in Otago, it would be interesting to obtain accurate informationn as to the manner in which it is adapting itself to the changed circumstances. <i>(Oct 21, 1908,Otago Witness,21</i>)

>  <i>Morepork Ninox novaeseelandiae novaeseelandiae</i> (GMELIN, 1788) ... OTHER NAMES:<i> Ruru, New Zealand owl, boobook owl</i>. <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,257</i>)

>  An interesting feature is a number of New Zealand birds - the kukupa, or New Zealand pigeon, the tui, kaka, and others, with two rare eggs of the New Zealand laughing owl, now nearly extinct. <i>(Jan 11, 1897,Evening Post,5</i>)

>  The question of importing the German "little owl" as a means of meeting the small bird nuisance was before the Canterbury Agricultural and Pastoral Association. <i>(Sep 10, 1908,Evening Post,3</i>)



## owl parrot
 
 <i>n.</i> an old name for the kakapo. [aka kakapo]

>  This remarkable bird, which is also known as the Nocturnal Ground Parrot, or Owl Parrot, is peculiar to New Zealand; and, although at one time it appears, from the accounts of the natives, to have been a common denizen of all the forests - it is now only met with in remote and unfrequented districts. <i>(Mar 19, 1864,Otago Witness,15</i>)

>  Stringops habroptilus (Owl Parrot, or Kakapo). <i>(1889,TrNZI,21/211 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> This [Kakapo] is one of the remarkable forms peculiar to New Zealand, and has been appropriately termed an Owl Parrot. ... As its name <i>Stringops</i> indicates, its face bears a superficial likeness to that of an owl. ... this bird [is] what the native name, Kakapo, implies, a night Parrot; ... <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,28,1888 2nd edn 1/179 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Owl-parrot, n.</i> a bird of New Zealand. See Kakapo. <i>(1898,Austral English,333</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Tarepo, ... Stringops habroptilus owl parrot or ground parrot. = kakapo. </i> <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,391</i>)

>  <b>DURRELL</b> The Kakapo's name is Owl Parrot, and this is singularly apt for even a professional ornithologist could be pardoned for mistaking it for an owl at first glance. <i>(1966,Two in the Bush,108</i>)

>  <i>owl-parrot</i> NZ former name of kakapo. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,536</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  The sweet-smelling kakapo, or owl parrot, is the very essence of a New Zealand bird, a naive innocent time traveller that struggles to belong in the modern world... <i>(Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/100</i>)



## owl's-foot moss
 
 <i>n.</i> Lycopodium volubile, aka waewaekoukou

>  <b>SATCHELL</b> The boys on the [Northland] station were getting the big shed ready for a dance ... another young man ... was engaged in looping up garlands of `waiwaikoko' or owl's-foot moss, together with branches of Christmas tree, aflame with blood-red flowers. <i>(1905,Toll of the Bush,236</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> The first named [Lycopodium volubile] - the OWL'S-FOOT or CLIMBING CLUB-MOSS - is found abundantly throughout New Zealand. <i>(1961,Flora NZ,90,1929 ed.</i>)



## owlet moth
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>GILLESPIE</b> The ones attracting most attention were the owlet moths, the caterpillars of which destroyed the first pastures and cereal and root crops. There were also porina moths (<i>Oxycanus spp.</i>) whose caterpillars also destroyed pastures; the bag moth (<i>Oeceticus omnivorus</i>) which left cigar-shaped cocoons hanging from shrubs and tree [sic]; and the ghost-like white moth ... of the flax grub (<i>Orthoclydon praefectata</i>) which eats longitudinal slits on the surface of [flax] leaves ... <i>(1958,South Canterbury,10,1971 ed.</i>)

 <i>n.</i> [Jim Cameron writes: The DNZE definition is not precisely accurate, in that the occupier may not be able to hold the freehold; the basic title may be leasehold, or the apartment may be occupied under a company lease system. ## ox tongue
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>ELL</b> Another plant with a flower like a dandelion is the prickly ox tongue, so named for its leaves. These are prickly and warty. <i>(1983,Wildflowers and Weeds,11</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> A little lower down where the seaweeds are permanently submerged we have such plants as the GRAPELET, the WIRE- WEED, and OX-TONGUE; the FLAP-JACK and GIRDLE WEEDS. In rather deeper water ... we have the (<i>Lessonia</i>) and the OARWEED, the GRAPEWEED, and numerous representatives of the red algae. <i>Ibid.</i> 20 <i>Ox-tongue (Glossophra kunthii</i>) The fronds of this dichotomously branched seaweed are coverd with rough outgrowths bearing the organs of reproduction, which resemble the surface of cow's tongue. <i>(1961,Flora NZ,5</i>)

>  <i>oxtongue [s] Picris echioides L. </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,55</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOPR</b> OXTONGUE (<i>Picris echioides</i>) ... All true leaves have raised spots, each carrying a short hooked bristle, which give Oxtongue a rough feel ... <i>(1981,Weeds of Roadsides,109</i>)

>  The plant is a weed known in England as ox-tongue, and usually called by that name in New Zealand, although yellow or blister thistle are also sometimes applied. <i>(Apr 10, 1901,Hawera & Normanby Star,2</i>)

>  Fibre-weed and tar-weed are also spreading rapidly, as is ox-tongue. <i>(Dec 23, 1903,Otago Witness,16</i>)



## ox-eye
 
 <i>n.</i> [n. pl.]

>  OX EYES. Cut rounds of bread, toast to a pale brown, dip in hot milk, butter generously, and place on buttered baking-tin. Separate the yolks and whites of as many eggs as there are rounds of toast. Add a pinch of salt to the whites, whip to a froth, and pile high on each toast-round. Make a hollow in the centre, drop in a yolk, sprinkle with pepper and salt and a morsel of butter, and put in a brisk oven to cook untill [sic] yolks are set. <i>(Sep 16, 1908,Otago Witness,74</i>)

>  <b>McCREDIE</b> OX-EYES ... thick slices of bread into rounds, smaller rounds ... soak the rings .. in milk ... Break an egg in each hole ... sprinkle with chopped parsley and bake ... <i>(1915,Bungalow Recipe Book, The,37</i>)

>  OX-EYES ... slices of stale bread cut into rounds ... pour over ... milk ... press down the centre ... break an egg ... in ... Bake ... <i>(1939,Bread - The Basic Food. Facts and Recipes,8</i>)

 <i>n.</i> =WOOD SORREL, =YELLOW SOUR WEED, =YELLOW WOOD SORREL (See [oca](../O#oca),[yam](../Y#yam))

>  OXALIS (<i>Oxalis corniculata</i>), sometimes called wood sorrel, or yellow wood sorrel, or yellow sour weed, is a small perennial common in lowland pastures over both islands. It is a native of New Zealand, but is found over most of the rest of the world as well. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,116,1967 p. 116 ed.</i>)

## Oxford scholar
 
 <i>n.</i> five-shilling (piece or sum); dollar [rhyming slang]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Oxford scholar. Five shillings (piece or sum): New Zealanders' rhyming s. on dollar: C. 20. Also from ca. 1870, in the S.W. of England; now ob. Cf. shirt collar (Everyman, March 26, 1931.) <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,596</i>)

>  `Oxford Scholar' is occasionally heard for five shillings; it probably comes from English rhyming slang for `dollar'. <i>(1943,English as it is spoken in NZ,BENNETT,91 ed.</i>)

>  NZ rhyming slang: Rhodes Scholar on dollar. Also from ca 1870 in SW of England. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang,20c</i>)

>  <b>HARGREAVES</b> Crown [:] dollar, bull, `oxford scholar' <i>(1972,From Beads to Banknotes,151</i>)

>  The crown was called a bull, a cartwheel, a caser, five bob, and most familiar of all a dollar, the latter dating from the time when the rate of exchange was four US dollars to the pound. The only instance recorded of Cockney rhyming slang term for a coin is Oxford scholar, the derivation from dollar being obvious. The half crown was called a half-dollar, a half-bull, half-caser, two-and-a-kick, and two-and-a-buck. In the latter instance buck stands for sixpence, though apparently this term was rarely used by itself for that coin denomination, but almost always in association with a number of shillings. The florin seems to have had fewer English nicknames, only two bob being recorded as being in use in New Zealand. <i>(Oct 1977,NZ Numismatic Journal,15</i>)



## oxidised country
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McARA</b> Oxidised country: This referred to country rock in which the iron minerals had been oxidised byexposure to the air above the permanent water-level giving it a brown instead of a bluish colour. The brown could be due to the oxidation of pyrite to limonite and haematite in the rock. Near the water-level there was sometimes a zone of semi-sulphides where the ore might be enriched by leaching downward of some of the values. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328</i>)

## oyster
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> . [see also Morris entry 1898 See AUCKLAND ROCK, BLUFF, DREDGE, FOVEAUX STRAIT, PACIFIC, STEWART, MUD]

>  ACCLIMATISING OYSTERS. <i>(May 4, 1917,Grey River Argus,3</i>)

>  <b>SHEPHERD</b> He shot several ducks Red bills a wood hen and killed a hare seal. ... cockles Mussels & oisters are plentiful and excellent. <i>(1826,Journal</i>) [NOTES: 1826 Shepherd Journal in 1940 Howard Rakiura 357]

>  <b>McDONNELL</b> The rivers are well stocked with fine fish ... - the very creeks swarm with them. There are abundance of lobsters, crawfish, oysters, prawns, and shrimps; besides clams, peppies, muscles, limpets, and cockles. <i>(1834,Extracts From a Journal,15,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>YATE</b> Those saltwater fish] most plentiful, and of greatest note, are, soles, mackarel, cod-fish, a species of salmon, whiting, snapper, mullet, bream, skate, gurnards, and a few smaller kinds, some not near so large as a sprat; with an abundance of crayfish, oysters, shrimps, prawns, muscles, and cockles. <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),71</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> fn Among the piscivorous tribes, are those well known in Europe under the appellations of snapper, tench, haddocks, pollock, salmon, gurnets, john dory, hakes, bream, sword-fish, elephant-fish, diodons, pipe-fish, cod, parrot-fish, flounders, skate, cat and dog-fish, sting-rays,eels. - Among the larger tribes that sport in shoals in the vicinity of the shore, are, sperm-whales, called <i>para</i>[as-acute] <i>paraua</i>[a3-acute] (physeter macrocephalus); black whale, <i>mungu nui (black physeter); right whale, <i>tohora</i> (balaena mysticetus); finback, (balaena physalis); sun-fish (diodon mola); large-lipped whale (balaena mysticetus); large-lipped whale (balaena musculus); hump-back (balaena gibbosa); shark, <i>mango</i> (squalus); hammer-head (zygaena); pilots, (scomber); flying-fish (exocaetus volitans); and an infinity of others, besides a great variety of testaceous and crustaceous fishes that almost cover the rocks under water, such as clams, muscles, oysters, crays, cuttles, limpets, wilks, gigantic muscles, a foot in length, cockles, sea-ears, sea-eggs, star-fish, mutton-fish, &c.; from the latter the pearl is procured which they insert in the eyes of their carved images and boxes. <i>(1840,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/202</i>)

>  <b>POWELL</b> AUCKLAND ROCK OYSTER (<i>Saxostrea glomerata</i>). Found only in the upper tidal rocky zone of the northern portion of the North Island and at the Chatham Islands. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,19</i>)

>  It must be confessed, that our Auckland "Opposition" is fearfully heavy-handed .. It opens oyster with daggers, and goes to market to buy an axe to break an egg. <i>(Feb 26, 1848,New Zealander, The,2</i>)

>  A number of oystermen were fined for breaches of the Fisheries Act, by taking oysters in the close season. <i>(Apr 3, 1886,West Coast Times,4</i>)

>  Bottles of Oysters. Those wishing a tasty oyster supper at home this evening can buy a bottle of the freshest at Yarrow's Taupo Quay. <i>(Mar 31, 1906,Wanganui Herald,7</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  This is below high watermark, and leased under the Oyster Fisheries Act, 1866. <i>(Oct 7, 1874,Star, The,2</i>)

>  The Auckland oyster is of the rock or deep shell variety, and, in the opinion of some connoisseurs, is better than the "Sydney rock". <i>(Apr 30, 1910,NZ Truth,4</i>)

>  Nobody knows exactly how the debate began; ... but probably every member of the N.Z.E.F. on Gallipoli took part in the discussion of the oyster question : Auckland v. Stewart Island. ... whether the delicacy and daintiness of the little rock were better or worse than the noble fatness of the Stewart. <i>(May 25, 1918,Quick March,1</i>)

>  The dredge oyster, Bluff oyster or Foveaux Strait oyster is by far the most popular in New Zealand. <i>(May 1985,Metro,47/28</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i> Dredge oyster Tiostrea lutaria</i> ... Alternatively Bluff, Foveaux Strait, or Stewart Island oyster. Native to New Zealand, with a similar species in Chile. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,166</i>)

>  <b>CARR</b> Rock oysters are found around Auckland and northwards principally ... In the straits between the Bluff and Stewart Island large beds exist of the flat, or mud oyster. <i>(1913,Country Work,25</i>)

>  After an absence from the menu for four months, the popular Foveaux Strait oysters appeared in Bluff and Invercargill for evening meals and supper. <i>(Feb 9, 1938,Auckland Weekly News,9</i>)

>  The Act of 1866 provided that "The word 'oyster', whenever used in this Act shall mean the under-mentioned species or varieties of the oysters that is to say:- 1. Shore oysters, excepting rock oysters between high and low water-mark .. " <i>(Nov 13, 1874,Daily Southern Cross,2</i>)

>  The Picton Oyster Ostrea discordia [sic] is the next, coming South, of importance. Those gathered off the banks are very fine and large. Allthough they have not been found in any great quantities still I would not be surprised to hear of the discovery of large oyster beds off Queen Charlotte's Sound or in the vicinity of Cook's Strait. <i>(Oct 13, 1892,Otago Witness,45</i>)

## oyster borer
 
 <i>n.</i> [CC Ransom notes: Is this the DRILL or OYSTER DRILL (Urosalpinx sp) that bores holes in oysters: a marine snail? There are a number of snails that attack molluscs but U. cinerea is said to be the oyster-specialist.]

>  <b>POWELL</b> OYSTER BORER (<i>Lepsiella scobina</i>). This is less than an inch in height, but it plays havoc on local rock oyster beds. The animal uses its teeth (the radula) to drill holes through the oyster's shell and then extracts the oyster piecemeal. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,28</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> Even then they [oysters] are subject to an ingenious predator, the OysterBorer which, as his name implies, bores a hole into the helpless oyster's shell - and devours it. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,172</i>)

## oystercatcher
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Haemotopus</i> spp. =SEA PIE, also called redbill, torea black or black and white wader of seashores and estuaries, having a long orange bill with which it picks out small molluscs and worms. spg also <i>oyster(-)catcher </i>

>  We have indeed only a vague idea of the number of volumes of Alison's history - from seeing them ranged on shelves, as countless almost as shags or oystercatchers crowded in a long row on a mud-bank when the tide is advancing upon them. <i>(Jul 11, 1857,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2</i>)

>  [see seapie, black bird, woodhen for quotations] <i>(1772,Cook's Journals,2/135</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> I shot two Curlews or rather <i>Oister catchers</i>, which are quite black & have very bright vermillion bills of the same structure as the English Sea Pie ... [ed. fn]Variable Oystercatcher. <i>Ibid.</i> 1773 May 13 2/276 ... abounding with New Zeeland black Shags, Oystercatchers (commonly called <i>Curlews</i>) ... <i>(Mar 29, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/243</i>)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Towards the southward there are also water hens of a large species, rails in vast numbers, cormorants, oyster catchers, or sea-pies, penguins, and some other sorts of aquatic birds. <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/256</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> The small <i>noddies</i> (sterna solida) abound in large flocks, as also the <i>oyster-catchers</i> and <i>water-hens</i>, whose excessive tameness has nearly annihilated them. <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),1/307</i>)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> After leaving the village we crossed to another bay, E-Taua. This bay also is spacious and pleasant. Kingfishers, oystercatchers, tuis [fn Anthochoera concinnata, Vig. and Horsfield.], andcormorants [fn Pelecanus pica, Forst.], enlivened the trees on its shores, and many birds were singing in the forest. <i>(1843,Travels in NZ,1/59</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Several kinds of birds are indigenous to the woods and waters; among them the oyster-catcher, bittern, kingfisher, cormorant, quail, wild-duck, mocking or [16] parson bird, parrots, parroquets, woodhen, pigeon, etc. (the [sic] gigantic Moa bird is believed to be now quite extinct). <i>(1867,Twelve Years in Canterbury,15</i>)

>  HAEMATOPUS LONGIROSTRIS, Vieil. Torea. Oyster-catcher, Red-bill. <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/69</i>) [NOTES: Potts Birds]

>  The following is a list of the birds shot ... <i>Platycercus auriceps</i>, yellow-top paroquet; <i>Nestor meridionalis</i>, brown parrot; <i>Carpophaga Novae Zealandiae</i>, wood pigeon; <i>Haematopus unicolor</i>, black oyster catcher, or red bill; <i>Haematopus longirostris</i>, pied oyster catcher; <i>Ocydromus australis</i>, Weka, or wood hen; <i>Casarca variegata</i>, Paradise duck; <i>(1872,Proceedings Otago Provincial Council,39</i>) [NOTES: Appendix. Black Stewart Island]

>  <b>DOMETT</b> `Slim oyster-catcher, avocet,/ And tripping beach-birds, seldom met/ Elsewhere.' <i>(1872,Ranolf and Amohia,274</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Torea, or oyster-catcher (<i>Haematopus longirostris</i>) is one of the sea-coast birds, and is often to be seen about our tidal rivers. It is a black bird. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/225</i>)

>  <b>DOMETT</b> <i>Bluff oyster-catcher, avocet</i>,/ And tripping beach-birds, seldom met/ Elsewhere - ... <i>Ibid.</i> 2/338 Oyster-catcher ..... G. Haematopus. <i>(1883,Ranolf and Amohia,2/63</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Oyster-catcher, n. common English bird-name. The Australasian species are - Pied, <i>Haematopus longirostris</i>, Vieill.; Black,<i> H. unicolor</i>, Wagler; and two other species <i>H. picatus<./i>, Vigors, and <i>H. australasianus</i>, Gould, with no vernacular name. [1846] <i>(1898,Austral English,333</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Torea, n. 1. Haematopus longirostris, oyster-catcher</i>, and<i> H. unicolor</i>, red-bill; shore birds. <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,513</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> ... a segregation of Oyster Catchers (<i>Haemotopus ostralegus</i>) five or six hundreds strong <i>(1936,NZ Naturalist,51</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> <i>Haematopus ... longirostris</i> ... The bill is shorter and stouter than the New Zealand type, ... Oyster Catchers are found in New Zealand, Australia and the Moluccas west of New Guinea. Oyster Catcher. Torea. <i>(1952,Chatham Islands,79</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> Our boat was carried swiftly along a channel between silvery mud-flats, where red-footed oyster-catchers and long-legged stilts were running about. <i>(1911,They Led the All Blacks,36</i>)

>  Bird enthusiasts are in for a treat with a good chance of seeing .. shags, white-fronted terns, reef herons and oystercatchers ... <i>(Nov 2006,Footnotes,26 / 8</i>)

>  Birds - Indigenous. - Kiwi .. toria or oyster-catcher ... <i>(Nov 16, 1861,Otago Witness,6</i>)

>  The red-billed gull has earned a quaint reputation for itself on account of its systematic plunder of the oyster-catcher. <i>(Aug 8, 1908,Otago Witness,17</i>)

>  on the estuary the oyster-catcher considers / journeys <i>(May 14, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),114</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Haemotopus reischeki </i> (See [also torea](../A#also-torea))

>  <b>COLENSO</b> A black and graceful species of <i>Haemotopus</i> with orange coloured bill and legs is common [near Tokomaru Bay - East Cape] ... The natives call them Torea. [FN] Presumably the black form of the North Island Oyster catcher (<i>Haemotopus reischeki</i>). <i>(1840,Excursion in the Northern Island of NZ</i>) [NOTES: 1844 Colenso Excursion North Island in1959 Taylor Early Travellers 14]



## oystering
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  We feel sure you are aware that the establishment of a canning factory would greatly expand and benefit generally the oystering industry, but it is useless to think of forming such a company while the act remains in its present condition: and, apart therefrom, there is a possibility of doing a large trade with South Africa in frozen oysters. <i>(Aug 12, 1908,Otago Witness,57</i>)

>  Whereas divers persons have been in the habit of visiting the island of Motutapu under pretence of fishing, oystering, or procuring ballast, but for the real purpose of stealing and carrying away of Sheep, Turkeys, Geese, Pheasants, Rabbits, &c., &c. <i>(Jul 4, 1866,Daily Southern Cross,1</i>)

>  She is intended at present for the oystering trade, but will, during the close season, carry cargoes, and her carrying capacity being very large for a vessel of her size and rig, very few hands will work her. <i>(Apr 25, 1883,Southland Times,2</i>)

>  Captain Anglem's retirement on Monday after a long and honorable connection with oystering, fishing, and whaling, was the signal for a representative gathering of oystermen in the Club Hotel (Bluff) that afternoon when advantage was taken of the occasion to express their keen regret at his step (says a local paper). <i>(Jun 10, 1910,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  EXPORTS. Per Wairarapa .. 15 sacks oysters, Bluff and F.S. Oystering Co. <i>(Mar 22, 1892,Southland Times,2</i>)

>  <b>HOWARD</b> Each had its cutter ... lying ready for oystering which was just commencing. <i>(1940,Rakiura,308</i>)

>  Oystering in the Straits [Foveaux] [heading].he;CULCH <i>(Aug 10, 1921,Quick March,31</i>)

>  Cricket, football, quoits, bat-trap and ball, shooting, boating, swinging, bathing, oystering, and feasting, filled up the pleasant day. <i>(Dec 29, 1863,Daily Southern Cross,3</i>)

>  One of my hands joined the 'Peter Laing', the little vessel that induced me to go oystering. <i>(Jun 20, 1862,Daily Southern Cross,4</i>)



## oysterman
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The oystermen were the first to feel the pinch. <i>(Sep 24, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),18</i>)

>  <b>WHIM-WHAN</b> There was a dispute between merchants and oystermen at Bluff, and no oyster-boats had put to sea. <i>(1942,Verses 1941-1942,19</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> crewman on oyster dredge boat (See [oyster opener](../O#oyster-opener))

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> By the time the 1951 award was settled, oystermen received 1s 7d a sack, with a bonus of twopence halfpenny for skippers and a penny halfpenny for engineers.<i>Ibid.</i>210 The Marine Department biologist investigating Foveaux Strait oysters said the potential continuing yield was 170,000-180,000 sacks. Merchants, skippers and the oystermen united to claim that a catch of that size would ruin the beds. <i>(2004,Hooked,207</i>)



## oysterpicker
 
 <i>n.</i> South Island (?) name for oystercatcher

>  Oysterpickers - two kinds - called Toria by natives. <i>(Jan 6, 1855,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,3</i>)

>  "I saw my first oysterpicker of the season this morning, while I was feeding the horses," Ross said. Oysterpickers (Ross prefers the word to "oystercatchers") usually arrive in the Maniototo to breed in the first week of August, he said. <i>(Sep 2004,NZ Geographic,No. 69/111</i>)



## oysters
 
 <i>n.</i> in fig. and transf. use - (usually pl.) (freezing works) sweetbreads [(freezing workers')]

>  <b>GORDON</b> Sweetbreads are called <i>oysters</i> and are reputed good for sexual potency. If a person is seen pinching them there are comments of `He' going out tonight'. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: ca1951 May 17 Feilding Freezing Works terms p.c. Colin Gordon]



 
 <i>n.</i> (usually pl.) (children's) opaque brown and grey marbles [also smokies ca early 1950's Naenae Wellington] (See [also smokies](../A#also-smokies))



## oysters on bugnets
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <i>OYSTERS ON BUGNETS</i> bacon [sliced] very thinly ... stamp out small rounds ... Season a bearded oyster with pepper and lemon ... place it between two rounds of bacon ... dip ... into ... batter and fry ... place each on a small round fried crouton ... serve ... <i>(1915,Our Boys Cookery Book,19</i>)

 <i>adj.</i> Australian

>  The Oz accent was sometimes a bit of an obstacle to absorbing these complexities. <i>(Mar 4, 1995,Listener (New Zealand Listener),64</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> Australia

>  But will the lovely Lizzie consider life in Oz with a self-confessed dickhead? <i>(Sep 20, 1998,Sunday Star Times,F6</i>)

>  But Australia is Oz and New Zealand is Newzild and I don't think we should confuse the two - particularly the accents. <i>(Aug 2, 1998,Sunday Star Times,A9</i>)

>  The occasional ill-spelt postcard from Oz hardly makes up the deficit. <i>(Oct 14, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),61</i>)

>  In <i>Shop Till You Drop</i>, Lizzie and Noelene have followed the shiny signs and taken the yellow brick road to the land of Oz. <i>(Dec 2, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),53</i>)

>  Graeme Wilson held the pa while the others were in Oz. <i>(Jun 1989,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,84/26</i>)

## Ozzification
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Just how many Strine lifestyle shows can the market bear?... A purveyor of Burke's Backyard and other outback gems in New Zealand prime-time since the channel's Ozzification in March, Prime has put in a draft bid for the Kiwi channels owned by Canadian powermonger CanWest. <i>(Jun 22, 2002,Evening Post,18</i>)
